# Translation Guide (SRV)

**Familiarization, Internalization, Articulation (FIA) Translation Guide** © 2023 SRV Partners Released under CC BY\-SA 4\.0 license. *Familiarization, Internalization, Articulation (FIA) Translation Guide* has been adapted in the following languages Tok Pisin, عربي, Français, हिंदी, Bahasa Indonesia, Português, Русский, Español, Kiswahili, 简体中文 from *Familiarization, Internalization, Articulation (FIA)* © 2023 SRV Partners Released under CC BY\-SA 4\.0 license by Mission Mutual



--------------------------------


## Ezra 1:1–11 (id: eng-ezr-p1-v1)

Hear and Heart
--------------

In this step, hear Ezra 1:1–11 and put it in your hearts.

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 1:1–11 three times, in three different translations, if possible.

Pause this audio here.

Now, as a group, discuss the following questions:

1. What do you like in this passage? Pause this audio here.
2. What do you not like or not understand in this passage? Pause this audio here.
3. What does this passage tell us about God? Pause this audio here.
4. What does this passage tell us about people? Pause this audio here.
5. How does this passage affect our daily lives? Pause this audio here.
6. Who do you know who needs to hear this passage?

Setting the Stage
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 1:1–11 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

Many years before this story, the Babylonians destroyed the city of Jerusalem and the temple of Yahweh, and took the people of Judah to Babylon. Now, Cyrus is the new ruler over Babylon. King Cyrus says the exiles from Judah can return from Babylon to Jerusalem and rebuild the temple in Jerusalem. Many of the people of Judah prepare to go back to Jerusalem. This is the first story in the books of Ezra and Nehemiah.

The first part of this story is very similar to the end of the book of 2 Chronicles.

Stop here and listen to your translation of 2 Chronicles 36:22–23, if you have already translated the book of 2 Chronicles. Pause this audio here.

Before this story begins, Cyrus, who was the king of Media, defeats not only the Babylonians but also other kings and kingdoms. Together, all of these lands become part of the very large kingdom of Persia, with Cyrus as the king over all of these kingdoms.

Stop here and look at a map of the kingdom of Persia as a group. Pause this audio here.

In the first year of the rule of King Cyrus, Yahweh causes Cyrus to let the exiled Israelites go back to Jerusalem. Remember that the prophet Jeremiah said Yahweh would return the exiles to Jerusalem after 70 years. Yahweh has not yet completely finished doing what he said he would in Jeremiah's prophecy. Surprisingly, Yahweh uses a foreign king to bring the people back to Judah. In the same way that Yahweh used the Babylonians to discipline the Israelites, Yahweh now uses a foreign ruler to bring the Israelites back to the land of Judah.

Although Yahweh is the one in control, it is possible that Cyrus also lets the people of Judah leave in order to gain their support and possibly to have a way to get to the land of Egypt easily, because Egypt is close to the land of Judah. It was common during the time of King Cyrus for a new ruler to give something to the people he ruled over so the people would become his loyal supporters. The Persians sent other people back to their lands, too.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: What do new leaders do in your culture to make the people loyal to the new leaders? Pause this audio here.

King Cyrus makes a proclamation that the Israelites can go home to the land of Judah. During the time of King Cyrus, the king told an important message to heralds, who traveled throughout the kingdom of Persia to tell the people the message. The heralds probably said the message in different languages so that all of the people could understand the message. In this story, the heralds probably speak the message in Hebrew, the language of the people of Judah. The Persians also write down the message so that no one forgets what the message is.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: Talk about a time when your leaders or rulers had an important message to give to your community. Who spoke the message to you? Pause this audio here.

In Cyrus' message, Cyrus says that Yahweh is the God of heaven, and that Yahweh gave Cyrus all of the kingdoms of the earth. Some people think this means that Cyrus worships Yahweh, but we know that Cyrus says similar things about other people groups' gods. Maybe Cyrus calls Yahweh the "God of heaven" because the Persians also have a god of heaven. We also know that in the book of Isaiah, the prophet Isaiah says that Yahweh chooses Cyrus to bring the people back to the land of Judah, but that Cyrus does not personally know Yahweh. It is likely that Cyrus heard about Yahweh from Daniel and other Israelite exiles who lived in the land of Babylon.

Cyrus also says that Yahweh told Cyrus to build a temple for Yahweh at Jerusalem in the land of Judah. Remember, the temple of Yahweh is where God's people worship God. Cyrus gives permission to any of the people of Judah who want to go build this temple for Yahweh in Jerusalem. Cyrus also says, "may their God be with them," which most people think is a blessing on the people who will return to the land of Judah. Other people think "may their God be with them" means that the Israelites will physically bring their God with them back to the land of Judah. At this time, many people believed that a god only had control over the land of the god's people. If the people brought their gods, in the form of idols, to the land of Babylon, then the people also needed to take their idols with them when they left Babylon.

Stop here and, as a group, look at a map that includes Jerusalem, the land of Judah, Babylon, and the kingdom of Persia. Pause this audio here.

Finally, Cyrus says that the neighbors of the exiles in Babylon should help the exiles who go back to the land of Judah. These neighbors should give the exiles silver, gold, goods, livestock, and freewill offerings to help the exiles rebuild the temple in Jerusalem. Goods are things for work, like equipment or utensils, and freewill offerings are gifts that the people choose to give freely in the temple of Yahweh. Some people think these neighbors are other Israelites who survived the exile but decided not to return to the land of Judah. Other people think these neighbors also include non\-Israelites who live in Babylon. However, it would be strange if non\-Israelites gave gifts for Yahweh, who is not their god.

The Israelites hear the proclamation from King Cyrus, and Yahweh moves the spirits of many Israelites to prepare to go back to the land of Judah in order to rebuild the temple of Yahweh in Jerusalem. These Israelites include many leaders of the tribes of Judah and Benjamin, priests, Levites, and probably also some other Israelites. You will remember that it was the tribes of Judah and Benjamin who made up the kingdom of Judah, so it was people from the tribes of Judah and Benjamin who were in exile in Babylon. Priests normally served in the temple, and the Levites took care of other tasks in the temple. Remember, there was no temple for the priests and Levites to serve in while the people were in exile in Babylon.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: Talk about a time when you and your family moved to a new place, or when someone you know moved to a new place. Why did you move? What did you bring with you? Pause this audio here.

The Israelites who prepare to return to the land of Judah collect the resources from their neighbors, whom King Cyrus instructed to give gifts to the exiles. The neighbors give silver and gold articles, goods and livestock, valuable gifts, and animals for freewill offerings that the people can give to Yahweh.

King Cyrus takes out the articles that belong to the temple of Yahweh. Remember that at the end of the book of 2 Chronicles, King Nebuchadnezzar took these articles from the temple of Yahweh and Nebuchadnezzar put these articles in his own temple of his gods in Babylon. During the time of King Nebuchadnezzar, when a king conquered another nation or people, the king took the conquered nation's gods and put their idols, or images, in his own temple. This showed that the conquering king won over the other people and that the conquering king had power over their gods. Unlike the other people groups, there were no images of Yahweh in Yahweh's temple, so King Nebuchadnezzar took the gold and silver articles from the temple of Yahweh instead and put these articles in his temple to show his victory over the Israelites.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: In your community or country, when one group conquers a different group, what does the winning group do to show their victory? For example, does the winning group take anything from the losing group? What does the winning group do with the things they take from the losing group? Pause this audio here.

It is surprising that Cyrus gives these valuable temple articles back to the Israelites. King Cyrus tells Mithredath, the treasurer, to bring out these articles, and Mithredath carefully counts the temple articles. Mithredath gives the articles to Sheshbazzar, the prince of Judah. Sheshbazzar is probably the leader of the people of Judah, and not the son of a king. Sheshbazzar brings these temple articles with the group of exiles who travel back to Jerusalem.

Defining the Scenes
-------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 1:1–11 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will define the scenes, characters, and setting of this passage. Then the group will visualize this passage.

This passage has four scenes.

**In the first scene:** Yahweh causes or moves the spirit of Cyrus, the king of Persia, to fulfill the word of Yahweh that Jeremiah spoke.

**In the second scene:** Cyrus sends a message to the Jewish exiles in Babylon. Cyrus gives the exiles permission to return home to Jerusalem in Judah in order to rebuild the temple. The neighbors of the exiles should give the exiles gifts as the Jews leave Babylon.

**In the third scene:** Some exiles prepare to return to Jerusalem in Judah. The neighbors of these people give gifts to the people who prepare to return to Jerusalem.

**In the fourth scene:** Cyrus gives the gold and silver articles from the temple of Yahweh back to the Jewish exiles so that the exiles can bring these articles back to Jerusalem. There are 5,400 articles.

The characters in this passage are:

* Cyrus, the king of Persia
* Yahweh
* Jeremiah
* The Jewish exiles in Babylon
* Neighbors who remain in the land of Babylon
* Leaders of the families of the tribes of Judah and Benjamin
* Priests and Levites
* King Nebuchadnezzar
* Mithredath, the treasurer
* And Sheshbazzar, the prince of Judah

As a group, pay attention to these parts of the passage's setting:

Remember that this story happens a long time after the Babylonians took the people of Judah into exile in Babylon. Remember also that the first part of the story is very similar to 2 Chronicles 36:22–23\. It is possible that the storyteller of 2 Chronicles is the same storyteller here in the book of Ezra. Or maybe the storyteller in Ezra wants to connect the two stories together so that people remember what happened in the book of 2 Chronicles.

In the first scene, we learn that it is the first year of the rule of King Cyrus of the kingdom of Persia. Remember that Cyrus is the new ruler over the entire region, including the lands of Babylon and Judah.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: Tell a few different stories that are about the same person or character. Pay attention to how you start the second or third story about this person or character. How do you introduce the person? How do you help people remember what happened in the last story so they are ready for a new story? How will you start this story about King Cyrus and the exiles who return to Judah from Babylon? Pause this audio here.

We also learn that the events in this story happen in order to make the prophecy of the prophet Jeremiah happen. Remember that the prophet Jeremiah said the Israelites would be in exile for 70 years. It has probably not yet been 70 years since the exiles went to Babylon from the land of Judah. But it is possible that the prophecy of Jeremiah means it is 70 years from the time the Babylonians destroy the temple of Yahweh to the time that the people rebuild the temple of Yahweh in Jerusalem.

The storyteller says the prophecy of Jeremiah is the word of Yahweh, that Jeremiah speaks with his mouth. "Jeremiah speaks with his mouth" is special language that means Jeremiah was the prophet who spoke the words about the return of the Israelites to their homeland. Yahweh told Jeremiah what to say to the people.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How will you translate the idea that Yahweh gave the prophet Jeremiah words to say, and that this current story makes those things happen as Jeremiah said they would? Pause this audio here.

Yahweh is the one who moves the spirit of King Cyrus to make a proclamation for all the people in the kingdom of Persia. Other ways to translate this are: "Yahweh puts a thought in the mind of Cyrus," or "Yahweh gives Cyrus this desire." Yahweh causes Cyrus to act, but Yahweh does not push Cyrus to act. We learn what the proclamation is in the next scene.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: Tell a story that includes a part about the spirit, which is the thoughts or desires of one of the characters. Pay attention to how you talk about this invisible part of that character. Pause this audio here.

In the second scene, the storyteller says the proclamation of King Cyrus. First, the storyteller says this is the proclamation of King Cyrus. This is a formal introduction to the message, and it was common for kings or rulers to make proclamations like this during the time of King Cyrus. Remember that this is an oral message that heralds told to the people all throughout the kingdom of Persia. Cyrus also put this proclamation in writing, maybe on a paper that the heralds carried with them as they shared the proclamation with people throughout the kingdom.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How will you introduce the message from King Cyrus? Here are some translation examples:

* I, Cyrus, ruler of Persia, say...; or
* This is what Cyrus, the king of Persia, says....

Pause this audio here.

There are three parts of the message of King Cyrus. First, Cyrus says that Yahweh told Cyrus to build Yahweh a temple in Jerusalem in Judah. Then, Cyrus says Yahweh's people from Judah have permission to go to Jerusalem to build the temple for Yahweh. Finally, Cyrus says the neighbors of the exiles should give gifts to the exiles for the temple for Yahweh. There is no specific information in this message about how Yahweh's people should rebuild the temple of Yahweh. Later in the book of Ezra, the storyteller repeats the message of Cyrus, and the storyteller includes specific instructions for how the people should rebuild the temple of Yahweh.

In the first part of his message, Cyrus says that Yahweh, the God of heaven, gave Cyrus all the kingdoms on the earth. Remember, although this could mean that Cyrus follows Yahweh, it probably just means that Cyrus respects other people and their gods. Yahweh told Cyrus to build a house for Yahweh in Jerusalem in the land of Judah. In this story, the "house" means the temple of God. Remember that the temple represented God's presence among his people, and it was very important for the Israelites to worship God in the temple.

Also, remember that there already was a temple in Jerusalem before the exile to Babylon. It may be helpful to say that Yahweh told Cyrus to rebuild the house for Yahweh in Jerusalem. We know that the books of Ezra and Nehemiah tell the story of how the people rebuild the house of God.

In the second part of the message, King Cyrus says that any of God's people who want to return to Jerusalem are free to go back. It seems that Cyrus himself will not go to Jerusalem to build the temple of Yahweh, but that Cyrus will permit the Jews, or Yahweh's people from the southern Israelite Kingdom of Judah, to go to Jerusalem to build the temple of Yahweh. In this story, "God's people" means the Jews and their descendants who worshiped Yahweh at the temple in Jerusalem.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How will you show that any of the people of God may return to Jerusalem? For example, it may be best to translate this as a question, "Who is there among you of all God's people?" Pause this audio here.

Cyrus also says, "may their God be with them." Cyrus is talking about all of the Jewish exiles who are going back to the land of Judah. Most people think that this means King Cyrus wants to bless, or speak good words about, the people who will return to the land of Judah.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: What are some common blessings in your language that you say to someone before that person goes on a long journey? Where will you include this blessing in your translation? For example, you could choose to include the blessing at the beginning of this part of the message or at the end of this part of the message. Pause this audio here.

Cyrus gives the people permission to leave the land of Babylon, but Cyrus does not order the people to leave Babylon. We know that some exiles choose to stay in Babylon, possibly because they are doing well economically and do not want to leave a good place. Remember that the exiles are not slaves or captives in Babylon at this time.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How will you show that Cyrus gives the people permission to leave Babylon? Pause this audio here.

Cyrus gives the exiles permission to go up to the city of Jerusalem in the land of Judah. The city of Jerusalem is on a hill, which is a raised area of land, so people often said, "Go up to Jerusalem." Also, people often traveled "up" to a sacred or important place, or people said "go up" to mean travel north. In this story, "go up to Jerusalem" means travel from Babylon along the Euphrates River to the city of Jerusalem.

Stop here and look at a map that includes Jerusalem, the land of Judah, Babylon, the city of Babylon, and the kingdom of Persia again as a group if needed. Pause this audio here.

Also, stop here and discuss this question as a group: Tell a story about a time you or someone you know traveled from one place to a more important or larger place or city. Pay attention to how you talk about going to that more important place or city. How will you translate "go up to Jerusalem" in this story? Pause this audio here.

Cyrus also gives the exiles permission to rebuild the temple of Yahweh in Jerusalem. In the original language, the storyteller says that Yahweh is the God of Israel, who is the God in Jerusalem. Remember that the Persians believed that gods only ruled over the land of their own people, but we know that God rules over all the world.

In the third part of the message, King Cyrus says the exiles' neighbors should help the exiles who choose to go back to Jerusalem in the land of Judah. In the original language, the storyteller says that "the men of the place should support each one who remains, in whatever place he may travel to." The "men of the place" means the neighbors, or people who live near the Jewish exiles. These neighbors could be other Jewish exiles who do not travel back to the land of Judah, or they could also include non\-Jewish neighbors in Babylon. "Each one who remains" probably means the other Jews who had also come to live in exile in Babylon. The main idea is that wherever the Jewish exiles are in the land of Babylon, their neighbors should give these exiles support as the exiles prepare to return to the land of Judah.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How will you translate this part of the message of King Cyrus about the gifts the neighbors should give to the exiles? Pause this audio here.

Cyrus says that the neighbors should give gifts to the exiles. The exiles will likely use these gifts for their journey and to help rebuild the temple in Jerusalem. Remember that the gifts include silver, gold, goods, livestock, and freewill offerings. Silver and gold are valuable metals that people use to make things like jewelry. Goods are things for work and can mean equipment, materials, or utensils. Livestock are animals that people raise, like sheep, goats, and cattle. Freewill offerings are gifts that Israelites give in the temple of Yahweh because they want to, not because Yahweh requires them to.

Stop here and look at pictures of silver and gold. Pause this audio here.

Also, stop here and discuss this question as a group: Describe a ritual that you do because you want to do the ritual, instead of because someone requires you to do the ritual. Pause this audio here.

In the third scene, the Jewish exiles hear the message from King Cyrus, and some of the exiles prepare to do exactly what King Cyrus says to do. These exiles prepare to return to Jerusalem to build the temple of Yahweh. Remember that the most important part about the exiles going back to Jerusalem is to rebuild the temple of Yahweh so that the Jewish exiles could worship Yahweh again.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: Think about your discussion from the previous step about a time when your leaders or rulers had an important message to give to your community. How did people respond to the message? What would be the best way to translate the beginning of this scene about how the Jewish exiles respond to the message of King Cyrus? Pause this audio here.

It is important to remember that Yahweh moves the spirits of some of the Jewish exiles so that these exiles want to return to Jerusalem. Not all of the exiles want to return to the land of Judah, but only some of the exiles. The storyteller uses the same words in the original language here for "moves the spirit" of the exiles as the storyteller used earlier in the story when Yahweh moved the spirit of Cyrus. Make sure to translate "move the spirit" in the same way that you did earlier in this story.

The Jews who prepare to travel back to the land of Judah include many leaders of the tribes of Judah and Benjamin. You may remember from 1 Chronicles that it was the tribes of Judah and Benjamin that the Babylonians took into exile. The storyteller says these people are the "heads of the father's houses," which probably means they are leaders over extended family groups. Some priests and Levites also prepare to make the journey back to Jerusalem. Yahweh may also move the spirits of other Jews from the land of Judah who are not leaders of the tribes of Judah and Benjamin or religious leaders. It is possible that other non\-leaders from Judah and Benjamin also came. Cyrus has not given the Jews a limit on how many people can go back. Cyrus has also not said when the people should go back.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How will you translate "heads of father's houses" in this story? How will you show that Yahweh moves the spirits of these Jewish exiles? Pause this audio here.

Before the exiles leave on their journey, it is likely that the exiles needed time to sell their property and time to prepare for the long journey. We know from another part of the book of Ezra that the journey from Babylon to Jerusalem would take the exiles about four to five months.

In the second part of the third scene, neighbors of the exiles who will return to Jerusalem give generous gifts to the returning exiles. Remember that it is possible these neighbors are other Jews, or they might also include non\-Jews. Some people think these gifts are similar to the gifts the Egyptians gave to the Israelites when the Israelites left Egypt many years before.

In the fourth scene, Cyrus brings out the articles that belong to the temple of Yahweh. These articles are valuable objects that people made of silver and gold that priests and Levites used in the temple of Yahweh to hold and deal with animals' blood, incense, or other offerings.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How will you translate "articles" in this story? Look at a picture of various temple articles like gold and silver bowls, censers for incense, and utensils. Pause this audio here.

Remember that before this story, King Nebuchadnezzar took these articles out of the temple of Yahweh and put the articles in Nebuchadnezzar's own temple in Babylon. The storyteller actually uses the same word in the original language that Nebuchadnezzar took these articles out of the temple of Yahweh, and that now Cyrus brings these things out of the temple of Nebuchadnezzar's god. King Nebuchadnezzar took these articles by force, but Cyrus sets these things free. It may be helpful to say first what King Nebuchadnezzar did in the past, and then say what King Cyrus does in this current story. For example, "In the past, King Nebuchadnezzar had taken the articles out of the temple of Yahweh in Jerusalem and brought these articles to the temple of his god in Babylon. Now, King Cyrus takes these same articles out of that temple in Babylon."

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: What is the best order to translate the actions of King Nebuchadnezzar in the past and King Cyrus in this story? How will you show the contrast between what King Nebuchadnezzar did in the past and what Cyrus does in this story? Pause this audio here.

We also learn that Cyrus does not bring out the articles himself, but Cyrus gives Mithredath, the treasurer, the responsibility to bring the articles out of the temple of the gods of Nebuchadnezzar. The Persians had a god named Mithra, and "Mithredath" means "gift of Mithra." Mithredath was an important official, and he was responsible for the treasures in the temples in Babylon. Mithredath counts out the temple articles, and then Mithredath gives these articles to Sheshbazzar, the prince of Judah. Sheshbazzar is the leader of the first groups of exiles who return to the land of Judah, but Sheshbazzar is not the son of a king.

Mithredath carefully counts the articles, and the articles include 30 gold dishes, 1,000 silver dishes, 29 knives, 30 gold bowls, 410 other silver bowls, and 1,000 other articles. It is difficult to know exactly how to translate each of these articles. "Dishes" might also be "basins," rounded dishes in which you can hold liquid, like a bowl. "Knives" could be "pans." The knives are possibly for people to use when they sacrifice animals in the temple. The "other silver bowls" could be bowls of a different kind.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How will you translate the names of each of the temple articles that Mithredath counts? Look at the photos of the temple articles again if needed. Pause this audio here.

It is also difficult to know why the total number of articles is 5,400\. The number of articles listed before the total is only 2,499\. Some people think that the list only includes the most important or valuable articles, or that someone made an error when they wrote the numbers of the articles. Remember, do not change any of the numbers, even though the numbers do not seem to add up. It is also possible that the list does not include the bronze articles, which are not as valuable. Or maybe Cyrus does not give back the total number of articles at the same time.

Sheshbazzar brings these articles with him when he returns to the land of Judah from the land of Babylon. Remember that other exiles also travel with Sheshbazzar, and these Jews are the descendants of people whom Nebuchadnezzar took into exile in Babylon. It is important for the history of the Jews that the Jews who return to the land of Judah will use the same articles in the temple of Yahweh that their ancestors used in the past before their exile to Babylon. Also, it is important that the Jews travel with these articles from the temple because these articles represent the presence of Yahweh for the exiles, since there is no longer any temple.

The storyteller does not describe the journey of the exiles from the land of Babylon to the land of Judah. We know from later in the book of Ezra that Ezra traveled for about four months to make the journey from Babylon to the city of Jerusalem, so it probably also took the exiles about four months to return from Babylon to Judah.

Stop here and look again at a map of Babylon, Jerusalem, and Judah. Pause this audio here.

Now, the group should storyboard, draw, or use objects to visualize the story and the action in it.

Embodying the Text
------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 1:1–11 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will dramatize the passage.

This passage has four scenes.

The characters in this passage are:

* Cyrus, the king of Persia
* Yahweh
* Jeremiah
* The Jewish exiles in Babylon
* Neighbors who remain in the land of Babylon
* Leaders of the families of the tribes of Judah and Benjamin
* Priests and Levites
* King Nebuchadnezzar
* Mithredath, the treasurer
* And Sheshbazzar, the prince of Judah

As a group, act out the passage twice. You should act out the passage in your own language.

First, act out the passage without stopping. Pay attention to the dialogue, flow, plotline, and chronology of the passage. Make sure you do not skip acting out the difficult or important parts of the passage. Help each other remember every part of the passage.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage.

The group should act out this passage a second time. At certain points in the drama, pause the drama and ask the person playing the character, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person playing the character should answer what they would be feeling or thinking if they were that character. Then continue the drama.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage a second time.

The following is an example of the drama and possible responses.

A long time before the start of this story, the prophet Jeremiah made a prophecy that one day the Jews would return from Babylon to the land of Judah. In this story, Cyrus became the king of Persia. In the first year of the rule of King Cyrus, Yahweh gave Cyrus the desire to make a proclamation for the people throughout the entire kingdom of Persia. The people also wrote down the proclamation. Yahweh did this in order to fulfill the prophecy of the prophet Jeremiah that the Jews would return to the land of Judah.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing King Cyrus, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* I am happy to be the new ruler of this large kingdom of Persia. I rule over many kings and kingdoms; or
* I think it is a good idea to make a proclamation for all of Persia to hear. I want the people to be happy in my kingdom, and this proclamation will help the people; or
* It is important to write down this proclamation so that no one forgets what I say in my message to all the people in the kingdom of Persia.

Also, ask the person playing Yahweh, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* I am willing to use another foreign ruler to fulfill my plans for my people, the Jews; or
* I spoke through my prophet, Jeremiah, that I would bring the exiles home from Babylon to the land of Judah, and I will keep my promise; or
* The Jews have been in exile long enough, and it is time for the exiles to come back home to the land of Judah.

> Continue the drama.

This is what Cyrus, the king of Persia, said: "Yahweh, the God of heaven, gave me all of the kingdoms of the earth. Yahweh chose me to build a temple for Yahweh at Jerusalem in the land of Judah. Any of the people who belong to Yahweh have my permission to go up to Jerusalem. You may go to Jerusalem to build a temple for Yahweh, the God of Israel, who is in Jerusalem. May your God help you! The neighbors of every place in Babylon should give gifts to the Jews who choose to return to the land of Judah. These neighbors should give silver, gold, equipment, animals, and other freewill offerings for the temple of God in Jerusalem."

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing King Cyrus, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* I recognize that Yahweh, the God of the Jews, is powerful. I do not want to build a temple for Yahweh myself, but I want the Jews to know that I honor and respect their God. So I am glad to let some of the Jews return to their homes so they can build a temple for Yahweh. I have also let other people go back to their lands, too; or
* I am happy to let some Jews go back to their land of Judah. The people will be happier in their own land, where the people will worship their own God. And the people will like me since I let them go home. It is better for me if some people leave so that all of the people are happier; or
* It is good for me if some of the Jews go back to the land of Judah. I hope to take the land of Egypt someday, and if there are some Jews in the land of Judah, they will help keep the land of Judah under my control.

Also, ask the people playing the Jews, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The people might answer things like:

* We are shocked that King Cyrus gave us permission to leave Babylon and return to the land of Judah! King Nebuchadnezzar forced us to come here in exile, and now a different foreign ruler is telling us we can finally go home to the land of Judah; or
* We are so happy to think about rebuilding the temple of Yahweh so that we can worship Yahweh in the temple in Jerusalem again. We want to return to the land of Judah as soon as we can; or
* We are not sure if we want to go back to the land of Judah. Judah is very far away, and we are content in our lives here in Babylon now. Maybe we will just stay here.

Continue the drama.

Yahweh stirs the spirits of many Jews so that these Jews want to return to Jerusalem to build the temple for Yahweh. These Jews include many leaders of the tribes of Judah and Benjamin, and many priests and Levites. The neighbors of these Jews give the Jews generous gifts of silver articles, gold, goods, livestock, expensive gifts, and also freewill offerings.

Pause the drama. Ask the people playing the heads of the families of the tribes of Judah and Benjamin and the priests and Levites, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The people might answer things like:

* We are very happy that we have permission to leave Babylon and finally go back to the land of Judah! We have heard about the land of Judah from our fathers and grandfathers, and we want to return to our land; or
* We are ready to sell our property and pack our things so we can make the long journey back to the land of Judah; or
* We are looking forward to being back in the land of Judah. We will finally be able to rebuild the temple of Yahweh and worship Yahweh in his temple; or
* We are glad that Yahweh is fulfilling the prophecy of the prophet Jeremiah. We can finally go home to the land of Judah; or
* We are surprised that our neighbors gave us gifts! This will help us make the journey back to the land of Judah, and the gifts will also help us build the temple of Yahweh and start new lives in the land of Judah.

Also, ask the people playing the neighbors who remain in the land of Babylonia, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The people might answer things like:

* We are Jews, but we definitely do not want to return to the land of Judah. That was the land of our ancestors, but we have new lives here in Babylon. We will be happy to give gifts to the Jews who choose to leave so that they have support for their journey; or
* We are happy for our neighbors, even if we do not worship the same god as they do. We will give these neighbors gifts so that they can have a good journey back to their land; or
* We wish we did not have to give our neighbors these gifts. Yahweh is not our god, and we do not want to give gifts for the temple of a different god. But King Cyrus told us to give these gifts, so we will give them.

> Continue the drama.

In the past, King Nebuchadnezzar had taken the articles out of the temple of Yahweh in Jerusalem and brought these articles to the temple of his god in Babylon. In this story, King Cyrus took these same articles out of that temple in Babylon. King Cyrus gave Mithredath, the treasurer, the task of bringing out the articles. Mithredath counted out the articles to Sheshbazzar, the prince of Judah. This is the inventory of the articles of the temple of Yahweh that Cyrus gave back to the Jews: 30 gold dishes, 1,000 silver dishes, 29 knives, 30 gold bowls, 410 other silver bowls, and 1,000 other articles. There were 5,400 gold and silver articles. Sheshbazzar brings all of these articles with him when the exiles leave Babylon to return to the land of Judah.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing King Cyrus, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* These Jews do not have any idols, so I want them to take these articles home to their land and their god instead of keeping these articles here in Babylon; or
* Even though these articles are valuable, it is important to me that the people in my kingdom are happy. I have sent other people home with their gods, and I think that every people should be able to worship their own god in their own land; or
* King Nebuchadnezzar lived a long time ago, and I do things differently from Nebuchadnezzar. Nebuchadnezzar chose to destroy the temple of these people and bring the people here to Babylon. I choose to let the people go back to their land and their god. I think the Jews will be loyal to me because I am being kind to them.

Also, ask the people playing the heads of the families of the tribes of Judah and Benjamin and the priests and Levites, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The people might answer things like:

* We are shocked that King Cyrus is returning these temple articles! Now we can return to the land of Judah and rebuild the temple of Yahweh to worship in the same way that our ancestors worshipped Yahweh; or
* It is very special to us that we will bring these temple articles back to the new temple in Jerusalem; or
* We are very thankful to King Cyrus because he is returning these important articles to us; or
* We know that Yahweh has power over all the kings of the earth because Yahweh told King Cyrus to let us go back to Jerusalem, and Cyrus is even returning the temple articles; or
* We hope that we can worship and follow Yahweh better than our ancestors did. We know that Yahweh punished our ancestors when the Babylonians took them into exile, and we hope that we can follow Yahweh faithfully.

Filling the Gaps
----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 1:1–11 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will discuss key terms and words in this passage.

Cyrus becomes the king of Persia. A king rules over a city or a territory called a kingdom. King Cyrus rules over a very large kingdom that includes many smaller kingdoms.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How will you translate **king** and **kingdom** in this passage? For more information on king and kingdom, refer to the Master Glossary. Pause this audio here.

Yahweh moves the spirit of King Cyrus so that King Cyrus would make a proclamation. Yahweh does this in order to fulfill the word of Yahweh that Jeremiah spoke in the past. Yahweh is God's personal name. The spirit of a person is the thoughts, feelings, and emotions of a person. A proclamation is an official message that a ruler sends throughout his kingdom. People speak the proclamation out loud for the people to hear the message from the ruler. The word of Yahweh is the message that Yahweh told to the prophet Jeremiah in the past.

Stop here and discuss as a group how you will translate **Yahweh** and "**word of Yahweh**." For more information, refer to Yahweh and Word in the Master Glossary. Translate Yahweh and "word of Yahweh" the same as you have in other parts of the Old Testament. Pause this audio here.

Also, stop here and discuss this question as a group: How will you translate the idea that Yahweh moves the **spirit** of King Cyrus? For more information on spirit, refer to the Master Glossary. Pause this audio here.

Also, stop here and discuss this question as a group: How will you translate "**proclamation**" in this story? Translate "proclamation" the same as you have in other parts of the Old Testament. Pause this audio here.

Cyrus says that Yahweh, the God of heaven, gave Cyrus all the kingdoms of the earth. Heaven is where God lives.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How will you translate **God** and **heaven** in this passage? For more information on God and heaven, refer to the Master Glossary. Pause this audio here.

Cyrus also says that Yahweh told Cyrus to build a new temple for Yahweh at Jerusalem in Judah. A temple is a place where people worship God, and other people have different temples to worship their gods. Another name for this temple for Yahweh is the "house of God," or the "house of Yahweh." Jerusalem was the capital city of the land of Judah, and the first temple was also in Jerusalem before King Nebuchadnezzar destroyed the temple. Judah was the name of the southern kingdom before King Nebuchadnezzar took the people of Judah into exile in Babylon.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How will you translate **temple**, **Jerusalem**, and **Judah** in this passage? For more information on temple or "house of God," Jerusalem, and Judah, refer to the Master Glossary. Pause this audio here.

Cyrus says that the people in his kingdom have permission to go up to Jerusalem in Judah to build the temple of Yahweh, the God of Israel. In this story, Israel means the people of Israel, or the descendants of Jacob.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How will you translate **Israel** in this passage? For more information on Israel, refer to the Master Glossary. Pause this audio here.

Cyrus says that the neighbors of the Jews should give gifts to the Jews who are returning, including freewill offerings. A freewill offering is a gift that the Jews gave to Yahweh in the temple because they wanted to give it, not because Yahweh required it.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How will you translate **freewill offerings** in this passage? For more information, refer to freewill offerings in the Master Glossary. Pause this audio here.

After the people hear the message from Cyrus, Yahweh moves the spirits of many Jewish exiles to return to Jerusalem to rebuild the temple of Yahweh. These Israelites include the leaders of the fathers' houses of Judah and Benjamin, the priests, the Levites, and maybe some other Israelites. "Heads of father's houses" means the leaders of the groups of households of the tribes of Judah and Benjamin. A household is all of the people who live together in one house. Priests are descendants of Aaron from the Israelite tribe of Levi, and priests worked in the temple of Yahweh in Jerusalem. Levites are also from the tribe of Levi, but they are not descendants of Aaron. The Levites also helped in the temple of Yahweh in Jerusalem.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How will you translate "**heads of fathers' houses**," **priests**, and **Levites** in this story? For more information on heads of fathers' houses, refer to **household** in the Master Glossary. For more information on priests and Levites, refer to the Master Glossary. Translate "heads of fathers' households," priests and Levites in the same way as you have in previous books of the Bible. Pause this audio here.

In the past, King Nebuchadnezzar took the articles from the temple of Yahweh and put these articles in the house of King Nebuchadnezzar's false gods. Nebuchadnezzar worshipped many gods, including Marduk, the king of the Babylonian gods. Babylon had many temples where the Babylonian people kept idols, or statues, of their gods.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How will you translate **gods** in this passage? For more information on gods, refer to the Master Glossary. Pause this audio here.

Sheshbazzar brings all of the gold and silver articles with him when the exiles all go back to Jerusalem. Exile means that a person must live outside of their own country, and that person is not able to return home. The Babylonians forced the people of Judah to leave Judah and to move to Babylon as exiles, but now the exiles are going home.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How will you translate **exile** in this passage?

Speaking the Word
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of the passage in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this session, retell the passage, as a group, in your own language.

First, the whole group should practice telling the passage in your own language. Remember to include the things you decided in previous steps, like chronology, words, phrases, emotions, or anything else you discussed.

One group member should tell a few sentences of the passage and pause. Another group member should tell the next part of the passage and pause. Do this until the group has retold the whole passage. The group members can correct each other in this step.

Pause this audio here.

Next, each member of the group should tell the whole passage without interruption at least one time. A group member can use random objects, motions, or their storyboard to help them remember the passage.

Pause this audio here.

Next, the group can decide whose version of the passage you like the most. You may decide that you liked the way one group member told one scene but you liked the way a different group member told another scene.

Pause this audio here.

Finally, the group can put together a final version of the passage that everyone agrees on. Each group member should practice telling this final version of the passage.

## Ezra 2:1–20 (id: eng-ezr-p2a-v1)

Hear and Heart
--------------

In this step, hear Ezra 2:1–20 and put it in your hearts.

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 2:1–20 three times, in three different translations, if possible.

Pause this audio here.

Now, as a group, discuss the following questions:

1. What do you like in this passage? Pause this audio here.
2. What do you not like or not understand in this passage? Pause this audio here.
3. What does this passage tell us about God? Pause this audio here.
4. What does this passage tell us about people? Pause this audio here.
5. How does this passage affect our daily lives? Pause this audio here.
6. Who do you know who needs to hear this passage?

Setting the Stage
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 2:1–20 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In the previous story, Yahweh moved the spirit of King Cyrus to say that the Jewish exiles could go back to Jerusalem in Judah to rebuild the temple of Yahweh. Yahweh also moved the spirits of many Jewish leaders, priests, and other Jews so that they would prepare to return to Jerusalem to rebuild the temple of Yahweh. Cyrus returned the temple articles to the Israelites, and Sheshbazzar brought these temple articles with the people when they returned to Jerusalem. Now, the storyteller lists the people who return from Babylon to Jerusalem in Judah. This is the first part of a six\-part list of the exiles who return to Jerusalem in the land of Judah.

It is not clear how much time has passed between the previous story and this current story. It is also not clear if this list only includes the people who are in the first group to return to Jerusalem in Judah, or if this list also includes people who return later. You will remember that later, people called these Israelites from the province of Judah "Jews." The storyteller in Ezra sometimes calls these people Israelites, and sometimes he calls them Jews. Remember that many Jews also choose to stay in Babylon, and these people do not return to the land of Judah.

As the passage begins, the storyteller says these are "the people of the province" who return from exile in Babylon. "The people of the province" probably means the people who used to live in the province, or land, of Judah. Remember that in the past, King Nebuchadnezzar took the people by force from Judah to Babylon into exile, and now King Cyrus of Persia has said that the Jews in Babylon can return home to Judah. It is possible that some Jews in Babylon were still alive from the time when King Nebuchadnezzar took the Israelites into exile. But many of the people in this list were born in exile in the land of Babylon. Remember that the exiles have been living in the land of Babylon for at least 50 years. When the exiles first came to the land of Babylon, they were all prisoners. But now the new generation of exiles is no longer prisoners.

Stop here and look at a map that includes the land of Babylon, the city of Babylon, the land of Judah, and the city of Jerusalem. Pause this audio here.

The people all returned to the cities or villages of their ancestors in the land of Judah. We know that all of the cities and villages in this list are very close to the city of Jerusalem in the land of Judah. Remember that many of the Israelites who make the journey to Judah have never been to Judah before. These people's ancestors told them about their cities and villages back in the land of Judah. Also, remember that the cities and villages in the land of Judah were probably mostly in ruins because King Nebuchadnezzar's soldiers had destroyed the cities and villages during the battle.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: Talk about a time when someone forced your ancestors, or the ancestors of someone you know, from their home. Where did your ancestors live? Did your ancestors return home? How did your ancestors know where to return? What did your ancestors do when they finally returned home? Pause this audio here.

There are 11 leaders who return with the people of Israel, and some people think that we should also include Sheshbazzar from the previous story. This would mean there are 12 leaders, which is similar to the 12 tribes of Israel. A similar passage in Nehemiah 7 says that 12 leaders return with the people of Israel, but the names of the leaders are a little different. Nehemiah 7 also includes lists of all the people returning to the land of Judah.

Stop here and listen to your recording of Nehemiah 7:4–73 if you have already translated this passage into your own language. Pause this audio here.

The leaders in this list include Zerubbabel, a descendant of King David, and Jeshua, a descendant of the last high priest before the exile. Other leaders are Nehemiah, Seraiah, Reelaiah, Mordecai, Bilshan, Mispar, Bigvai, Rehum, and Baanah. It is interesting that several of these leaders have Persian or Babylonian names, which shows that at least some of the Israelites in exile gave their children Persian or Babylonian names.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: In your culture, how do you decide what to name your children? Talk about someone you know who has an unusual name in your culture. Where did this unusual name come from? Pause this audio here.

In the rest of this list, and in the following five lists, we hear the number of men from each family of the people of Israel who return to the land of Judah. This list does not include women and children, and it probably only includes men over the age of 12\. For this passage, the storyteller lists the names of 18 families and the number of people in each family who return from Babylon to the land of Judah. It is interesting that the people in this current passage are not religious leaders, but ordinary Israelites. It is also likely that many of these people were wealthy or from the upper class because we learn in a later part of the list that many of the people in the list had servants or slaves.

For the first family, the storyteller says that 1,272 "sons" or descendants of Parosh come back to the land of Judah. Parosh is the name of these men's common ancestor, and not the name of their family or clan leader. It is possible that Parosh was the head of a family when King Nebuchadnezzar took the people from Judah to Babylon in exile, and now all of the descendants of Parosh share this common ancestor.

Stop here and discuss as a group: Give a list of your ancestors. Pay attention to how you identify the families of your ancestors. Pause this audio here.

For each family, the storyteller gives the name of the common ancestor and the number of the men who return to the land of Judah. In this passage, there is a total of 15,604 Israelite men who return to the land of Judah, plus the women and children in each of these men's families.

Many people have different ideas about why this list of returning exiles is so important. Some people think that it is a list to show who owns the land after the exile, or to know how many people owe taxes. Other people think this list shows who the true Jews from Judah are, the people who obey the commands of Yahweh and have not married other people outside of Israel. Or maybe people used this list in later generations to show that their ancestors were faithful to Yahweh.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: Talk about the list of ancestors you listed earlier. Why is your list of your ancestors important to you? Pause this audio here.

This list is also important because it shows that the true Israelites are the same people from before the exile. The Israelites need to be pure so that they can worship Yahweh correctly in the temple of Yahweh, and this list shows that the people who return to Judah have ancestors from Judah. Yahweh is not starting over with a new people, but Yahweh is continuing the story with the same people, the people of Israel.

Defining the Scenes
-------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 2:1–20 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will define the scenes, characters, and setting of this passage. Then the group will visualize this passage.

This passage has two scenes.

**In the first scene:** Many exiles return from Babylon to Jerusalem in Judah. There are 11 leaders of these Israelites.

**In the second scene:** The storyteller lists the names of 18 families and the number of people in each family who return to Jerusalem in Judah.

The characters in this passage are:

* The Israelites who return to the land of Judah
* King Nebuchadnezzar
* 11 Israelite leaders, including Zerubbabel and Jeshua
* 18 Israelite families
* And the Israelites in each of the 18 Israelite families

As a group, pay attention to these parts of the passage's setting:

Remember that in the previous story, King Cyrus gave the Jewish exiles permission to return to Jerusalem in Judah to build the temple of Yahweh. Many of the exiles prepared to make the journey from Babylon to Jerusalem in Judah. At the end of the previous story, King Cyrus also gave the temple articles back to the exiles to bring to Jerusalem in Judah. Remember that the exiles returned to the land of Judah to rebuild the temple of Yahweh.

Now, the storyteller pauses the story's action to give the list of the people who return from Babylon to Jerusalem in Judah. These exiles show great faith in Yahweh by listening to Yahweh and by returning to the land of Judah, even though Yahweh did not promise that he would restore the kingdom of Judah. This list begins a new section in the book of Ezra. This is the first part of the six\-part list of the exiles who return from Babylon to Jerusalem in Judah. The storyteller lists a different group of people in each of the six parts of the list.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: Tell a story that includes a list of the ancestors of some of the characters in the story. Pay attention to how you begin the list of ancestors in the story. How will you start this new section of the book of Ezra? Pause this audio here.

In the first scene, the "people of the province" return from Babylon to the land of Judah. These Israelites are mostly from the Israelite tribes of Judah and Benjamin, and they used to live in the province of Judah. A province is a part of a land or a part of a kingdom. In the original language, the storyteller says the "sons of the province," which means the people who belong to a specific land. Remember that "people of the province" probably means the people from the land of Judah, who are Israelites, or more specifically, "Jewish" people. However, some people think it could mean people from the province of Babylon or people from the province of the Persian Empire. Remember that the land of Judah is not an independent nation at this time, so "people of the province" might mean the people who used to live in Judah but now are part of a province of the Persian Empire.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How will you translate "people of the province" in this passage? Pause this audio here.

In the original language, the storyteller says that the exiles go up to Jerusalem in Judah. The Jews always said that a person goes "up" to Jerusalem because the city of Jerusalem was on a hill and was an important holy place.

Stop here and look at a map that includes the land of Babylon, the city of Babylon, the land of Judah, and the city of Jerusalem again as a group, if needed. Pause this audio here.

Also, stop here and discuss this question as a group: What is the best way to translate this part of the passage? Some ideas include: "The exiles came up to Jerusalem," "The exiles went up to Jerusalem," and "The exiles returned to Jerusalem." Pause this audio here.

Remember that King Nebuchadnezzar and the Babylonians took these people, and the ancestors of these people, into exile in Babylon in the past. Other ways to describe the idea of exile are that the Babylonians captured the Israelites, the Babylonians took the Israelites away from their country, or the Babylonians made the Israelites captives. The Babylonians forced the Israelites to live in Babylon for a long time, probably about 50 years. Now, these Israelites from Judah who went into exile, and the descendants of these Israelites, are returning to Jerusalem in Judah.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How will you translate this part of the passage to show what King Nebuchadnezzar did in the past when he captured the exiles and took them to Babylon? How will you translate what the Israelites are doing now in this passage? Would it be best to say first what King Nebuchadnezzar did in the past? Pause this audio here.

The exiles went back to the towns of their ancestors. A town is a place where people live together in an organized community. Remember that many of the people going to Jerusalem have never been there before, but the people all go to the towns of their ancestors. In the original language, a town could be a very small settlement like a village, or a large settlement with walls around it like a city.

This story gives the names of 11 leaders who return to Jerusalem with the Israelites. Remember that some people think that Sheshbazzar also returns with the exiles. If that is true, then there would be 12 leaders altogether. However, in Nehemiah 7, the list of 12 leaders is different from the list in this current passage. It is possible that the Ezra 2 list and the Nehemiah 7 lists are different because of copying errors. However, you should not change the list in Ezra 2 to match Nehemiah 7\. There is no additional information about these 11 leaders in this list, but we know about the leaders from other parts of the Old Testament.

Zerubbabel and Jeshua are two important leaders who return with the exiles. Remember that Zerubbabel is a descendant of King David. Zerubbabel later becomes the governor of the land of Judah. Zerubbabel's grandfather was Jehoiachin, the king of Judah who was exiled to Babylon, and Zerubbabel was a descendant of King David. Some people think that Sheshbazzar came at the same time as Zerubbabel with this first group of exiles. Maybe Sheshbazzar was the representative from the Persian government, and Zerubbabel was the leader of the Israelites. Other people think that Sheshbazzar came first, and then Zerubbabel came back to Judah with a second group of exiles to rebuild the temple.

Jeshua is the descendant of Jehozadak, the last high priest before the exile. In other parts of the Old Testament, the storytellers call Jeshua "Joshua" instead, but it is the same person. This is not the same Joshua who led the Israelites into the promised land. Nehemiah in this list is not the same Nehemiah who rebuilt the Jerusalem wall years later that we hear about in the book of Nehemiah. Seraiah is the father of Ezra, the storyteller in this book. Mordecai is not the same Mordecai as in the book of Esther.

In the second scene, the storyteller lists the families of men who return to Jerusalem in Judah. Remember, this is only the first part of the six\-part list of exiles who return. It is interesting that the first part of the list only includes common people, and not the religious leaders of the Israelites. The storyteller calls these men the "men of the Israelites." In later passages, the storyteller lists people by the places they come from or the jobs they do, including priests, Levites, singers, temple servants, and other workers.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How will you introduce this list of the Israelites who return to Jerusalem in Judah? Here are some translation ideas:

* The list of the men of the people of Israel; or
* This is the list of the clans of Israel with the number of men who returned; or
* Here is a list of how many men returned from each family.

Pause this audio here.

Remember that these are the people who respond when Yahweh moves their spirits. Remember that other ways to translate this are: "Yahweh puts a thought in the minds of the people," or "Yahweh gives the people this desire," or "Yahweh causes the people to act, but Yahweh does not push the people to act." These people leave Babylon to restore the temple of Yahweh in Jerusalem in Judah. This could be a list of the people who are a true part of Israel, and not only the first people to return. Also, remember that the names in this list are not the names of the "heads" or leaders of the households. These names are all the names of ancestors before the exile, and these people probably used to live in Jerusalem before the exile to Babylon.

For each of the 18 families in the list in this passage, the storyteller first says, "the sons of," followed by the ancestor's name, and the number of men in the family of that ancestor. Remember that "sons" means descendants in this passage. Here are some translation ideas:

* There were 1,272 men related to the ancestor Parosh.
* There were 1,272 men from the clan of Parosh.
* Sons of Parosh, 1,272\.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: Remember your discussion in the first step about giving a list of your ancestors in your language. How will you translate the list of family groups in this passage? Be consistent in how you translate each family clan in the list. Pause this audio here.

The storyteller includes additional information for two of the families in this passage. The storyteller says that there are 2,812 men from the family of Pahath\-Moab. In the original language, "Pahath\-Moab" means the governor of Moab. It is possible that the name is two names, Pahath Moab. The storyteller says that these men are also descendants of Jeshua and Joab, who are probably in the same family as Pahath\-Moab.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How will you translate the information about the family of Pahath\-Moab? Pause this audio here.

The storyteller also includes more information about the sons of Ater and about Hezekiah. Some people think that Ater and Hezekiah are two different people, and that Ater is Hezekiah's distant relative. These people say that Hezekiah is a descendant of Ater in the same family group. Other people think that Ater and Hezekiah are the same person.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How will you translate the information about the family of Ater? Pause this audio here.

Remember that people have different ideas about why this list of returning exiles is important. The storyteller may include this list for practical reasons. Maybe the Israelite leaders used this list to help them organize the people on the journey back to Judah, or maybe the list was a record of who came back, even after people could no longer remember who came back. Some people think that the list shows who owns the land, and that future generations would look back at this list to prove their rights to the land in Judah.

This list could also be important for the Babylonian authorities to know who left Babylon, and for the people of the places where the Israelites traveled to know who and how many people passed through their land. Officials in Judah also may have needed to know who lived in Judah in order to help the people pay taxes to the Persian Empire.

The storyteller may include this list for spiritual reasons. The list might show who is a true Israelite, someone who obeys Yahweh. In future generations, it will be important to show that someone is a true Israelite, someone who has not intermarried with others and who still follows Yahweh. This list also shows that these Israelites are from the same people who had followed Yahweh before their exile to Babylon.

After this six\-part list, the storyteller will return to the main action of the story when the people begin to rebuild the temple of Yahweh.

Now, the group should storyboard, draw, or use objects to visualize the passage and the action in it.

Embodying the Text
------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 2:1–20 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will dramatize the passage.

This passage has two scenes.

The characters in this passage are:

* The Israelites who return to the land of Judah
* King Nebuchadnezzar
* 11 Israelite leaders, including Zerubbabel and Jeshua
* 18 Israelite families
* And the Israelites in each of the 18 Israelite families

Remember that many people probably heard this list at the time that the storyteller wrote the list. Also, remember that in future generations, people probably heard this list of the Israelites who returned to the land of Judah. So, here are some additional characters that might help you understand some of the emotions of the characters in this passage:

* Israelites who might hear this list in future generations
* And Babylonian or Persian officials who hear this list

As a group, act out the passage twice. You should act out the passage in your own language.

First, act out the passage without stopping. Pay attention to the dialogue, flow, plotline, and chronology of the passage. Make sure you do not skip acting out the difficult or important parts of the passage. Help each other remember every part of the passage.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage.

The group should act out this passage a second time. At certain points in the drama, pause the drama and ask the person playing the character, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person playing the character should answer what they would be feeling or thinking if they were that character. Then continue the drama.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage a second time.

The following is an example of the drama and possible responses.

In the past, King Nebuchadnezzar of Babylon captured many people in the land of Judah. King Nebuchadnezzar took these people to live in exile in the land of Babylon. Now, the descendants of these Israelite exiles return from the land of Babylon to the city of Jerusalem and to the land of Judah. These Israelites live in the towns where their ancestors used to live before the exile. The leaders of the Israelites are Zerubbabel, Jeshua, Nehemiah, Seraiah, Reelaiah, Mordecai, Bilshan, Mispar, Bigvai, Rehum, and Baanah.

Pause the drama. Ask the people playing the Israelites who return to the land of Judah, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The people might answer things like:

* We are happy to finally return to the land of our ancestors; or
* We have heard a lot about the land of Judah from our ancestors, and we are glad to finally get to see this land for ourselves; or
* That was a long journey from the land of Babylon to the land of Judah. We need time to rest and rebuild our towns.

Also, ask the people playing the leaders of the Israelites, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The people might answer things like:

* We are honored to lead the Israelites back to the land of Judah; or
* We are glad that King Cyrus of Persia let us leave the land of Babylon to return to the land of Judah; or
* We need to get ourselves organized before we start to rebuild the temple of Yahweh.

> Continue the drama.

And here is a list of how many men returned from each family. From the family of Parosh, there were 2,172 men. From the family of Shephatiah, there were 372 men. From the family of Arah, there were 775 men. From the family of Pahath\-Moab, through the sons of Jeshua and Joab, there were 2,812 men. From the family of Elam, there were 1,254 men. From the family of Zattu, there were 945 men. From the family of Zaccai, there were 760 men. From the family of Bani, there were 642 men. From the family of Bebai, there were 623 men.

Pause the drama. Ask the people who are playing Israelites who might hear this list in later generations, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The people might answer things like:

* We are proud that our ancestors are in this list of the people who returned from the land of Babylon to the land of Judah because our ancestors are true Israelites; or
* We know that our ancestors were a part of the people of Israel before the exile, and now we see that more of our ancestors also came back to the land of Judah after the exile; or
* Even though the number of men is small from each of these families, we know that each of these men, and their families, obeyed Yahweh when they came back to the land of Judah.

Continue the drama.

From the family of Azgad, there were 1,222 men. From the family of Adonikam, there were 666 men. From the family of Bigvai, there were 2,056 men. From the family of Adin, there were 454 men. From the family of Ater, whose son was Hezekiah, there were 98 men. From the family of Bezai, there were 323 men. From the family of Jorah, there were 112 men. From the family of Hashum, there were 223 men. From the family of Gibbar, there were 95 men.

Pause the drama. Ask the people who are playing the Babylonian or Persian officials who might hear this list, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The people might answer things like:

* This list is important because all of these people left the land of Babylon; or
* We need to keep track of these people so that we make sure these people pay all of the taxes that they need to pay to the Persian Empire; or
* We do not care if these people live in the land of Babylon or in the land of Judah. We only want these people to pay the taxes that they must pay.

Filling the Gaps
----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 2:1–20 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will discuss key terms and words in this passage.

"The people of the province," who are the people of the land of Judah, return to the land of Judah. These are the people who lived in Babylon in **exile** for many years. In the past, **King** Nebuchadnezzar of Babylon took these people into exile in the land of Babylon. A king is someone who rules over a city or a territory. Translate exile and king the same as you have in previous passages. For more information on king, refer to the Master Glossary.

The Israelites return to the city of **Jerusalem** and to the land of **Judah**. Jerusalem was the capital city of the kingdom of Judah in the past. The temple of Yahweh used to be in the city of Jerusalem. The kingdom of Judah was the southern kingdom after the nation of Israel split into the northern kingdom of Israel and the southern kingdom of Judah. Translate Jerusalem and Judah the same as you have in previous passages. For more information on Jerusalem and Judah, refer to the Master Glossary.

The Israelites also return to their own towns or cities. These are the towns where the ancestors of the Israelites lived before the exile to the land of Babylon. These towns are all within the land of Judah and are also close to the city of Jerusalem. In this case, towns could refer to a very small settlement like a village, or a large settlement with walls around it like a city.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How will you translate **town** in this passage? Pause this audio here.

The **Israelites** return to the land of Judah with 11 leaders. The storyteller gives a list of the men of the people of Israel who return to the land of Judah. The people of Israel are the "Israelites," or the descendants of Abraham, Isaac, and Jacob. We know that the Israelites who return to the land of Judah are mostly from the tribes of Judah and Benjamin, which used to be part of the southern kingdom of Judah. Remember that people used to call the northern kingdom "Israel," and these people intermarried and mostly no longer followed Yahweh. The storyteller probably says "Israelites" in this list to show that the people who return are true Israelites who still follow Yahweh and did not intermarry with other people groups who are not Israelites. Translate "people of Israel" the same as you have in previous passages. For more information on "the people of Israel," refer to **Israel** and Israelite in the Master Glossary.

The storyteller lists the number of sons of each family who return to the land of Israel. Sons means descendants in this list.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How will you translate **sons** in this passage? For more information on sons, refer to **descendants** in the Master Glossary.

Speaking the Word
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of the passage in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this session, retell the passage, as a group, in your own language.

First, the whole group should practice telling the passage in your own language. Remember to include the things you decided in previous steps, like chronology, words, phrases, emotions, or anything else you discussed.

One group member should tell a few sentences of the passage and pause. Another group member should tell the next part of the passage and pause. Do this until the group has retold the whole passage. The group members can correct each other in this step.

Pause this audio here.

Next, each member of the group should tell the whole passage without interruption at least one time. A group member can use random objects, motions, or their storyboard to help them remember the passage.

Pause this audio here.

Next, the group can decide whose version of the passage you like the most. You may decide that you liked the way one group member told one scene but you liked the way a different group member told another scene.

Pause this audio here.

Finally, the group can put together a final version of the passage that everyone agrees on. Each group member should practice telling this final version of the passage.

## Ezra 2:21–35 (id: eng-ezr-p2b-v1)

Hear and Heart
--------------

In this step, hear Ezra 2:21–35 and put it in your hearts.

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 2:21–35 three times, in three different translations, if possible.

Pause this audio here.

Now, as a group, discuss the following questions:

1. What do you like in this passage? Pause this audio here.
2. What do you not like or not understand in this passage? Pause this audio here.
3. What does this passage tell us about God? Pause this audio here.
4. What does this passage tell us about people? Pause this audio here.
5. How does this passage affect our daily lives? Pause this audio here.
