Setting the Stage
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 2:21–35 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In the previous passage, the storyteller began the list of the exiles who returned to Jerusalem in the land of Judah. Remember that Yahweh had moved the spirit of King Cyrus to let the Israelites go back to Jerusalem in Judah to rebuild the temple of Yahweh. In the first part of the list of the exiles who returned to Judah, we heard the names of the 11 leaders and the names of 18 families who also returned with the leaders. The storyteller said the number of people in each family who returned. Remember, these first 18 families are ordinary Israelites, and not religious leaders. Now, in this current passage, the storyteller says the names of 15 more groups of people. This is the second part of a six\-part list of the exiles who return to Jerusalem in the land of Judah.

In this passage, the storyteller lists the people according to the names of places in the land of Judah instead of according to the names of the people. It is not clear why the storyteller says the place names in this passage instead of the names of the people. Some people think that these people might be the poorer people who did not own land in their own names in the land of Judah. Other people think the place names in this list show how the people of Israel continue to have a place in the land of Israel, even after the exile. This shows that Yahweh continues to provide for the people of Israel. It is also possible that the storyteller chooses to describe the people in this passage according to place names because this was common during that time to list people in different ways in official documents.

Also, it is most likely that these are the names of places where the ancestors of the returning Israelites are from. Perhaps the Israelites who returned from Babylon also returned to live in these towns of their ancestors, but we are not sure. The people in this part of the list have not lived in any of these towns in the land of Judah before, but the ancestors of these people used to live in these towns before the exile to Babylon. Remember that this entire six\-part list includes only the men who choose to return to the land of Judah. However, we know that women and children also come back with the men.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: Talk about where you are from. Where are your ancestors from? Why is it important to be from a certain place, or in our culture, why would you want to know where someone is from? Pause this audio here.

The list of family groups in this passage who return to the land of Judah is also in the book of Nehemiah. The list in the book of Nehemiah is very similar to the list in this current passage.

Stop here and listen to your recording of Nehemiah 7:26–38 if you have already translated this passage into your own language. Pause this audio here.

For each of the 15 groups of people in this passage, the storyteller says the place or places where their ancestors are from, and the number of people in the group who return to the land of Judah. For example, the storyteller says that there are 123 sons of Bethlehem, and there are 56 men of Netophah. "Men of" and "sons of" have the same meaning, and they both show that these men had ancestors who used to live in the towns of Bethlehem or Netophah before the exile to Babylon. Throughout this passage, the storyteller sometimes says "men" of a place or "sons" of a place to describe each group of people who return to the land of Judah.

The storyteller lists the places in this passage in a specific order. The list starts with places in the south of the land of Judah. Then the list moves north, then northwest, then far north, then southwest, then northwest, and finally northeast. Maybe the storyteller lists the places this way in order to keep the list organized.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: List the important towns and cities in your region or country. Pay attention to how you listed them. Where did you start? What did you list next? Why do you think you listed these important towns and cities in this order? Pause this audio here.

The first two places are Bethlehem and Netophah. Both of these towns are south of Jerusalem in an area that used to be in the land of the tribe of Judah. Netophah was a city for the Levites.

Stop here and, as a group, look at a map that includes all 21 towns in this passage and the city of Jerusalem. Find Bethlehem and Netophah on the map. Pause this audio here.

All of the other places in this passage are in an area that used to be the land of the tribe of Benjamin. Anathoth and Azmaveth are both towns north of Jerusalem. Anathoth was the home of the prophet Jeremiah.

Stop here and, as a group, look at a map that includes all 21 towns in this passage and the city of Jerusalem. Find Anathoth and Azmaveth on the map. Pause this audio here.

Kiriath Jearim, Kephirah, and Beeroth are three towns northwest of Jerusalem. The storyteller groups these three towns together to give a total number of people for all three towns. This may be because these three towns are close together. We know from the book of 1 Samuel that the Israelites put the ark of Yahweh in the town of Kiriath Jearim after the Philistines gave the ark of Yahweh back to the Israelites. Kiriath Jearim, Kephirah, and Beeroth were three of the cities of the Gibeonites who tricked Joshua and made a treaty with the Israelites.

Stop here and, as a group, look at a map that includes all 21 towns in this passage and the city of Jerusalem. Find Kiriath Jearim, Kephirah, and Beeroth on the map. Pause this audio here.

Ramah, Geba, Micmash, Bethel, and Ai are all towns to the far north of Jerusalem. The storyteller groups Ramah and Geba together, and Bethel and Ai together. Again, this is probably because these towns are close together. Remember that Micmash is the place where Jonathan, the son of King Saul, fought against the Philistines. Most people believe that the Babylonians did not destroy Bethel, but that people destroyed Bethel later when the Persians became the rulers of the land.

Stop here and, as a group, look at a map that includes all 21 towns in this passage and the city of Jerusalem. Find Ramah, Geba, Micmash, Bethel, and Ai on the map. Pause this audio here.

Nebo, Magbish, the other Elam, and Harim are all towns southwest of Jerusalem. Remember, there was a person named Elam in the previous passage. It is possible that in this current passage, "the other Elam" means a person, and not the name of a town called "Elam." It is possible that Elam took the name of the place where he lived as his family name or as his personal name. This group of people is not the same group of people as in the previous passage.

Stop here and, as a group, look at a map that includes all 21 towns in this passage and the city of Jerusalem. Find Nebo, Magbish, the other Elam, and Harim on the map. Pause this audio here.

Lod, Hadid, and Ono are towns northwest of Jerusalem. Jericho and Senaah are both towns northeast of Jerusalem. The largest number of Israelites return to Senaah. Some people think that Senaah is not a place, but a group of all the poor people who return to the land of Judah.

Stop here and, as a group, look at a map that includes all 21 towns in this passage and the city of Jerusalem. Find Lod, Hadid, Ono, Jericho, and Senaah on the map. Pause this audio here.

We know that later in the book of Jeremiah, many of the people whose ancestors are from these places help to rebuild the walls of Jerusalem.

Defining the Scenes
-------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 2:21–35 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will define the scenes, characters, and setting of this passage. Then the group will visualize this passage.

This passage has seven scenes.

**In the first scene:** The Israelites return whose ancestors lived in the towns of Bethlehem and Netophah. These towns are south of Jerusalem.

**In the second scene:** The Israelites return whose ancestors lived in Anathoth and Azmaveth. These towns are north of Jerusalem.

**In the third scene:** The Israelites return whose ancestors lived in Kiriath Jearim, Kephirah, and Beeroth. These three towns are northwest of Jerusalem.

**In the fourth scene:** The Israelites return whose ancestors lived in Ramah, Geba, Micmash, Bethel, and Ai. These towns are all to the far north of Jerusalem.

**In the fifth scene:** The Israelites return whose ancestors lived in Nebo, Magbish, the other Elam, and Harim. These towns are southwest of Jerusalem.

**In the sixth scene:** The Israelites return whose ancestors lived in Lod, Hadid, and Ono. These towns are northwest of Jerusalem.

**In the seventh scene:** The Israelites return whose ancestors lived in Jericho and Senaah. These towns are northeast of Jerusalem.

The characters in this passage are:

* Israelites who return whose ancestors lived in Bethlehem and Netophah
* Israelites who return whose ancestors lived in Kiriath Jearim, Kephirah, and Beeroth
* Israelites who return whose ancestors lived in Ramah, Geba, Micmash, Bethel, and Ai
* Israelites who return whose ancestors lived in Nebo, Magbish, the other Elam, and Harim
* Israelites who return whose ancestors lived in Lod, Hadid, and Ono
* And Israelites who return whose ancestors lived in Jericho and Senaah

As a group, pay attention to these parts of the passage's setting:

It might be helpful to start this passage by reminding people that this is a continuation of the list of exiles who return from Babylon to Jerusalem in Judah. Also, this is a part of the list with the numbers of the men whose ancestors had lived in these towns before the exile to Babylon.

In the first scene, the Israelites return whose ancestors came from the towns of Bethlehem and Netophah, which are both towns south of Jerusalem. The storyteller says there are 123 men whose ancestors had lived in Bethlehem and 56 men whose ancestors had lived in Netophah. Bethlehem is about 10 kilometers south of the city of Jerusalem, and Netophah is near Bethlehem. Other ways to say this could be, "these men were from the town of Bethlehem," or "these are men whose families originally came from Bethlehem." Remember that "men of" and "sons of" have the same meaning.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How will you translate the number of men whose ancestors lived in the towns of Bethlehem and Netophah? Pause this audio here.

Remember that the towns in the first scene are in the land that used to be the land of the tribe of Judah. Also, remember that the storyteller tells the towns in a specific order based on the location of the towns, and that the storyteller groups certain towns together, probably because these towns are close to each other.

Stop here and look at a map that includes all 21 towns in this passage, and the city of Jerusalem again as a group, if needed. Find Bethlehem and Netophah on the map. Pause this audio here.

Also, stop here and discuss this question as a group: Would it be helpful to include the general location of the groups of towns in this scene as they relate to the city of Jerusalem? For example, maybe you would translate the first scene as, "The towns of Bethlehem and Netophah are south of Jerusalem in land that used to be in the land of the tribe of Judah. There are 123 men whose families originally came from Bethlehem, and there are 56 men whose families originally came from Netophah."

Or you can choose not to include the additional information about the locations of the towns, and that the land used to be in the land of the tribe of Judah. If so, you might translate this scene as, "There are 123 men whose families originally came from Bethlehem, and there are 56 men whose families originally came from Netophah." Pause this audio here.

The remaining scenes in this passage all have towns that are in the land that used to belong to the tribe of Benjamin.

In the second scene, the Israelites return whose ancestors came from the towns of Anathoth and Azmaveth. Remember, these towns are both north of Jerusalem, in the land that used to belong to the tribe of Benjamin. Anathoth is about 5 kilometers north of Jerusalem, and Azmaveth is about 8 kilometers north of Jerusalem.

Stop here and look at a map that includes all 21 towns in this passage, and the city of Jerusalem again as a group, if needed. Find Anathoth and Azmaveth on the map. Pause this audio here.

In the third scene, the Israelites return whose ancestors came from the towns of Kiriath Jearim, Kephirah, and Beeroth. These three towns are northwest of Jerusalem. In the original language, the storyteller says the name "Kiriath Arim," but most scholars believe this is the same town that people call Kiriath Jearim in other passages. Kiriath Jearim is about 13 kilometers northwest of Jerusalem. Kephirah is north of Kiriath Jearim, and Beeroth is about 19 kilometers north of Jerusalem.

Stop here and look at a map that includes all 21 towns in this passage, and the city of Jerusalem again as a group, if needed. Find Kiriath Jearim, Kephirah, and Beeroth on the map. Pause this audio here.

In the fourth scene, the Israelites return whose ancestors came from the towns of Ramah, Geba, Micmash, Bethel, and Ai. These towns are all north of Jerusalem. In the original language, Bethel means "house of God." Bethel is a town on the border. Ramah is about 8 kilometers north of Jerusalem, and Geba is east of Ramah. Micmash is about 13 kilometers northeast of Jerusalem, and Bethel is 10 kilometers north of Jerusalem. Ai is 2 kilometers east of Bethel.

Stop here and look at a map that includes all 21 towns in this passage, and the city of Jerusalem again as a group, if needed. Find Ramah, Geba, Micmash, Bethel, and Ai on the map. Pause this audio here.

In the fifth scene, the Israelites return whose ancestors were from the towns of Nebo, Magbish, the other Elam, and Harim. These towns are southwest of Jerusalem. Nebo of Judah is not the same as the town of Nebo of Moab, which is to the east of Jerusalem in the land that used to belong to the tribes of Reuben and Gad. People do not know exactly where Nebo of Judah is. Magbish is probably southwest of a town called Adullam. Elam was a well\-known town in the place that is modern\-day Iran, but this Elam is a different Elam, near Jerusalem. People are not certain where Harim is, but it is near the other towns in this scene.

Stop here and look at a map that includes all 21 towns in this passage, and the city of Jerusalem again as a group, if needed. Find Nebo, Magbish, the other Elam, and Harim on the map. Pause this audio here.

In the sixth scene, the Israelites return whose ancestors were from the towns of Lod, Hadid, and Ono. These towns are northwest of Jerusalem near the coast of the Mediterranean Sea. In the New Testament, the storytellers call Lod "Lydda." Lod is about 18 kilometers southeast of Joppa. Hadid is a town in the valley of Aijalon. Ono is about 8 kilometers north of Lod, and nearly 48 kilometers northwest of Jerusalem.

Stop here and look at a map that includes all 21 towns in this passage, and the city of Jerusalem again as a group, if needed. Find Lod, Hadid, and Ono on the map. Pause this audio here.

In the seventh scene, the Israelites return whose ancestors were from the towns of Jericho and Senaah. Jericho and Senaah are northeast of Jerusalem. Jericho is a town about 16 kilometers northwest of the Dead Sea. People do not know where the town of Senaah is, but some people think that Senaah might be about 8 kilometers north of Jericho.

Stop here and look at a map that includes all 21 towns in this passage, and the city of Jerusalem again as a group, if needed. Find Jericho and Senaah on the map. Pause this audio here.

Some people think that Senaah sounds like "the sons of the person people hated" in the original language. These people think that the large group of people whose ancestors were from the town of Senaah might actually be a group of poor people, because they are "the sons of the person people hated." It would be best to translate this scene with Senaah as the name of a town because we are not sure that it means a group of poor people returning to the land of Judah from exile in Babylon.

Now, the group should storyboard, draw, or use objects to visualize the passage and the action in it.

Embodying the Text
------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 2:21–35 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will dramatize the passage.

This passage has seven scenes.

The characters in this passage are:

* Israelites who return whose ancestors lived in Bethlehem and Netophah
* Israelites who return whose ancestors lived in Kiriath Jearim, Kephirah, and Beeroth
* Israelites who return whose ancestors lived in Ramah, Geba, Micmash, Bethel, and Ai
* Israelites who return whose ancestors lived in Nebo, Magbish, the other Elam, and Harim
* Israelites who return whose ancestors lived in Lod, Hadid, and Ono
* And Israelites who return whose ancestors lived in Jericho and Senaah

As a group, act out the passage twice. You should act out the passage in your own language.

First, act out the passage without stopping. Pay attention to the dialogue, flow, plotline, and chronology of the passage. Make sure you do not skip acting out the difficult or important parts of the passage. Help each other remember every part of the passage.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage.

The group should act out this passage a second time. At certain points in the drama, pause the drama and ask the person playing the character, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person playing the character should answer what they would be feeling or thinking if they were that character. Then continue the drama.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage a second time.

The following is an example of the drama and possible responses.

This is a continuation of the list of exiles who return from Babylon to Jerusalem in Judah. This is a part of the list with the numbers of the men whose ancestors had lived in these towns before the exile to Babylon.

Pause the drama. Ask the people playing all of the Israelites who return to the land of Judah, whose ancestors lived in each of the towns in this passage, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The people might answer things like:

* We are honored to see the names of the towns of our ancestors in this list of exiles who return from Babylon to the land of Judah; or
* We are very happy that we can finally return to the land of our ancestors. We have never been here before, but we have heard a lot about the land of Judah, and we are happy to be here; or
* We are a little nervous about how we will live in the land of Judah now. In the land of Babylon, we had homes and work, and now we have to start over completely here in the land of our ancestors.

> Continue the drama.

The towns of Bethlehem and Netophah are south of Jerusalem in land that used to belong to the land of the tribe of Judah. There are 123 men whose families originally came from Bethlehem, and there are 56 men whose families originally came from Netophah.

The towns of Anathoth and Azmaveth are north of Jerusalem, in land that used to belong to the tribe of Benjamin. There are 128 men whose families originally came from Anathoth, and 42 men whose families originally came from Azmaveth.

The towns of Kiriath Jearim, Kephirah, and Beeroth are northwest of Jerusalem, in land that used to belong to the tribe of Benjamin. There are 743 men whose families originally came from the towns of Kiriath Jearim, Kephirah, and Beeroth.

The towns of Ramah, Geba, Micmash, Bethel, and Ai are all north of Jerusalem, in land that used to be the land of the tribe of Benjamin. There are 621 men whose families originally came from the towns of Ramah and Geba. There are 122 men whose families originally came from the town of Micmash. And there are 223 men whose families originally came from the towns of Bethel and Ai.

The towns of Nebo, Magbish, the other Elam, and Harim are southwest of Jerusalem, in land that used to belong to the tribe of Benjamin. There are 52 men whose families originally came from the town of Nebo, and there are 156 men whose families originally came from the town of Magbish. There are 1,254 men whose families originally came from the town of Elam, and there are 320 men whose families originally came from the town of Harim.

The towns of Lod, Hadid, and Ono are northwest of Jerusalem, in land that used to belong to the tribe of Benjamin. There are 725 men whose families originally came from the towns of Lod, Hadid, and Ono.

The towns of Jericho and Senaah are northeast of Jerusalem, in land that used to be in the land of the tribe of Benjamin. There are 3,630 men whose families originally came from the town of Senaah.

Filling the Gaps
----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 2:21–35 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will discuss key terms and words in this passage.

The storyteller gives the names of 21 **towns** in this passage, and many of these towns are well\-known from other parts of the Old Testament. In your translation, it may be good to say that these are towns so that people know what these names refer to. If you include town in your translation, remember that a town can be any size of a settlement. You should use the same general word for town that you used in the previous list.

There are 123 men whose ancestors are from the town of **Bethlehem**. These 123 men return to the land of Judah from the exile in Babylon. The name Bethlehem means "house of bread." King David was born in Bethlehem, and Jesus would also be born in Bethlehem. Translate Bethlehem the same as you have in other parts of the Old Testament. For more information on Bethlehem, refer to the Master Glossary.

Speaking the Word
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of the passage in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this session, retell the passage, as a group, in your own language.

First, the whole group should practice telling the passage in your own language. Remember to include the things you decided in previous steps, like chronology, words, phrases, emotions, or anything else you discussed.

One group member should tell a few sentences of the passage and pause. Another group member should tell the next part of the passage and pause. Do this until the group has retold the whole passage. The group members can correct each other in this step.

Pause this audio here.

Next, each member of the group should tell the whole passage without interruption at least one time. A group member can use random objects, motions, or their storyboard to help them remember the passage.

Pause this audio here.

Next, the group can decide whose version of the passage you like the most. You may decide that you liked the way one group member told one scene but you liked the way a different group member told another scene.

Pause this audio here.

Finally, the group can put together a final version of the passage that everyone agrees on. Each group member should practice telling this final version of the passage.

* **Associated Passages:** Ezra 2:21-35
* **Associated Articles:** Bethlehem (ID: `eng-t13-v1@FIAKeyTerms`)

* **Associated ACAI Concepts:** Harim (ID: `person:Harim.2`); Beth-azmaveth (ID: `place:Beth-azmaveth`); Hadid (ID: `place:Hadid`); Netophah (ID: `place:Netophah`); Senaah (ID: `place:Senaah`); Magbish (ID: `place:Magbish`); Nebo (ID: `place:Nebo.2`); Geba (ID: `place:Geba`); Ramah (ID: `place:Ramah.2`); Bethel (ID: `place:Bethel`); Bethlehem (ID: `place:Bethlehem`); Michmash (ID: `place:Michmash`); Elam (ID: `person:Elam`); Chephirah (ID: `place:Chephirah`); Beeroth (ID: `place:Beeroth`); Ono (ID: `place:Ono`); Jericho (ID: `place:Jericho`); Ai (ID: `place:Ai`); Lod (ID: `place:Lod`); Anathoth (ID: `place:Anathoth`); Kiriath-jearim (ID: `place:Kiriath-jearim`)

## Ezra 3:1–7 (id: eng-ezr-p3-v1)

Hear and Heart
--------------

In this step, hear Ezra 3:1–7 and put it in your hearts.

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 3:1–7 three times, in three different translations, if possible.

Pause this audio here.

Now, as a group, discuss the following questions:

1. What do you like in this passage? Pause this audio here.
2. What do you not like or not understand in this passage? Pause this audio here.
3. What does this passage tell us about God? Pause this audio here.
4. What does this passage tell us about people? Pause this audio here.
5. How does this passage affect our daily lives? Pause this audio here.
6. Who do you know who needs to hear this passage?

Setting the Stage
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 3:1–7 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

King Cyrus was king of Persia at the time of this story, and Cyrus gave the Israelites permission to travel back to the land of Israel. In the previous passage, the storyteller provided a list of families who traveled back to Israel. This story is about how the Israelites obey Yahweh's laws again. The story shows us how the Israelites have returned to Israel and to praising Yahweh the way Yahweh commanded.

The Israelites finished moving into their towns in the seventh month of the year. The Israelites often prepared to gather their crops during the seventh month of the year, which is September or October in our calendar. The seventh month is important to the Israelites because Yahweh commanded the Israelites to celebrate many special events in this month. Yahweh commanded the Israelites to celebrate many of these special events because each one reminded the Israelites of all the ways Yahweh had taken care of them.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How does your culture keep track of the days and months? What are some important times of year in your culture? Pause this audio here.

After the Israelites finished moving, the entire group of Israelites met in Jerusalem because they all wanted to start rebuilding Yahweh's temple. At that time, a man named Jeshua was the leader of the priests, and a man named Zerubbabel was a leader of the Israelites. Jeshua and Zerubbabel were the leaders of the construction project, and they started by building Yahweh's altar. Israelites used the altar to give important gifts to Yahweh. Because Yahweh's gifts were very important, Jeshua and Zerubbabel started building the altar first so they could give Yahweh gifts before the Israelites finished the temple. The Israelites probably built the altar using stones and made a flat surface on top that they would use to burn animals as a gift to Yahweh. The Israelites would give these gifts to Yahweh every morning and every night. On special days, the Israelites would give more gifts to Yahweh in the way Yahweh commanded the people.

Stop here and look at a map of Israel and find Jerusalem as a group. Notice where the Israelites would have needed to travel to build the temple. Look at a picture of an altar as a group and talk about similar objects that people in your culture may use in religious or cultural ceremonies. Pause this audio here.

Several groups of local people were living in the area when the Israelites moved back into Israel. Some of these groups of people were the Ammonites, Samaritans, Moabites, and Edomites, and they had fought and disagreed with the Israelites for hundreds of years. You will remember that the Samaritans were the Israelites who were from the Northern Kingdom of Israel. The Samaritans had not followed Yahweh, and Yahweh had allowed foreign countries to conquer them. These people did not follow Yahweh the way Yahweh instructed and were unfriendly to the Israelites when they tried to build the altar. The storyteller does not explain why these local people were not friendly to the Israelites, but it could be because they disagreed with the Israelites on how and where the Israelites built the altar. The most important thing to know is that the Israelites were afraid because of the local people, but the Israelites chose to build the altar anyway.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: When was a time you were afraid of something but chose to act anyway? What happened as a result? Pause this audio here.

After the Israelites finished building the altar, the Israelites started celebrating the special religious events Yahweh commanded the Israelites to celebrate during the seventh month of the year. One of these events is called the Festival of Tabernacles. This celebration reminded the Israelites about how Yahweh had saved them when they were slaves in Egypt many centuries before this story. During the Festival of Booths, Israelite families would make tents out of tree branches and live in the tents for eight days. While the Israelites lived in tents for eight days, the priests would make a special burnt offering to Yahweh on each day of the celebration.

Stop here and look at a picture of a tent that the Israelites would make for the Festival of Booths celebration. Pause this audio here.

The Israelites also started celebrating the Festival of the New Moon again. The Israelites celebrated this festival at the beginning of every month when they saw the new moon rise in the sky. The Israelites would celebrate by blowing trumpets and making burnt offerings to thank Yahweh for the new month.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: Tell a story of a time when you celebrated a new beginning. Pause this audio here.

When the Israelites started celebrating the festivals again, the Israelites also started making the usual burnt sacrifices to Yahweh as Yahweh had commanded in the law. The Israelites also made sacrifices to Yahweh that Yahweh did not require. The Israelites made these "freewill" sacrifices just because they were very thankful to Yahweh for taking care of the Israelites. It is important to understand why the Israelites were giving Yahweh sacrifices because it shows that the Israelites were focusing on worshipping Yahweh even though they had not built the temple yet.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: What kind of events does your culture view as important to remember? What does your culture do to remember these events? Pause this audio here.

The Israelites now have an altar, but the Israelites still do not have a temple. To build the temple, the Israelites gave money to the "masons," or people who cut stones, and to the "carpenters," or people who made things from wood for building. The Israelites also gave oil, wine, and food to the Sidonians and Tyrians. The Sidonians and Tyrians were people from a region next to Israel. In exchange for the supplies and money, the Sidonians and Tyrians gave the Israelites cedar trees. You will remember that the Israelites used the cedar trees from the Sidonians and Tyrians to build Yahweh's first temple many centuries before this story. To send the cedar trees to the Israelites, the Sidonians and Tyrians tied the logs together and floated the logs on the sea to Joppa. It is important to know that Joppa is a city in Israel that is close to the sea. Then the Israelites would bring the logs a long distance over the land to Jerusalem. The Israelites could do all this work to rebuild the temple because King Cyrus gave them special permission.

Stop here and look at a picture of a cedar tree. Note the size and shape of the logs. Also, look at a map of the trading route the Israelites used to get what they needed to build the temple.

Defining the Scenes
-------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 3:1–7 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will define the scenes, characters, and setting of this passage. Then the group will visualize this passage.

This passage has three scenes.

**In the first scene:** The Israelites finish moving into their towns and decide to meet in Jerusalem. Then Jeshua and Zerubbabel help the Israelites start building the altar. The Israelites are afraid of the local people but continue to work on the altar anyway.

**In the second scene:** The Israelites finish building the altar, and the Israelites start making sacrifices to Yahweh. The Israelites also start celebrating festivals like the Festival of Booths and the Festival of the New Moon again.

**In the third scene:** The Israelites want to start building the temple. The Israelites give money to stone and wood workers to build the temple. The Israelites also give supplies to the Sidonians and Tyrians in exchange for special cedar trees.

The characters in this passage are:

* The Israelites
* The local people
* Jeshua, the lead priest
* Zerubbabel, a leader of the Israelites
* Yahweh
* Wood workers
* Stone workers
* Sidonians
* And Tyrians

As a group, pay attention to these parts of the passage's setting:

In scene one, the Israelites finish moving into their towns and decide to meet in Jerusalem. The storyteller says that the Israelites all gathered as one. This means that because all the Israelites agreed that it was important to meet together to build the temple, it was like one person had made the decision. The storyteller uses this phrase to explain that the Israelites felt very unified in their decision. This is important because it shows that all of the Israelites thought it was important to start worshipping Yahweh again.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How do you talk about people gathering in your language? Pause the audio here.

The Israelites meet in Jerusalem in the seventh month of the year. The storyteller uses these words to give background to the story and to explain that something new is starting.

Stop here and tell a story to the group that includes a time of year when an event begins. Pause the audio here.

The Israelite's decision to meet in Jerusalem is the first thing the Israelites do when they have settled in Israel. When the Israelites arrive in Jerusalem, Jeshua the priest and Zerubbabel the leader take charge of the construction of the altar.

The Israelites were scared of the people around them when they built the altar. Some people say that the reason the Israelites built the altar to Yahweh is that the Israelites were scared of the local people. Some other people say that the Israelites were already scared of the local people and built the altar despite their fear. We do not know which opinion is correct, but we know for sure that the Israelites were scared of the local people when the Israelites built the altar.

Stop here and discuss your choice as a group. Why do you think the Israelites were afraid? Pause this audio here.

In scene two, after the Israelites build the altar, the Israelites start to make sacrifices and worship Yahweh. The order of this scene can be hard to follow. The Israelites build the altar first and then start to offer daily morning and evening sacrifices to Yahweh on the first day of the seventh month. Although the storyteller first talks about the most important festival in the seventh month, the Festival of Tabernacles, this festival does not happen until later in the month. Instead, the Israelites probably celebrate the Festival of the New Moon first at the beginning of the month and begin offering all the regular, daily burnt offerings that Yahweh required in his law. The Israelites celebrate the Festival of Tabernacles later in the month after the Festival of the New Moon.

Stop here and tell a story to the group. Pay attention to how you tell the story. How do you explain to the audience what order the events happened in? Pause this audio here.

Throughout the seventh month, the Israelites also celebrate other festivals the way Yahweh had commanded. Some of the Israelites even choose to bring some optional sacrifices for Yahweh simply because they are thankful. The storyteller is very clear that the Israelites begin to celebrate all the festivals and special events the same way Yahweh said to do in the law he gave to Moses, and that Moses wrote down. In other words, the Israelites do all these things to obey what "was written" in Scripture. This means that the Israelites obey Yahweh and follow Yahweh's commands well.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How do you explain that something was done the right way in your culture? Pause this audio here.

In scene three, the Israelites have been making sacrifices to Yahweh, but still have not built the base of the temple. When the Israelites built a building, the Israelites had to put down a strong base before they built the walls and roof. The Israelites may have built this base from large, strong stones. This kept the building strong and helped it to last longer.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: What words do you use to describe the base of a building? What words do you use to describe the parts of a building that make it stand strong? Pause this audio here.

So, the Israelites decide to build the temple. The Israelites need people to make the temple, so they give money to stonecutters and woodworkers. Masons, or stonecutters, are people who work with stone to make buildings. Carpenters, or woodworkers, are people who work with wood for buildings or furnishings. At the same time, the Israelites give food, wine, and oil to the Sidonians and Tyrians in exchange for the cedar trees that the Israelites use to make the temple.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: What do you call people who work with stone and wood in your culture? Pause this audio here.

Now, the group should storyboard, draw, or use objects to visualize the passage and the action in it.

Embodying the Text
------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 3:1–7 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will dramatize the passage.

This passage has three scenes.

The characters in this passage are:

* The Israelites
* The local people
* Jeshua, the lead priest
* Zerubbabel, a leader of the Israelites
* Yahweh
* Wood workers
* Stone workers
* Sidonians
* And Tyrians

As a group, act out the passage twice. You should act out the passage in your own language.

First, act out the passage without stopping. Pay attention to the dialogue, flow, plotline, and chronology of the passage. Make sure you do not skip acting out the difficult or important parts of the passage. Help each other remember every part of the passage.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage.

The group should act out this passage a second time. At certain points in the drama, pause the drama and ask the person playing the character, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person playing the character should answer what they would be feeling or thinking if they were that character. Then continue the drama.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage a second time.

The following is an example of the drama and possible responses.

In scene one, the people playing the Israelites finish moving into their homes and travel to Jerusalem because they want to build the temple. The people playing Jeshua and Zerubbabel lead the Israelites as they build the altar, even though the Israelites are scared of the local people.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing the Israelites, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* I am excited to be worshiping Yahweh again! or
* I am scared that the local people around us will kill us.

Ask the person playing the local people, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* The Israelites told us we could not help them build the temple, and now we are angry that the Israelites are building the altar without us; or
* The Israelites took away our land. Now, the Israelites are building an altar, which must mean the Israelites will stay here. That makes us angry!

Ask the people playing Jeshua and Zerubbabel, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The people might answer things like:

* We must worship Yahweh again, as Yahweh commanded us. We must help the Israelites follow Yahweh!

Continue the drama.

In scene two, the people playing the Israelites start making daily sacrifices to Yahweh and celebrating the Festival of Tabernacles and the Festival of the New Moon. Some of the people playing the Israelites can also choose to bring their own sacrifice to the altar to show their thankfulness to Yahweh.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing Yahweh, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* I love my people so much, and it makes me so happy that they are obeying me again.

Ask the person playing the Israelites, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* I am so thankful that Yahweh has brought us back into Israel! or
* Following Yahweh is a lot of work! or
* I am so happy that I can worship Yahweh again the way he commanded us to; or
* I hope that Yahweh saves us from the anger of the people around us.

Continue the drama.

In scene three, the people playing the Israelites give money and supplies to the woodworkers, stonecutters, and the Sidonians and Tyrians to build the temple. The people playing the Sidonians and Tyrians then float cedar trees on the sea so that the Israelites can use the trees for the temple.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing the Israelites, "What are you feeling and thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* The first temple to Yahweh was much more beautiful. I am so sad that we cannot give Yahweh more; or
* I am so happy that we can finally give Yahweh a temple again!

Filling the Gaps
----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 3:1–7 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will discuss key terms and words in this passage.

The storyteller mentions that in the seventh month of the year, the people of **Israel** lived in their towns. Israel is the name of the territory where the Israelites lived. Israel also refers to one of Abraham's sons, who was named Israel. For more information on Israel, refer to the Master Glossary. Use the same word or phrase for Israel as you used in previous passages.

The Israelites moved into **towns** in Israel after King Cyrus of Persia gave them permission to leave. Use the same word or phrase for towns as you used in previous passages.

The Israelites decided to meet in **Jerusalem** to rebuild Yahweh's temple. Jerusalem is one of the most important cities in Israel. For more information on Jerusalem, refer to the Master Glossary. Use the same word or phrase for Jerusalem as you used in previous passages.

Jeshua and Zerubbabel built the altar to Yahweh with the help of their relatives, associates, fellow priests, or brothers. The people were other priests and maybe some regular Israelites who worked with Jeshua and Zerubbabel to build the altar. Some of these people were probably relatives, or part of Jeshua and Zerubbabel's brothers and close family, and some may have just been other Israelites who wanted to help.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **priests**. If you have already translated this word in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

The Israelites built an altar to Yahweh in Jerusalem. An altar is a place where people make many kinds of offerings to Yahweh. The Israelites would often make altars with stones and with a flat surface on top.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **altar**. Look up altar in the Master Glossary for more information. If you have already translated this word in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

The Israelites built an altar to the **God** of Israel. In the Bible, God refers to God who is powerful and created the whole world. It is the same God who made promises to Abraham. God is also the same God that the Israelites have worshipped for many centuries. For more information on God, refer to the Master Glossary. Use the same word or phrase for God as you used in previous passages.

A burnt offering is a specific form of sacrifice. A priest burned the plants or animals that people meant to give to Yahweh. Yahweh commanded the Israelites to give burnt offerings in special ways in the Old Testament.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **burnt offering**. Look up burnt offering in the Master Glossary for more information. If you have already translated this word in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

The Israelites built the altar and made sacrifices on it according to the Law of Moses. The Law of Moses is the law that Yahweh gave to Moses that explains how the Israelites should live. The law tells the Israelites how to worship Yahweh and how to treat the people around them. "According to the Law of Moses" in this story means that the Israelites gave Yahweh sacrifices in the way Yahweh commanded.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **law**. Look up law in the Master Glossary for more information. If you have already translated this word in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

The Israelites made offerings and sacrifices to **Yahweh**. Yahweh is a name for God that God gave Moses many centuries before this story. The name Yahweh reminds the Israelites of God's promises to the Israelites. This name reminds everyone that God never changes and always keeps his promises. For more information on Yahweh, refer to the Master Glossary. Use the same word or phrase for Yahweh as you used in previous passages.

The Israelites celebrated the "Festival of Tabernacles" or "Festival of Booths." It was a special event that the Israelites celebrated to remember when Yahweh saved them from slavery in Egypt many centuries before this story. During the Festival of Tabernacles, the Israelites set up tents and lived in them for eight days. The Israelites celebrated this event to remember when their ancestors had walked in the desert for many years before moving into Israel.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for the F**estival of Tabernacles**. Look up "Festival of Tabernacles" in the Master Glossary for more information. If you have already translated this word in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

The Israelites also celebrated the "New Moon festival." In Old Testament times, the people of Israel celebrated this festival at the beginning of each month, when the people could see the new moon rise in the sky. During this festival, the Israelites stopped their work, offered special sacrifices, and blew trumpets to celebrate.

Stop here and discuss as a group how you will translate the **New Moon Festival**. Refer to "Festival of New Moon" in the Master Glossary. Pause this audio here.

The Israelites made sacrifices to Yahweh and celebrated all the **appointed festivals of Yahweh**. The appointed festivals of Yahweh are the festivals that Yahweh commanded the Israelites to celebrate.

The Israelites chose to give Yahweh "freewill offerings" when they started celebrating Yahweh's festivals again. A freewill offering is another specific form of sacrifice. A freewill offering is a sacrifice that the Israelites would bring to Yahweh when they were thankful for Yahweh. It is important to know that Yahweh does not require the Israelites to make freewill offerings.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **freewill offering**. Look up freewill offering in the Master Glossary for more information. If you have already translated this word in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

The Israelites gave sacrifices to Yahweh even though they had not built the foundation of the temple yet. A foundation is the base of a building. People build a foundation first to make a building stable and strong for a long time.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **foundation**. If you have already translated this word in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

The Israelites met in Jerusalem because they wanted to build Yahweh's **temple**. The temple is a place in Jerusalem that the Israelites built so that they could worship Yahweh as he commanded. The "house of God" is another name for the temple. King Solomon first built the temple many centuries before this story. After King Cyrus gave the Israelites permission to move into Israel again, the Israelites wanted to rebuild the temple so that they could start worshiping Yahweh again. For more information on the temple, refer to the Master Glossary. Use the same word or phrase for temple as you used in previous passages.

After the Israelites built the altar, they gave money to masons and carpenters to build Yahweh's temple. Masons and carpenters are people who have special skills to work with different kinds of supplies. Masons cut and shape stones and rocks, and carpenters cut and shape wood. Masons and carpenters both use their skills to make buildings and objects.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **masons** and **carpenters**. Discuss what you call people who build with stone and wood in your culture. If you have already translated this word in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

The Israelites gave wine, oil, and food to the Sidonians and Tyrians in exchange for cedar trees to build the temple. Wine is a drink that people make from fermented grape juice. Grapes are small, soft, and juicy berries that grow in several different colors.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **wine**. Look up wine in the Master Glossary for more information. If you have already translated this word in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

The storyteller mentions that **King** Cyrus of Persia gave special permission for the Israelites to get cedar trees from the Sidonians and Tyrians. A king is a special person who rules over a city or area. For more information on king, refer to the Master Glossary. Use the same word or phrase for king as you used in previous passages.

Speaking the Word
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of the passage in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this session, retell the passage, as a group, in your own language.

First, the whole group should practice telling the passage in your own language. Remember to include the things you decided in previous steps, like chronology, words, phrases, emotions, or anything else you discussed.

One group member should tell a few sentences of the passage and pause. Another group member should tell the next part of the passage and pause. Do this until the group has retold the whole passage. The group members can correct each other in this step.

Pause this audio here.

Next, each member of the group should tell the whole passage without interruption at least one time. A group member can use random objects, motions, or their storyboard to help them remember the passage.

Pause this audio here.

Next, the group can decide whose version of the passage you like the most. You may decide that you liked the way one group member told one scene but you liked the way a different group member told another scene.

Pause this audio here.

Finally, the group can put together a final version of the passage that everyone agrees on. Each group member should practice telling this final version of the passage.

* **Associated Passages:** Ezra 3:1-7

## Ezra 3:8–13 (id: eng-ezr-p4-v1)

Hear and Heart
--------------

In this step, hear Ezra 3:8–13 and put it in your hearts.

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 3:8–13 three times, in three different translations, if possible.

Pause this audio here.

Now, as a group, discuss the following questions:

1. What do you like in this passage? Pause this audio here.
2. What do you not like or not understand in this passage? Pause this audio here.
3. What does this passage tell us about God? Pause this audio here.
4. What does this passage tell us about people? Pause this audio here.
5. How does this passage affect our daily lives? Pause this audio here.
6. Who do you know who needs to hear this passage?

Setting the Stage
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 3:8–13 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In the last story, Jeshua and Zerubbabel helped the Israelites build Yahweh's altar. It is important to remember that the Israelites wanted to start worshipping Yahweh again in the way that Yahweh had asked them to. The Israelites started following Yahweh's commandments in the law of Moses and making sacrifices to Yahweh on the altar. It is also important to remember that the Israelites gave money and supplies to many workers to gather cedar trees and to start to build the temple. This story is about how the Israelites continue to obey Yahweh and begin to build Yahweh's temple.

This story takes place about eight months after the Israelites built the altar, so this is during the second year that the Israelites were again in Jerusalem. This story happens in the second month of the second year. It is important to know that this means that the Israelites did not start to work on the temple right after they finished building the altar. This story also takes place in the second month of the year. This is also important because it is the best time of the year for the Israelites to start building. For the Israelites, the second month of the year happened after the spring rains made the ground wet, so the Israelites would be able to build the temple on dry ground. The Israelites also built Yahweh's first temple during the second month of the year.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How does your culture decide when to start a building project? Pause this audio here.

In the second month of the year, Jeshua and Zerubbabel helped the Israelites start to build the temple, just as they had helped the Israelites build the altar. Jeshua and Zerubbabel used the help of Jeshua and his family, Kadmiel and his family, and the sons of Henadad and their families. All these people were from the Israelite tribe of Levi.

Stop here and tell a story to the group of a time when your family or a group of people in your community worked together on a project or activity. Pause this audio here.

Jeshua and Zerubbabel gathered all the Levites who were 20 years old or older to oversee the workers building the temple. It is important to know that in the Israelites' culture, an Israelite could work as a Levite or soldier when he was 20 years old. These Levites watched the workers so that they could make sure that the workers were building the temple the way Yahweh commanded.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How do people in your culture decide when the community considers someone to be an adult with all the same rights as other adults? When can people in your community start working important jobs? Pause this audio here.

When the workers started or were working on the base or foundation of the temple, the priests celebrated! The priests put on the special clothes they wore when they served Yahweh and blew into trumpets to worship Yahweh with music.

Stop here and look at a picture of the special clothes the priests wore. Discuss this question as a group: What special clothes do people wear for important events in your culture? Why? Pause this audio here.

For the celebration, the priests played trumpets and a special group of Levites played cymbals to praise Yahweh. A trumpet was a short metal tube that a priest would blow into to make a high sound. Cymbals were flat metal circles that the Levites would hit together to make a loud sound. The priests and the Levites played music and sang songs to Yahweh to lead the people to praise and thank Yahweh for all he had done.

Stop here and look at a picture of a trumpet and cymbals. Also, discuss this question as a group: How do people in your culture celebrate when something good happens? What kinds of instruments do people in your culture play at big events? Pause this audio here.

The storyteller explains that a special family of priests played the instruments at the Israelites' celebration. A priest named Asaph lived when King David ruled over the Israelites, many centuries before this story. During his rule, King David made Asaph and Asaph's family in charge of playing the music and leading the songs that people used to worship Yahweh at the temple. In this story, the special Levites who sang at the temple were all part of Asaph's family. This is important to know because it shows that the Israelites are trying to worship Yahweh the same way they used to many years ago.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: The Israelites had special people to lead them when they worshipped Yahweh with music. How do people in your culture lead music at important events? Pause this audio here.

The Israelites sang a song to worship Yahweh because Yahweh did good things for the Israelites and promised to love Israel forever with a steadfast love. When the Israelites finished singing, the Israelites shouted loudly. The Israelites yelled because they were very thankful to Yahweh for all he had done, and because the Israelites were so happy that they could worship Yahweh in a temple again.

Stop here and tell a story of a time in your life when you were so happy you could shout or do something to show your happiness. What did that moment feel like to you? Pause this audio here.

Many of the Israelites were very happy to have the temple again. But some of the older priests and Israelites were very sad. The older Israelites were sad when they remembered Yahweh's first temple, which the Israelites had built many centuries before this story. The older Israelites were so sad that no one could tell who was shouting because they were happy and who was yelling because they were sad. The storyteller even explains that when the Israelites yelled, the noise was so loud that people could hear it from a long distance away.

Stop here and tell a story to the group about a time when you were sad during a celebration.

Defining the Scenes
-------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 3:8–13 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will define the scenes, characters, and setting of this passage. Then the group will visualize this passage.

This passage has three scenes.

**In the first scene:** Jeshua and Zerubbabel help the Israelites build the temple's base. Many of the priests and their families lead the workers to help make sure everything is done correctly.

**In the second scene:** The Israelites finish construction on the temple's base, and the priests and Levites lead the Israelites to set up a party to worship and celebrate all that Yahweh did for them. The priests and Levites use instruments and sing songs.

**In the third scene:** Some Israelites see the new temple and are very sad. Some Israelites see the temple and are very happy! All the Israelites let out a loud yell that people can hear from a great distance away.

The characters in this passage are:

* Zerubbabel
* Jeshua
* Jeshua's brothers and family
* Kadmiel
* Kadmiel's family
* Henadad's family
* All the other Levites
* Yahweh
* Asaph's family of Levites
* The Israelites
* The head of the Israelite families
* The builders who laid the temple's foundation
* And the older Israelites who were sad

As a group, pay attention to these parts of the passage's setting:

In scene one, the storyteller mentions that the story happens in the second year after the Israelites arrived in Jerusalem, during the second month. This is several months after the Israelites had built the altar. Remember that the second month of the year is important because it is after the spring rains. It is the best time of year for building.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: In your culture, how would you describe the second month of the year, during the second year that the Israelites are there in Jerusalem? Pause this audio here.

In the second month of the year, Zerubbel and Jeshua gathered their brothers, families, and all of the priests and prepared to help the Israelites finally build Yahweh's temple. The storyteller mentions that Jeshua and Zerubbabel only gathered the priests, Levites, and other Israelites who had moved back into Israel. You will remember that some Israelites did not go to Babylon with the rest of the Israelites and were still living in Israel before this story. There is tension between the two groups of Israelites in this story—those who stayed and those who came back.

Stop here and discuss as a group how you will include the extra information that the Israelites who returned only gathered the people who had returned. Pause the audio here.

Jeshua and Zerubbabel only gathered people from their families and from the other Levite families who were 20 years old or older. Jeshua and Zerubbabel gathered these Levites so that the Levites could supervise the workers as the workers built the temple. This way, the Levites could make sure that the workers were building the temple the way Yahweh commanded.

Stop here and remember the story you told the group about a time you and your family or a group of people from your community worked together on a project. Discuss this question as a group: How did the person who told the story describe what happened? What kinds of words did he or she use to talk about working as a family? Pause this audio here.

Jeshua and Zerubbabel led the Israelites to build the temple's foundation first. A building's foundation is the base of the building. The Israelites often used rocks or stones to build the foundations of their important buildings. This foundation helps keep the building strong and keeps it from shifting or sinking over time. The foundation is a very important part of the building process, especially at the time of this story.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How do people in your culture make sure that buildings are stable and strong? Pause this audio here.

In scene two, the priests and Levites led the Israelites in a celebration to thank Yahweh. The storyteller does not clearly explain when in the story this celebration happens. Some people think the Israelites celebrated after they finished the base or foundation of the temple. Other people think the Israelites celebrated while the workers were building the temple's base. We do not know which of these opinions is correct. It is important to know that the celebration happened close to the time that the workers built the temple's base. It is also important to know that the celebration was because the people wanted to worship Yahweh and thank Yahweh for helping them.

Stop here and discuss your choice as a group. When do you think the celebration took place? Pause this audio here.

For the celebration, the priests played trumpets, and the special group of Levites who were part of Asaph's family played cymbals. Both the priests and Levites sang songs that worshiped and thanked Yahweh. The songs praised Yahweh because the things Yahweh does are good and Yahweh promised to always love Israel. The priests and Levites led this celebration the same way King David told them to many centuries before this story.

Stop here and look at the pictures of trumpets and cymbals again. How would you describe instruments like this in your culture? Pause this audio here.

In scene three, the Israelites then let out a loud shout of praise to Yahweh. Some people say the Israelites' loud shout was like a shout of victory in battle. The Israelites shouted because they were worshipping Yahweh with all their hearts.

Many of the Israelites were very happy and thankful for the new temple. Some of the older Israelites were very sad when they saw the new temple's base. The storyteller does not explain why these people were sad. Most people say that the older Israelites were sad because they remembered what Yahweh's first temple looked like. Yahweh's first temple was really big and fancy, and this new temple was probably not that fancy. The older Israelites might be sad that they were not able to give Yahweh as nice a temple this time. Some other people say that the older Israelites were sad because they were sorry that Israel's enemies destroyed the first temple because of Israel's sin. We do not know for sure why the older Israelites are sad, but it is important to know that the older Israelites who had seen Yahweh's first temple were sad.

The Israelites' shout of sadness and thankfulness was really loud. The shout was so loud that people in the crowd could not tell who was shouting because they were sad and who was shouting because they were happy. The shout was also so loud that people could hear it from a long distance away.

Now, the group should storyboard, draw, or use objects to visualize the passage and the action in it.

Embodying the Text
------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 3:8–13 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will dramatize the passage.

This passage has three scenes.

The characters in this passage are:

* Zerubbabel
* Jeshua
* Jeshua's brothers and family
* Kadmiel
* Kadmiel's family
* Henadad's family
* All the other Levites
* Yahweh
* Asaph's family of Levites
* The Israelites
* The head of the Israelite families
* The builders who laid the temple's foundations
* And the older Israelites who were sad

As a group, act out the passage twice. You should act out the passage in your own language.

First, act out the passage without stopping. Pay attention to the dialogue, flow, plotline, and chronology of the passage. Make sure you do not skip acting out the difficult or important parts of the passage. Help each other remember every part of the passage.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage.

The group should act out this passage a second time. At certain points in the drama, pause the drama and ask the person playing the character, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person playing the character should answer what they would be feeling or thinking if they were that character. Then continue the drama.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage a second time.

The following is an example of the drama and possible responses.

The Israelites finished building the altar a few months before this story. The Israelites are preparing to start building the temple.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing Jeshua and Zerubbabel, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* Yahweh's temple is so important. I am so nervous about doing it correctly! or
* We have not been able to worship Yahweh correctly for so long. I am so excited to be able to worship Yahweh in the temple again!

> Continue the drama.

Next, Jeshua and Zerubbabel gather all the other priests, Levites, and all the other people of Israel who had returned to Israel. Jeshua and Zerubbabel prepared to start the work. Jeshua and Zerubbabel appointed the Levites who were 20 years old or older to help lead the workers.

Pause the drama. Ask the people playing all the Levite families, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The people might answer things like:

* I feel so important since I am able to help make sure Yahweh's temple is built correctly; or
* I am scared I am going to make a mistake and make Yahweh upset with me; or
* I am very excited to help build the temple so we can worship Yahweh in it sooner!

> Continue the drama.

The workers work on the base of the temple, and the priests and Levites have a celebration. The priests and Levites play trumpets and cymbals and sing songs of praise to Yahweh.

Pause the drama. Ask the people playing the priests and Levites who lead the celebration, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The people might answer things like:

* I am so thankful that Yahweh led us back to Israel, protected us from our enemies, and has helped us to start building the temple; or
* I finally get to praise Yahweh the correct way again! or
* I hope Yahweh is happy with his new temple.

Ask the person playing Yahweh, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* It makes me so happy to see my people wanting to follow me correctly again; or
* I love my people so much. It makes me excited to see my people wanting to praise me.

Continue the drama.

Then, all the Israelites shouted very loudly because they wanted to praise Yahweh so much. Some of the older Israelites who remembered Yahweh's first temple were very sad.

Pause the drama. Ask the people playing the older Israelites, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The people might answer things like:

* I remember Yahweh's first temple, which was so big, beautiful, and fancy. I am so sad that we cannot give Yahweh something better than this temple; or
* Our enemies destroyed Yahweh's first beautiful temple because of our sins. I am so sad that our people were the reason our enemies destroyed Yahweh's first temple.

Continue the drama.

The Israelites shouted happy and sad shouts so loudly that people far away could hear the Israelites!

Pause the drama. Ask the people playing the Israelites, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The people might answer things like:

* I am so happy! We can finally worship and praise Yahweh in our own land again, the way our family did for many centuries before us. This event is so special! or
* I want to be as loud as I can because I am so thankful to Yahweh for helping us to start building the temple again! I want everyone around us to know how happy I am.

Filling the Gaps
----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 3:8–13 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will discuss key terms and words in this passage.

In this story, the Israelites met at the **house of God**. The house of God is the same thing as the "house of Yahweh" that the storyteller mentions later in the story. Remember that the house of God is a way to talk about the place on earth where Yahweh lived among his people. It is important to remember that the house of God can mean the temple, but can also mean other places. Use the same word or phrase for the house of God as you used in previous passages. For more information on the house of God, refer to the Master Glossary.

Jeshua and Zerubbabel started working on the temple with all their "**brothers**" or relatives. Remember that these people are close family, like Jeshua and Zerubbabel's brothers and sisters or children. Brothers can also sometimes mean cousins and other relatives. Use the same word or phrase for brothers or relatives as you used in previous passages.

Jeshua and Zerubbabel also worked on the temple with the Israelite **priests** and Levites. The priests were a special group of Levites whom Yahweh chose to serve Yahweh in the temple. The priests made all of the sacrifices to Yahweh and performed many other special duties that served Yahweh. Use the same word or phrase for priests as you used in previous passages. For more information on priests, refer to the Master Glossary.

When Jeshua and Zerubbabel started working on the temple, they chose **Levites** who were 20 years old or older to help them. Remember, Levites are the group of Israelites whom Yahweh chose to serve Yahweh in the temple. Use the same word or phrase for Levites as you used in previous passages. For more information on Levites, refer to the Master Glossary.

Jeshua and Zerubbabel chose Levites to help supervise the workers who were building the house of **Yahweh**. Remember, Yahweh is a name God gives himself. The name Yahweh reminds people of God's promises to the Israelites. Use the same word or phrase for Yahweh as you used in previous passages. For more information on Yahweh, refer to the Master Glossary.

The storyteller lists some of the important Levite families that helped work on the temple. The storyteller mentions Kadmiel and the **sons of Judah**, which can also be translated as the "sons of Hodaviah." No matter which way people translate the phrase, the storyteller uses this phrase to remind us of the names of the important Levite families he already described in the book of Ezra. Use the same word or phrase for the "sons of Judah" or "sons of Hodaviah" as you used in previous passages.

The people working on the temple laid the foundation of the **temple**. We do not know exactly what this means, but it probably means that the workers started rebuilding the base of the temple building on which it could stand strong. It could also mean that the workers fixed the part of Yahweh's first temple that was still standing. Use the same word or phrase for temple as you used in previous passages. For more information on temple, refer to the Master Glossary.

Stop here and discuss as a group how you will talk about the Israelites **laying a foundation** or **building a foundation** or base for the temple. Pause this audio here.

When the workers build the base of the temple, a special group of priests holds a celebration. These priests wear special clothes during the celebration. These special clothes were clothes that the priests often wore for important events. Yahweh commanded the priests to wear these clothes in the law of Moses.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **special clothes**. If you have already translated this word in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

The storyteller describes the celebration the priests had when the workers built the base of the temple. In this celebration, the priests play trumpets to praise Yahweh. This means that the Israelites play trumpets to show Yahweh how grateful and thankful they are for all Yahweh has done for the people. Trumpets are long metal tubes. People blow into one side of a trumpet, and it makes music.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **trumpets**. If you have already translated trumpet in another book of the Bible, use the same words that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

The Levites also played cymbals at the celebration. Cymbals are flat metal discs. People hit cymbals together to make a loud, crashing sound. People use cymbals to make music.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **cymbals**. If you have already translated this word in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

The priests and Levites used trumpets and cymbals to praise Yahweh. Praise is when you speak out loud and say all the good things Yahweh has done. Praise is a way to give thanks and honor to Yahweh.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **praise**. Look up praise in the Master Glossary for more information. If you have already translated this word in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

The priests played music and sang songs to Yahweh as **King** David had told them to do many years before this story. Remember, a king is someone who rules over a group of people. Use the same word or phrase for king as you used in other passages. For more information on king, refer to the Master Glossary.

The storyteller describes King David as the king of **Israel**. Remember, Israel is the land where the Israelites lived. When the storyteller says that King David was king of Israel, the storyteller means that King David ruled over the land of Israel and the Israelites. Use the same word for Israel as you used in previous passages. For more information on Israel, refer to the Master Glossary.

During the priests' celebration event, the Israelites sang songs that praised Yahweh and thanked Yahweh for all he had done. One of those songs praised Yahweh for his steadfast love. Some people also translate steadfast love as "faithful love," "kindness," or "lovingkindness." Yahweh is a faithful God. Yahweh always keeps his promises! Steadfast love is a way to describe Yahweh's faithfulness. It reminds the Israelites of Yahweh's loyalty and that Yahweh will always keep the promises Yahweh made to the Israelites.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **steadfast love**. Look up steadfast love in the Master Glossary for more information. If you have already translated this word in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

When the Israelites sing about Yahweh's steadfast love, they sing that Yahweh's love endures forever. This means that Yahweh's steadfast love will never end. Yahweh will always be faithful, loyal, and merciful to his people forever!

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **endures forever**.

Speaking the Word
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of the passage in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this session, retell the passage, as a group, in your own language.

First, the whole group should practice telling the passage in your own language. Remember to include the things you decided in previous steps, like chronology, words, phrases, emotions, or anything else you discussed.

One group member should tell a few sentences of the passage and pause. Another group member should tell the next part of the passage and pause. Do this until the group has retold the whole passage. The group members can correct each other in this step.

Pause this audio here.

Next, each member of the group should tell the whole passage without interruption at least one time. A group member can use random objects, motions, or their storyboard to help them remember the passage.

Pause this audio here.

Next, the group can decide whose version of the passage you like the most. You may decide that you liked the way one group member told one scene but you liked the way a different group member told another scene.

Pause this audio here.

Finally, the group can put together a final version of the passage that everyone agrees on. Each group member should practice telling this final version of the passage.

* **Associated Passages:** Ezra 3:8-13
* **Associated Articles:** Priest's Clothing (ID: `a1667@FIAImages`); Priest's Clothing (ID: `a1668@FIAImages`); Priest's Clothing (ID: `a1669@FIAImages`); Priests Blowing Trumpets (ID: `a1831@FIAImages`); Israel (ID: `eng-t74-v2@FIAKeyTerms`); priest (ID: `eng-t103-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); temple (ID: `eng-t138-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); Yahweh (ID: `eng-t174-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); house of God (ID: `eng-t183-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); praise (ID: `eng-t219-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); king (ID: `eng-t226-v2@FIAKeyTerms`); Levite (ID: `eng-t244-v1@FIAKeyTerms`)
