* **Associated ACAI Concepts:** Tribe of Levi (ID: `group:Levi`); LORD (ID: `deity:Lord`); Levi (ID: `person:Levi.3`); House (ID: `realia:House`); Israel (ID: `person:Israel`); Hodaviah (ID: `person:Hodaviah.4`); Henadad (ID: `person:Henadad`); Jeshua (ID: `person:Jeshua.4`); Kadmiel (ID: `person:Kadmiel`); David (ID: `person:David`); Jehozadak (ID: `person:Jehozadak`); Kindness (ID: `keyterm:Kindness`); Jerusalem (ID: `place:Jerusalem`); Zerubbabel (ID: `person:Zerubbabel.2`); Joshua (ID: `person:Joshua.5`); Shealtiel (ID: `person:Shealtiel.2`); Hodiah (ID: `person:Hodiah.2`); Asaph (ID: `person:Asaph.2`); Trumpet (ID: `realia:Trumpet`); Cymbals (ID: `realia:Cymbals`)

## Ezra 4:1–6 (id: eng-ezr-p5-v1)

Hear and Heart
--------------

In this step, hear Ezra 4:1–6 and put it in your hearts.

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 4:1–6 three times, in three different translations, if possible.

Pause this audio here.

Now, as a group, discuss the following questions:

1. What do you like in this passage? Pause this audio here.
2. What do you not like or not understand in this passage? Pause this audio here.
3. What does this passage tell us about God? Pause this audio here.
4. What does this passage tell us about people? Pause this audio here.
5. How does this passage affect our daily lives? Pause this audio here.
6. Who do you know who needs to hear this passage?

Setting the Stage
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 4:1–6 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

Cyrus, the king who ruled the kingdom of Persia, had allowed the exiles from the tribes of Judah and Benjamin to return to their hometown, called Jerusalem, to build the temple of Yahweh, the God of Israel. In the previous passage, Jeshua, son of Jozadak, and Zerubbabel, son of Shealtiel, and their helpers had rebuilt the altar of Yahweh and the foundation of the temple.

The storyteller continues the previous story and explains how the neighboring people, whom he calls the enemies of Judah and Benjamin, responded to the efforts of the exiles to rebuild the temple of Yahweh. The people of the land, or enemies of Judah and Benjamin, may be people whom the Assyrian king defeated and sent to live in the neighboring land of Samaria.

By this time, these enemies of Judah and Benjamin had lived in Samaria for several decades. Many of these people were likely born in this land. To these people, the exiles are "foreigners" who have come to take away these people's land and influence.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How well do people in your culture get along with foreigners? Talk about a time when someone came back to your area to claim something that they believed was theirs. Pause this audio here.

It is entirely possible that the enemies of Judah and Benjamin heard the great shouts of joy and the trumpets that the priests blew when they celebrated that they had finished the foundation of the temple. Or it is possible that the enemies heard a report from someone about how the exiles came back to the land to rebuild.

Stop here and talk about a time when people in your community reacted when they heard loud celebrations or heard news that something big had happened. How did people respond? Pause this audio here.

The enemies came to Zerubbabel and the leaders of the families of Israel and asked to help build the temple. The enemies claimed to worship Israel's God as well and to have made sacrifices to Yahweh since King Esarhaddon of Assyria brought them to that land. We know that King Esarhaddon ruled from 681 to 669 B.C. King Esarhaddon forced these foreign communities to settle in Samaria after Esarhaddon conquered Samaria, which was the former northern Kingdom of Israel. An Israelite priest probably went to teach the people who settled there the law of the god of the land. However, the people ended up mixing religions when they worshiped Yahweh but also served their carved images or idols. Thus, the enemy people's statement that they worship Israel's God as Israel does may have been partially true.

Stop here and discuss together as a group how people in your community mix worship of God with other gods or ancestral practices. Pause this audio here.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: Talk about a time when someone offered to help you but really had other motives. Pause this audio here.

The exiled leaders tell their enemies that the enemies cannot help the exiles rebuild the temple. The exiles refer to the political decree or order that King Cyrus gave, that the exiles were the only ones the king permitted to build the temple. The exiles clearly called Yahweh the exiles' God and so seem to indicate that the exiles want to keep the temple building pure. The people of the land were upset by this response from the exiles and decided to try to keep the exiles from building the temple by making the exiles afraid. The exiles could do very little to protect themselves. Enemies surrounded the small group of exiled people in Jerusalem. The exiled people could not defend themselves.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: Talk about a time when someone tried to make you afraid. How did you feel about this? Pause this audio here

The people of the land hired or bribed "counselors" or officers to make it difficult for the exiles to continue to build the temple. To bribe someone is to pay that person to do something they should not do. This continues from the reign of King Cyrus of Persia into the reign of King Darius of Persia. This is a period of about 20 years. During the reign of King Darius, the Jews began rebuilding the temple again.

Stop here and discuss together as a group: Share a story about a time when you or someone else gave a bribe, or when someone tried to bribe you or another person. What do people in your culture think about people who try to bribe others? Pause this audio here.

After King Darius of Persia ruled his kingdom, King Xerxes ruled his kingdom. The enemies of Judah wrote a letter to King Xerxes of Persia to accuse the people of Jerusalem and Judah. It is not clear what the enemies of Judah accused the people of Judah and Jerusalem of doing. King Xerxes' first year of reign started in 485 B.C. King Xerxes was the son of King Darius. At the time of King Xerxes' first year of rule, there was a rebellion in Egypt, so it is likely that King Xerxes did nothing about the letter of accusation that King Xerxes received.

Stop here and discuss this together as a group: Talk about a time when you or someone else accused another person of doing something wrong, but the leader or person in charge did nothing about it. How did you or someone else feel about what happened?

Defining the Scenes
-------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 4:1–6 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will define the scenes, characters, and setting of this passage. Then the group will visualize this passage.

This passage has four scenes.

**In the first scene:** The enemies of Judah heard that the exiles were rebuilding the temple of Yahweh. The enemies of Judah came to where the exiles from Judah were building and asked Zerubbabel and the leaders of the families if the enemies of Judah could help build the temple alongside the people of Judah. The enemies of Judah said that they wanted to help because they also worship the Israelites' God. The enemies of Judah said that they have sacrificed to the God of Israel since King Esarhaddon brought them into the land.

**In the second scene:** Zerubbabel, Jeshua, and the leaders of the families of Judah, or Israel, refused the help of the enemies of Judah. The leaders of Israel told the enemies of Judah that the enemies of Judah had no place helping the Israelites. The Israelites told the enemies of Judah that King Cyrus' decree said that the Israelites would be the ones to rebuild the temple of their God.

**In the third scene:** The enemies of Judah try to make the people of Judah afraid and stop the plans to rebuild the temple. The enemies of Judah bribe the Persian officers to make it difficult for Judah to rebuild the temple. This happens through the rest of King Cyrus' reign into the reign of King Darius.

**In the fourth scene:** Many years later, during the reign of King Xerxes, the enemies of Judah wrote a letter to the king to complain. The enemies accused the people of Judah of doing something wrong.

The characters in this passage are:

* The enemies of Judah and Benjamin
* The exiled Israelites from the Israelite tribes of Judah and Benjamin
* Zerubbabel
* Jeshua
* Leaders of the Israelite families
* Yahweh
* King Esarhaddon
* Officers or counselors
* King Cyrus
* King Darius
* And King Xerxes

As a group, pay attention to these parts of the passage's setting:

This story takes place in Jerusalem after the exiled Israelites, or the "children of the exiles," return to rebuild the temple of Yahweh. The exiles had already built the foundation of the temple. The exiles are continuing the work on the temple. This story starts when a neighboring people group, who are enemies of the people of Judah and Benjamin, hears that the exiles are working to rebuild the temple.

Stop here and discuss as a group how you will say "children of the exiles." Think about how you would talk about people who were taken away in the past and their children who returned.

Pause this audio here.

When the enemies of the people of Judah and Benjamin hear that the exiles are rebuilding the temple, they approach Zerubbabel and the leaders of Israel. The storyteller directly quotes the enemies to show clearly and strongly what they said. The enemies of Judah and Benjamin say, "We will build with you because like you we try to follow your God. We have sacrificed to your God ever since King Esarhaddon of Assyria brought us here."

Then Zerubbabel and Jeshua and the leaders of the Israelite families respond strongly and directly: "You have no part with us in building a temple to our God. We alone will build it for Yahweh, the God of Israel, as King Cyrus, the king of Persia, commanded us." You will notice that here the storyteller calls the leaders of the exiles the leaders of the Israelite families. The people from the tribes of Judah and Benjamin were also Israelites. By this time, they were the only tribes of Israel who still followed Yahweh. Because of this, storytellers often call the people of Judah and Benjamin the true Israelites. You will also notice that the storyteller uses the title "king" twice for Cyrus. The exiles want to be clear to their enemies that the Israelites are following the command of the King of Persia, who is King Cyrus.

Stop here and discuss as a group how you will translate the title of Cyrus. The title "King" shows someone with great authority. This strong title means that the Israelites were obeying the most powerful ruler of their time. How can you translate this title in your language? Pause this audio here.

After the Israelite rulers reject the request of their enemies, the enemies try to discourage the exiles or cause the exiles to lose their courage so that the exiles will not continue to build the temple. The word discourage means "to weaken the hands." So the enemies are trying to weaken the exiles' hands from working.

Stop here and discuss as a group how you will translate to "discourage" or "weaken the hands." Pause this audio here.

The enemies of Judah also try to frighten the exiles, though the passage does not say how they try to frighten the people. The enemies also "bribed" or paid officers to do things they were not supposed to do to keep the exiles from building the temple. The enemies of Judah discouraged and frightened the Israelites all the days of King Cyrus until the days of King Darius.

Then, many years later, when King Xerxes started to rule his kingdom, the enemies of Judah sent King Xerxes a letter to accuse the exiles who were living in Judah and Jerusalem. Some translations use the Hebrew name for King Xerxes, which is Ahasuerus.

Now, the group should storyboard, draw, or use objects to visualize the passage and the action in it.

Embodying the Text
------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 4:1–6 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will dramatize the passage.

This passage has four scenes.

The characters in this passage are:

* The enemies of Judah and Benjamin
* The exiled Israelites from Judah and Benjamin
* Zerubbabel
* Jeshua
* Leaders of the Israelite families
* Yahweh
* King Esarhaddon
* Officers or counselors
* King Cyrus
* King Darius
* And King Xerxes

As a group, act out the passage twice. You should act out the passage in your own language.

First, act out the passage without stopping. Pay attention to the dialogue, flow, plotline, and chronology of the passage. Make sure you do not skip acting out the difficult or important parts of the passage. Help each other remember every part of the passage.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage.

The group should act out this passage a second time. At certain points in the drama, pause the drama and ask the person playing the character, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person playing the character should answer what they would be feeling or thinking if they were that character. Then continue the drama.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage a second time.

The following is an example of the drama and possible responses.

The enemies of Judah heard that exiles were rebuilding the temple of Yahweh.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing the enemies of Judah, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* Who do these people think they are? Why do these people get to rebuild a temple in our land? or
* I wonder what these people are doing here? I feel like these people should not build without talking to us.

> Continue the drama.

The enemies of Judah came to where the exiles were building and asked Zerubbabel and the leaders of the families if the enemies of Judah could help build the temple alongside the exiles. The enemies of Judah said that they wanted to help because they also worshiped the exiles' God. The enemies of Judah said that they had sacrificed to the God of Israel since King Esarhaddon brought them into the land.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing the enemies of Judah, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* Since this is where we live, these people should at least let us help them out; or
* We learned about these people's God. We should be able to worship this God as well; or
* I hope these people let us help. Then we can keep an eye on these people and maybe overthrow them.

> Continue the drama.

Zerubbabel, Jeshua, and the leaders of the families of Israel refused the help of the enemies of Judah. The leaders of Israel told the enemies of Judah that the enemies of Judah had no place helping the Israelites. The Israelites told the enemies of Judah that King Cyrus' decree said that the Israelites would be the ones to rebuild the temple of their God.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing the exiles, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* Do they really think we will let them help? We are here to build a house for the one true God! or
* I am suspicious about what these people really want from us. I do not think they actually want to help.

Continue the drama.

The enemies of Judah try to make the people of Judah afraid and disrupt the plans to rebuild the temple. The enemies of Judah bribe the Persian officers to make it difficult for Judah to rebuild the temple. This happens through the rest of King Cyrus' rule into the rule of King Darius.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing the exiles, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* This is so frustrating. I do not understand why God does not help us out; or
* This is really going slowly now since we have so much opposition. But we will get it done; or
* How long, God, will we have to endure these enemies?

> Continue the drama.

Many years later, during the reign of King Xerxes, the enemies of Judah wrote a letter to the king to make accusations against the people of Judah.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing the enemies of Judah, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* I feel angry. I do not want these people to grow strong again; or
* I want the king to stop these people. I feel worried that the people of Judah will rise and become powerful.

Ask the person playing the exiles, "When you learned that your enemies sent a letter against you, what were you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* I feel confused. Why do these people hate us so much? or
* I feel afraid. What will the king do to us? or
* I feel sad. We only want to rebuild God's house.

Filling the Gaps
----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 4:1–6 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will discuss key terms and words in this passage.

The enemies of **Judah** and **Benjamin** heard that the people of Judah and Benjamin who returned from **exile** were building the **temple** for **Yahweh**, the **God** of **Israel**. Judah and Benjamin were two of the tribes in the southern kingdom. Israelites are the people of God who were descendants of Abraham and, more specifically, descendants of Jacob. Exile is when a conquering nation does not allow the people to live in their home country. The temple for Yahweh was the place where the Israelites worshipped and made sacrifices to Yahweh. The God of Israel is the God who created everything, whom Israel worships. See the Master Glossary for more information on Israel, Judah, temple, God, and Yahweh. Translate exile and the children or people of the exile in the same way you have in previous passages.

The enemies of the exiles came to where Judah was building and asked **Zerubbabel**, one of the leaders of Judah, and the leaders of the families if the enemies of Judah could help build the temple alongside the Israelites. The enemies of Judah said that they wanted to help because they also "**seek**," or try to ask for help from, the exiles' God. These enemies said that they also give honor to Yahweh, the God of Israel. The enemies of Judah said that they had **sacrificed** to the God of Israel since **King** Esarhaddon brought them into the land. To sacrifice is to give to God something of great importance or value. People usually slaughtered an animal when they made a sacrifice. A king is the ruler of an area. See the Master Glossary for more information on sacrifice and king.

Zerubbabel, Jeshua, and the **leaders of the families** of Israel refused to allow the enemies of Judah to help build the temple. The leaders of Israel told the enemies of Judah that the enemies of Judah had no place helping the Israelites. The Israelites told the enemies of Judah that King Cyrus commanded that the Israelites would be the ones to build the temple for Yahweh, the God of the Israelites. Translate "leaders" or "heads" of families the same way you have in previous passages.

The enemies of Judah try to make the people of Judah afraid and stop the plans to rebuild the temple. The enemies of Judah **bribe** the **officers** to make it difficult for Judah to rebuild the temple. This happens through the rest of King Cyrus' **kingdom**, or reign, and into the kingdom, or reign, of King Darius. An officer is an advisor to the King of Persia. Use the same words for government officer and for kingdom as you have previously, and refer to kingdom in the Master Glossary for more information. To bribe someone means to pay that person money so that the person will do something you want, even though that action is illegal.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **bribe**. If you have already translated this word in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

Many years later, at the beginning of the reign of King Xerxes, the enemies of Judah wrote a letter to the king to make accusations against the people of Judah.

Speaking the Word
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of the passage in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this session, retell the passage, as a group, in your own language.

First, the whole group should practice telling the passage in your own language. Remember to include the things you decided in previous steps, like chronology, words, phrases, emotions, or anything else you discussed.

One group member should tell a few sentences of the passage and pause. Another group member should tell the next part of the passage and pause. Do this until the group has retold the whole passage. The group members can correct each other in this step.

Pause this audio here.

Next, each member of the group should tell the whole passage without interruption at least one time. A group member can use random objects, motions, or their storyboard to help them remember the passage.

Pause this audio here.

Next, the group can decide whose version of the passage you like the most. You may decide that you liked the way one group member told one scene but you liked the way a different group member told another scene.

Pause this audio here.

Finally, the group can put together a final version of the passage that everyone agrees on. Each group member should practice telling this final version of the passage.

* **Associated Passages:** Ezra 4:1-6
* **Associated Articles:** Israel (ID: `eng-t74-v2@FIAKeyTerms`); Judea (ID: `eng-t81-v2@FIAKeyTerms`); sacrifice (ID: `eng-t114-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); temple (ID: `eng-t138-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); kingdom (ID: `eng-t157-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); Yahweh (ID: `eng-t174-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); God (ID: `eng-t189-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); king (ID: `eng-t226-v2@FIAKeyTerms`)

* **Associated ACAI Concepts:** Judah (ID: `person:Judah.4`); LORD (ID: `deity:Lord`); Tribe of Judah (ID: `group:Judah`); Israel (ID: `person:Israel`); Zerubbabel (ID: `person:Zerubbabel.2`); Cyrus (ID: `person:Cyrus`); Persia (ID: `place:Persia`); Relatives (ID: `group:Relatives`); Esarhaddon (ID: `person:Esarhaddon`); Ahasuerus (ID: `person:Ahasuerus`); Assyria (ID: `place:Assyria`); Darius (ID: `person:Darius.2`); Jerusalem (ID: `place:Jerusalem`); Counsel (ID: `keyterm:Counsel`); Joshua (ID: `person:Joshua.5`); Benjamin (ID: `person:Benjamin`); Tribe of Benjamin (ID: `group:Benjamin`); House (ID: `realia:House`)

## Ezra 4:7–16 (id: eng-ezr-p6-v1)

Hear and Heart
--------------

In this step, hear Ezra 4:7–16 and put it in your hearts.

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 4:7–16 three times, in three different translations, if possible.

Pause this audio here.

Now, as a group, discuss the following questions:

1. What do you like in this passage? Pause this audio here.
2. What do you not like or not understand in this passage? Pause this audio here.
3. What does this passage tell us about God? Pause this audio here.
4. What does this passage tell us about people? Pause this audio here.
5. How does this passage affect our daily lives? Pause this audio here.
6. Who do you know who needs to hear this passage?

Setting the Stage
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 4:7–16 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

The people of Judah and Benjamin returned from exile to Jerusalem to rebuild the city. The people who were living in the land did not want the exiles to rebuild Jerusalem. We know that the people began working on the temple again during the reign of King Darius. The people finished the temple during Darius' reign. It is likely that the people continued to rebuild Jerusalem during the reign of King Xerxes, who reigned after King Darius. But now King Artaxerxes reigns, and we will see that the enemies of the exiles will again oppose the exiles. Artaxerxes ruled in Persia from 465 to 424 B.C. At that time, Bishlam, Mithredath, Tabeel, and their fellow workers wrote a letter to Artaxerxes to complain about the Israelites. The storyteller does not say anything else about these men and their helpers. These men wrote the letter in Aramaic. Aramaic was the official language of the Persian Empire. Some translations mention that the people translated the letter, but it is unclear into what language people translated the letter.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How do people in your culture talk to or get the attention of the leaders or government officials to complain? Share a story of when you had to make a complaint to an official. Pause this audio here.

Rehum, the commander, and Shimshai, the scribe, also wrote a letter to Artaxerxes against Jerusalem. A commander is a high\-ranking official who reports to the king. The scribe was also a high official who wrote official letters or translated official documents. The rest of this passage is a copy of this letter.

The writers of the letter identify themselves as Rehum and Shimshai, along with the rest of their fellow workers. Rehum and Shimshai are writing the letter on behalf of all of the people that they mention as their fellow workers. Rehum and Shimshai list these people by their positions and the cities they come from.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: What effect does it have in your community when a message comes from many groups instead of just one? Why do people do this? Pause this audio here.

The people whom Rehum and Shimshai listed by origin were the people whom Osnappar, also called Ashurbanipal, king of Assyria, deported and settled in Samaria and in the area beyond the river. Some translations call this area the "Trans\-Euphrates." This area includes everything west of the Euphrates River to the Mediterranean Sea.

Stop here and, if needed, look at a map of the Trans\-Euphrates region. Pause this audio here.

These people would have objected to the Persian leaders giving special treatment to the exiles.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: Have you seen some people become jealous or upset when leaders give one group special treatment? What happened? Pause this audio here.

Rehum and Shimshai were attempting to pressure King Artaxerxes when they said that they were speaking for the whole area of people and that they feared for the safety of Persia.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How would someone in your culture try to pressure a ruler to do what they wanted the ruler to do? Do you have examples of a time when someone tried to pressure a leader to do something? What actions or words did that person use? Pause this audio here.

Then the letter identifies who the letter is to and who sent the letter. The writers of the letter show respect to the king when they call themselves the king's servants. The writers also identify themselves by their location in a province they call "beyond the River."

The writers of the letter warn the king of the issues that will come if the king allows the Jews who came from Babylon to finish building Jerusalem. You may remember that people called the exiles from the Israelite tribes of Judah and Benjamin "Jews." The writers refer to Jerusalem as an evil and rebellious city. The writers of the letter insist that if the Jews build Jerusalem, then the Jews will no longer pay their taxes to the Persian king, and the king will have less income. The Persian Empire thought taxation was very important. The Persian Empire believed that if people stopped paying taxes, those people were rebelling.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: In your culture, what happens when people do not pay their taxes or dues to the leaders? Pause this audio here.

The writers of the letter say that they share or eat the salt of the palace in order to show that the writers are loyal to the king and that the king pays the writers to work for the king. The writers state that they do not want the king to be put to shame, and so the writers suggest a solution. When the Persians replaced the Assyrian and Babylonian empires, they got their records. These records were in the form of clay tablets, or leather or papyrus scrolls, in the royal archives. These records would report that the rulers in Jerusalem rebelled against the Assyrians and Babylonians, especially when the rulers fought against the Babylonian rulers who destroyed Jerusalem in 586 B.C.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How have people in your culture kept records of the past? Tell a story that your tribe, culture, or people group has passed down. Pause this audio here.

The writers of the letter state their final conclusion: that if the Jews rebuild the city of Jerusalem, then the king of Persia will no longer have control over the land beyond the River because the Jews will take over this province.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: Why do people become afraid when they think they will lose control, power, or land? How have you seen this in your community?

Defining the Scenes
-------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 4:7–16 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will define the scenes, characters, and setting of this passage. Then the group will visualize this passage.

This passage has three scenes.

**In the first scene:** Bishlam, Mithredath, Tabeel, and the enemies of Judah complain to King Artaxerxes about the Jews rebuilding Jerusalem. Bishlam, Mithredath, Tabeel, and those with him wrote the letter in Aramaic, the language people used for official letters in the Persian Empire. The king's servant translated the letter into another language.

**In the second scene:** Rehum, a commanding officer, and Shimshai, the secretary, also wrote a letter to King Artaxerxes against Jerusalem. The beginning of this letter gives the names of the people who are sending the letter. This gives the idea of who the letter is from and who the letter represents.

**In the third scene:** Rehum's and Shimshai's letter to King Artaxerxes warns the king about the Jews rebuilding Jerusalem and the damage it will do to King Artaxerxes' kingdom.

The characters in this passage are:

* Artaxerxes, king of Persia
* Bishlam, Mithredath, Tabeel, and their fellow workers
* Rehum, the commanding officer
* Shimshai, the secretary
* Judges
* Officials
* Administrators
* Ashurbanipal, or Osnappar, king of Assyria
* And the Jews who are rebuilding Jerusalem

As a group, pay attention to these parts of the passage's setting:

This story takes place in the days of King Artaxerxes, the king of Persia. People complained against the exiles who worked to rebuild Jerusalem in the time of King Darius, and people continued to complain in the days of King Artaxerxes.

In the first scene, we do not know what Bishlam, Mithredath, and Tabeel wrote to King Artaxerxes. Because the first scene is within the context of several complaints people sent to the kings of Persia, we assume that this letter was also a complaint about the exiles. Bishlam, Mithredath, and Tabeel wrote this letter in Aramaic, which was the official language of the Persian Empire.

In the second scene, Rehum, the commander, and Shimshai, the secretary or scribe, wrote a letter against the Jews to King Artaxerxes as well. This is a separate letter, and here we have the text of the letter. The storyteller tells us that he is beginning to directly quote the letter.

The letter starts with who wrote the letter and who the letter is for. Some translations include this as the beginning of the letter. Someone would write the names of the people who sent the letter on the outside of the paper, along with a brief explanation of what the letter was about. Some translations show that the list of names of the people who wrote the letter is not an actual part of the letter.

Rehum's title is commanding officer. This is a high\-ranking non\-military official who could give orders and reports to the king. Shimshai was a scribe, or a person who wrote official documents. The rest of Rehum and Shimshai's associates, or people who were connected to them, were all people of high importance in the government. This was the government over the people who "Osnappar," or Ashurbanipal, settled in Samaria, or "beyond the River" in the Trans\-Euphrates. These people kept their ethnic identity even when Osnappar deported or forcefully moved them out of their own land and settled in the area. Osnappar, or Ashurbanipal, was the Assyrian king who deported these groups of people. Here, the letter writer refers to Osnappar as "great and honorable" or noble. These words describe a very powerful ruler.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How do you refer to a person who writes important letters for leaders? Pause this audio here.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How do you say "the people connected to them" in your language? How would you refer to people who work together as a group? Pause this audio here.

Stop here and discuss as a group how you will describe this list of people that the letter represents. Will this be a part of the letter or just a separate list? Do people in your culture write the sender's information on a letter? Pause this audio here.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How do you say that a ruler is very powerful and respected? How does your language show honor without adding too much praise? Pause this audio here.

The third scene is the text of the letter to King Artaxerxes. The letter tells the king that the Jews who came from Babylon are rebuilding Jerusalem. Jerusalem is a wicked and rebellious city. The writers tell the king that if the Jews rebuild the city, the Jews will no longer pay taxes, tributes, and duties to the king. Taxes are payments to the ruler. Tributes are gifts or payments to show respect to a powerful king. Duties are fees that people pay at the city gates or trading places. These are three separate kinds of payments to the king's treasury, but you could group them all together as "taxes." The letter states that if the Jews do not pay their taxes, then the kingdom itself will suffer from the financial loss.

Stop here and discuss as a group: What are the different types of taxes or payments that people in your culture pay to the leaders or government? How will you talk about these taxes in your translation? Pause this audio here.

Stop here and discuss as a group: How will you talk about the way a kingdom or a kingdom's wealth "suffers"? How do people in your language talk about a leader who loses land or authority? Pause this audio here.

The writers use words to try to get favor from the king and to get the king to listen to the writers. The writers describe themselves as loyal to the king. The phrase "we eat the salt of the palace" shows that the writers receive from the palace and therefore owe a debt to the king. The writers tell the king they are writing so that no one will dishonor or shame the king. This language makes the letter sound as if the writers have the king's best interest in mind and have no other motive to write the letter.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: In your culture, how do people say they are loyal to a leader because they depend on the leader for food, support, or life? Pause this audio here.

Stop here and discuss together as a group: How would you send information to a leader in your culture? Discuss what you would say to a leader in your community that you want to do something for you. Make your message sound like you are more concerned about that leader than you really are. Pay attention to the words and phrases you use to show that. Pause this audio here.

Now, the group should storyboard, draw, or use objects to visualize the passage and the action in it.

Embodying the Text
------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 4:7–16 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will dramatize the passage.

This passage has three scenes.

The characters in this passage are:

* Artaxerxes, king of Persia
* Bishlam, Mithredath, Tabeel, and their fellow workers
* Rehum, the commanding officer
* Shimshai, the secretary
* Judges
* Officials
* Administrators
* Ashurbanipal, or Osnappar, king of Assyria
* And the Jews who are rebuilding Jerusalem

As a group, act out the passage twice. You should act out the passage in your own language.

First, act out the passage without stopping. Pay attention to the dialogue, flow, plotline, and chronology of the passage. Make sure you do not skip acting out the difficult or important parts of the passage. Help each other remember every part of the passage.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage.

The group should act out this passage a second time. At certain points in the drama, pause the drama and ask the person playing the character, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person playing the character should answer what they would be feeling or thinking if they were that character. Then continue the drama.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage a second time.

The following is an example of the drama and possible responses.

Bishlam, Mithredath, Tabeel, and the enemies of Judah complain to King Artaxerxes about the Jews rebuilding Jerusalem. The enemies of Judah wrote this letter and translated it into Aramaic.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing Bishlam, Mithredeth, and Tabeel, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* I hope King Artaxerxes pays attention to our complaints; or
* I cannot believe these Jews are taking back our territory and rebuilding their city! I am so mad that the Jews are taking our land!

> Continue the drama.

Rehum, a commanding officer, and Shimshai, the secretary, wrote a strong letter to King Artaxerxes against Jerusalem. The men write on behalf of many leaders in the region, including judges, officials, and administrators.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing Rehum or Shimshai, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* We must make this letter sound very serious. I feel pressure to get the king's attention; or
* I feel proud because many leaders trust me to write this letter for them; or
* I feel worried that the Jews will become powerful again. I want to stop the Jews before it is too late.

Ask the person playing King Artaxerxes, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* There seem to be a lot of complaints about the Jews! Maybe I should pay attention to this; or
* What now? Why are people sending me so many letters about the Jews in the area beyond the River? or
* I really wish someone else could deal with these letters.

> Continue the drama.

This passage includes a copy of the letter that Rehum and Shimshai and their associates wrote. Rehum and Shimshai wrote this letter to King Artaxerxes to warn the king about the Jews rebuilding Jerusalem and the damage it would do to King Artaxerxes' kingdom.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing King Artaxerxes, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* I definitely do not want to lose the taxes that I get now. I had better do something about this; or
* Rehum and Shimshai are clearly good men to look out for me like this. I will see how I can help these men out! or
* I do not like it when people try to get favors from me

Ask the person playing Rehum and Shimshai, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* I hope this works. We wrote the letter from everyone of importance in this area, so I am sure the king will listen! or
* I am so angry because those Jews are trying to take our territory! or
* I am sure if we keep at it, we will stop the Jews from rebuilding, and the king will listen to what we ask of him!

Ask the person playing the exiles rebuilding Jerusalem, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* Why are these people against us? It makes me feel sad and confused; or
* I feel afraid. If the king listens to those letters, we may have to stop building; or
* I feel tired. Trouble keeps coming again and again, but I still want to trust God.

Filling the Gaps
----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 4:7–16 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will discuss key terms and words in this passage.

During the reign of Artaxerxes, **king** of Persia, Bishlam, Mithredath, Tabeel, and their associates wrote a letter to the king. These men wrote the letter and translated it into Aramaic. The king is a person who rules over a territory or region. Be sure to translate king in the same way you have previously. King is in the Master Glossary.

Aramaic was the official language of the Persian Empire.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **Aramaic**. If you have already translated this word in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

Rehum, the commanding officer, and Shimshai, the scribe or secretary, wrote a letter against Jerusalem to Artaxerxes.

Stop here and remember how you discussed what you call a person who writes important letters for leaders. What will you call the **scribe** in this passage? For more information, refer to scribe in the Master Glossary. Pause this audio here.

Rehum, Shimshai, and their associates were judges, officials, and administrators over the people from Persia, Uruk and Babylon, the Elamites and Susa, and the other people who were settled in the city of Samaria and the Trans\-Euphrates. "Trans\-Euphrates" means the land "beyond the River," on the west side of the Euphrates River. Judges decide whether what people do is right or wrong and whether or not those people are guilty or innocent. Officials help run the land. Administrators give orders for daily work in the kingdom.

Stop here and discuss as a group what words or phrases you will use for **judges**, **officials**, and **administrators**. If you have already translated these words in another book of the Bible, use the same words that you have used there. For more information on "judge," refer to the Master Glossary. Pause this audio here.

Stop here and discuss as a group what words or phrases you will use for **Persia**, **Uruk**, **Babylon**, **Elamites**, and **Susa**. If you have already translated these words in another book of the Bible, use the same words that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

Stop here and repeat the words you decided upon in the proper order. Pause this audio here.

Stop here and discuss as a group what words or phrases you will use for **Trans Euphrates** or "beyond the River." If you have already translated this word in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

The writers of the letter sent the letter to King Artaxerxes. The writers call themselves **servants** of the king, who live in the Trans\-Euphrates. In this story, "servant" refers to people who are under the authority of the king. Use the same word or phrase for servant when it means someone who works for a king as you have previously. Servant is in the Master Glossary.

The writers tell the king that the **Jews**, or people who came from Persia back to Jerusalem, are rebuilding the wicked and rebellious city. "Wicked" is very harmful or evil. When someone is rebellious, that person refuses to obey the rules. These Jews are restoring the walls and the foundations of this city. The Jews are descendants of Jacob. The Jews are the Israelites from the tribes of Judah and Benjamin who were exiles in Babylon. You will remember that in previous passages in Ezra, King Cyrus sent the Jews back to Jerusalem to rebuild the temple.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **Jews**. Look up Jews in the Master Glossary. If you have already translated this word in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

Stop here and discuss as a group what words or phrases you will use for **wicked** and **rebellion**. Look up wickedness in the Master Glossary. If you have already translated this word in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

The writers also tell the king that if the Jews build and restore Jerusalem, they will no longer pay taxes, tributes, or duties to the king, and the king's income will suffer losses. Taxes are payments to the ruler. Tributes are gifts or payments to show respect to a powerful king. Duties are fees that people pay at the city gates or trading places.

Stop here and discuss as a group what words or phrases you will use for **taxes**, **tributes**, and **duties**. Remember that you can simply use a general word for "taxes" if you do not have different words for different kinds of taxes in your language. If you have already translated these words in another book of the Bible, use the same words that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

The writers serve the king in his palace and are obligated to serve the king well. A king's palace is the house where the king lives and often rules from.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **palace**, or **king's house**. Pause this audio here.

Since the writers are in service to the kingdom, the writers do not want people to dishonor the king. That is why the writers sent the warning to the king. The king should then search the old records, or **scrolls**, from the previous kings and learn that Jerusalem was rebellious. The kings who controlled the land destroyed Jerusalem because of its history of revolt. To revolt is to rebel against someone or something.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **revolt**. If you have already translated this word in another book in the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **records** or **scrolls**. Most kings' officials wrote their records for the kingdom on long pieces of paper or animal skins, which they then wrapped around long sticks on both sides. You may use your word for scrolls, or a more general word for "documents of a king." For more information about scrolls, refer to scroll in the Master Glossary. Pause this audio here.

The writers then tell King Artaxerxes that if people rebuild the city, then all of the province west of the Euphrates River will no longer belong to King Artaxerxes.

Speaking the Word
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of the passage in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this session, retell the passage, as a group, in your own language.

First, the whole group should practice telling the passage in your own language. Remember to include the things you decided in previous steps, like chronology, words, phrases, emotions, or anything else you discussed.

One group member should tell a few sentences of the passage and pause. Another group member should tell the next part of the passage and pause. Do this until the group has retold the whole passage. The group members can correct each other in this step.

Pause this audio here.

Next, each member of the group should tell the whole passage without interruption at least one time. A group member can use random objects, motions, or their storyboard to help them remember the passage.

Pause this audio here.

Next, the group can decide whose version of the passage you like the most. You may decide that you liked the way one group member told one scene but you liked the way a different group member told another scene.

Pause this audio here.

Finally, the group can put together a final version of the passage that everyone agrees on. Each group member should practice telling this final version of the passage.

* **Associated Passages:** Ezra 4:7-16
* **Associated Articles:** Jew (ID: `eng-t77-v2@FIAKeyTerms`); servant (ID: `eng-t123-v3@FIAKeyTerms`); scribe (ID: `eng-t155-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); wickedness (ID: `eng-t215-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); king (ID: `eng-t226-v2@FIAKeyTerms`); judge (ID: `eng-t289-v2@FIAKeyTerms`)

* **Associated ACAI Concepts:** Artaxerxes (ID: `person:Artaxerxes.2`); Euphrates River (ID: `place:Euphrates`); Rehum (ID: `person:Rehum.2`); Shimshai (ID: `person:Shimshai`); Persia (ID: `place:Persia`); Jerusalem (ID: `place:Jerusalem`); Tabeel (ID: `person:Tabeel`); Dinaite (ID: `person:Dinaite`); Erech (ID: `place:Erech`); Mithredath (ID: `person:Mithredath.2`); Bishlam (ID: `person:Bishlam`); Osnappar (ID: `person:Osnappar`); Babylonians (ID: `group:Babylon`); Persians (ID: `group:Persia`); Babylon (ID: `place:Babylon`); Elam (ID: `place:Elam`); Elamites (ID: `group:Elam`); Jews (ID: `group:Jews`); Samaria (ID: `place:Samaria`); Susa (ID: `place:Susa`); Foundation (ID: `realia:Foundation`); Scroll (ID: `realia:Scroll`)

## Ezra 4:17–24 (id: eng-ezr-p7-v1)

Hear and Heart
--------------

In this step, hear Ezra 4:17–24 and put it in your hearts.

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 4:17–24 three times, in three different translations, if possible.

Pause this audio here.

Now, as a group, discuss the following questions:

1. What do you like in this passage? Pause this audio here.
2. What do you not like or not understand in this passage? Pause this audio here.
3. What does this passage tell us about God? Pause this audio here.
4. What does this passage tell us about people? Pause this audio here.
5. How does this passage affect our daily lives? Pause this audio here.
6. Who do you know who needs to hear this passage?

Setting the Stage
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 4:17–24 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

The enemies of the exiles in Jerusalem, Rehum and Shimshai, wrote a letter to King Artaxerxes of Persia to complain about the exiles that returned to rebuild Jerusalem. In this passage, the king writes a reply to the enemies of the Jewish exiles in Jerusalem.

King Artaxerxes answers the letter of the Jewish enemies. The enemies of the exiles wrote the letter in Aramaic. Someone translated and read the letter to the King of Persia. Because of the letter, King Artaxerxes ordered someone to search the records for information about the kings of Jerusalem.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: In your culture, what kinds of orders do rulers give? What happens if a person ignores or tries to change an order? Pause this audio here.

The records showed that the people in the city of Jerusalem had rebelled against other kings.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: What kinds of records or stories do people keep about your culture, or the cultures in the surrounding areas? How do people in your culture keep these records? Who is able to access these records? Pause this audio here.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: Have you heard of a place or group that people know as troublemakers in your community? How does that reputation affect that place or group, even years later? Pause this audio here.

Many mighty kings had ruled Jerusalem and the Trans\-Euphrates, or the province "beyond the River." We do not know if these records are specifically about the great Israelite kings like David and Solomon or about other kings like the Assyrian and Babylonian kings. These Assyrian and Babylonian kings collected taxes from the people in the province. Those kings of Jerusalem, however, received tributes, customs, and tolls. These are all types of taxes that the people paid to kings.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: Why do leaders in your culture care so much about taxes or contributions? What happens when people stop paying taxes or contributions? Have one or two people act as leaders who are reacting to people who have stopped paying taxes. Pause this audio here.

Therefore, the enemies of Israel are to issue an order to stop people from rebuilding the city until King Artaxerxes makes an order that people can rebuild again. This order is important because no one could change a Persian King's order once he ordered it. King Artaxerxes tells the enemies of Israel not to waste any time but to make the Israelites stop immediately.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: In your culture, how do leaders show that what they command is urgent and that people must do it quickly? Pause this audio here.

King Artaxerxes asks a question that the king does not want anyone to answer. King Artaxerxes asks why the king should let things get worse and damage the king's interests. The king does not tell the enemies to destroy the progress the Jews have already made on rebuilding Jerusalem. However, it is possible that this is the time when the enemies destroyed the walls of Jerusalem and set the gates on fire.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How have your community leaders reacted when people made complaints? What must people do when the leaders give orders to fix those problems? Pause this audio here.

When someone read the letter from King Artaxerxes to Rehum the commander, and Shimshai the scribe, and their fellow workers, the ones who heard the letter went to the Jews who returned from exile and forced the people to stop rebuilding the city of Jerusalem. It is most likely that the officials took many Samaritan soldiers with them to force the Israelites to stop building.

Most likely, the storyteller tells us about this opposition during the reign of King Artaxerxes to remind us that the enemies of the Jews opposed the Jews a lot! The Jews had to persevere through many hard times! But now the storyteller goes back in time to his main story about when the enemies of the Jews stopped the work on the "house of God," or temple, in Jerusalem during the reign of King Cyrus many years before King Artaxerxes. You will remember that during the reign of King Cyrus, enemies of the Jews stopped the work on the temple. But King Darius allowed the Jews to start the work again, and the Jews completed the temple during Darius' reign. In the next passage, the storyteller will return to the story when the Jews started to rebuild the temple for the second time during King Darius' reign.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: Share experiences where you, someone else, or your community were forced to wait for something to start again that had stopped for a long time. How did people feel? What happened during the wait?

Defining the Scenes
-------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 4:17–24 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will define the scenes, characters, and setting of this passage. Then the group will visualize this passage.

This passage has three scenes.

**In the first scene:** King Artaxerxes answers the letter of the Jewish enemies from the previous passage. King Artaxerxes takes the writers' accusation seriously. The king reports that the records confirm that the people of Jerusalem rebel against kings a lot. The king gives an order to stop the Jews from rebuilding the city until King Artaxerxes says that the Jews can restart.

**In the second scene:** When someone read the letter from King Artaxerxes to Rehum the commander, and Shimshai the scribe, and their fellow workers, the ones who heard the letter went to the Jews who returned from exile and forced them to stop rebuilding the city of Jerusalem.

**In the third scene:** In the time of King Darius, the exiles of the tribes of Judah and Benjamin stopped the work on the house of God in Jerusalem until the second year of the reign of King Darius of Persia.

The characters in this passage are:

* King Artaxerxes
* Rehum
* Shimshai
* Fellow workers of Rehum and Shimshai
* Other previous and powerful kings of Jerusalem
* The Jewish exiles rebuilding Jerusalem
* And King Darius

As a group, pay attention to these parts of the passage's setting: The king of Persia sends an answer in the form of a letter, and we hear the letter in this passage. The letter is in the traditional letter\-writing form of that culture. The author names the people to whom the king writes and gives an opening greeting. The king names the same two people and their fellow workers who wrote the first letter. These people live in Samaria and elsewhere in the province of the Trans\-Euphrates. You will remember that Samaria is the former Northern Kingdom of Israel.

In the first scene, the opening of the king's letter includes a normal greeting, "peace." The king hopes the people are doing well and are at peace with the people around them. This is an unusual greeting from a king to his people.

Stop here and discuss as a group what a typical greeting is that you would expect to receive from a leader in your culture. How will you translate the king's greeting of "peace" in this letter? Pause this audio here.

The king states that he received the letter, and someone translated the letter and read it to the king. The king talks about "us" receiving the letter. The king may be referring to himself and his government officials as the people who received the letter. Sometimes, kings in that time referred to themselves as "us" or "we" as a sign that they were royal and of high status.

Stop here and discuss as a group how your kings or leaders talk about themselves. How will you talk about who the king says received the letter? Pause this audio here.

The king ordered someone to search the records to find information about Jerusalem.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How does your culture talk about looking into old writings or past decisions? Do you have a natural way to say "official records" or "written history"? Think about how you translated the idea of historical records from the previous passage. Pause this audio here.

We do not know exactly what the king ordered, but the king probably ordered his people to search the records. You will remember that in the Persian kingdom, people could not change the order a king made.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How do you refer to a command that people cannot change? How is a ruler's command different from an ordinary request? Pause this audio here.

The king uses the same words for search and found that were used in Rehum and Shimshai's letter to the king.

Therefore, the enemies of the Jews are to issue an order to stop the rebuilding of the city until King Artaxerxes makes an order that they can rebuild again. King Artaxerxes tells the enemies of Israel not to waste any time in getting the Israelites to stop. The king asks a question that he does not expect anyone to answer. The king asks, "Why should you let the Jewish people be a threat to our kingdom?" The king means that he wants the enemies of the Jews to stop the work.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How do you show urgency and force in your language? Pause this audio here.

\[At the end of this passage, the storyteller takes us back in time and returns to his original story during the reign of King Darius, when the Jews were able to start building the temple again.]{.mark} Remember that the exiles returned to Jerusalem and began rebuilding the temple under King Cyrus. Enemies of the Jews stopped the Jews from rebuilding the temple for a long time, until the time of King Darius, when the Jews were able to finish rebuilding the temple.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How does your language show long periods of activity in a story? How can you help listeners feel the weight of waiting many years? Pause this audio here.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How will you show that the storyteller has returned to his previous story that he had paused at chapter 4 verse 5? Pause this audio here.

Now, the group should storyboard, draw, or use objects to visualize the passage and the action in it.

Embodying the Text
------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 4:17–24 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will dramatize the passage.

This passage has three scenes.

The characters in this passage are:

* King Artaxerxes
* Rehum
* Shimshai
* Fellow workers of Rehum and Shimshai
* Other previous and powerful kings of Jerusalem
* The Jewish exiles rebuilding Jerusalem
* And King Darius

As a group, act out the passage twice. You should act out the passage in your own language.

First, act out the passage without stopping. Pay attention to the dialogue, flow, plotline, and chronology of the passage. Make sure you do not skip acting out the difficult or important parts of the passage. Help each other remember every part of the passage.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage.

The group should act out this passage a second time. At certain points in the drama, pause the drama and ask the person playing the character, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person playing the character should answer what they would be feeling or thinking if they were that character. Then continue the drama.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage a second time.

The following is an example of the drama and possible responses.

King Artaxerxes answers the letter of the Jewish enemies. King Artaxerxes takes the writers' accusation seriously. The king reports that he has confirmed from the historical records the information that the enemies of the Jews sent to the king about the rebellion and sedition of Jerusalem. Therefore, the enemies of Israel are to issue an order to stop the rebuilding of the city until King Artaxerxes makes an order that people can start again.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing King Artaxerxes, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* I am so thankful that the people wrote to me about the rebuilding of Jerusalem; or
* I am serious about my order to make the Israelites stop rebuilding! or
* I am glad I have good people to tell me important information about my kingdom.

Continue the drama.

Someone read the letter from King Artaxerxes to Rehum the commander, and Shimshai the scribe, and their associates. The ones who heard the letter went to the Jews who returned from exile and forced them to stop rebuilding the city of Jerusalem.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing Rehum and Shimshai, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* Yes! I knew our letter would work! We are vindicated! or
* Now we get to go tell those Israelites to stop their work! The Israelites will be sorry they came here. I feel so justified; or
* Finally, we have a king who listens to us!

> Continue the drama.

Rehum, Shimshai, and their men arrive in Jerusalem and force the Jews to stop rebuilding the city.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing Rehum or Shimshai, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* It feels good to be in control at last; or
* Now the people must obey us. I feel strong and satisfied; or
* I want to make sure the work stops completely. I feel strict and determined.

Continue the drama.

The people of Judah and Benjamin stop working on the city of Jerusalem.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing the Israelites, "What are you feeling or thinking?"

The person might answer things like:

* My heart feels heavy. We were obeying God, and now we must stop; or
* I feel disappointed and tired after all our hard work; or
* I do not understand God's plan, but I still want to trust God; or
* This feels like a great loss. Everything we built is now silent.

Continue the drama.

Going back to the time of King Darius, the exiles of the tribes of Judah and Benjamin stopped the work on the house of God in Jerusalem until the second year of the reign of King Darius of Persia.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing the Israelites, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* We just want to do what God asked us to. Why did God allow this to happen? I am confused; or
* I trust that God will have his way with God's enemies. This is not the end of the temple! or
* I am so defeated. We were making such good progress.

Filling the Gaps
----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 4:17–24 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will discuss key terms and words in this passage.

**King** Artaxerxes answers the letter from the enemies of the exiles in Jerusalem: Rehum the **commander**, and Shimshai the **scribe**, and their fellow workers. King Artaxerxes begins the letter with the greeting of peace. Use the same words for king, commander, and scribe as you have used in previous passages. King and scribe are in the Master Glossary.

Stop here and discuss as a group how you will translate the king's greeting of "**peace**." For more information about peace, refer to the Master Glossary. Pause this audio here.

The king tells the enemies of the exiles that his people translated the **letter** that the people had written in Aramaic and read it to the king. Because of the letter, King Artaxerxes made an order, which is a law that no one can change\*\*.\*\* Because of that order, someone searched the records for information about the kings of Jerusalem. The records showed that the city of Jerusalem had risen against other kings in **rebellion** and **revolt**. Use the same words for letter, rebellion, and revolt that you have used in previous passages.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **order**. Pause this audio here.

Many strong kings had ruled Jerusalem in the **Trans\-Euphrates** or "province beyond the River." Those kings of Jerusalem received **taxes**, **tributes**, and **duties**. These are all types of taxes that people paid to kings. Be sure to use the same words for Trans\-Euphrates, taxes, tributes, and duties as you did in previous passages.

Therefore, the enemies of Israel are to issue an order to stop people from rebuilding the city until King Artaxerxes makes a decree. King Artaxerxes tells the enemies of the **Jews** not to waste any time but to make the Jews stop immediately. King Artaxerxes asks why the king should let things get worse and damage the king's interests. Most people use the word "Jew" to refer to those Israelites who mostly came from the tribes of Benjamin and Judah. These were the Israelites who lived in the southern kingdom of Israel called Judah. These Israelites came back to Judah, the southern kingdom of Israel, when King Cyrus said they could come back. Use the same word or phrase for Jew as you used in the previous passage. For more information about Jews, refer to the Master Glossary.

When people read the letter from King Artaxerxes to Rehum the commander, and Shimshai the scribe, and their associates, the ones who heard the letter went to the Jews who returned from exile and forced them to stop rebuilding Jerusalem.

The exiles of the tribes of Judah and Benjamin stopped the work on the **temple** or "**house of God**" in Jerusalem until the second year of the reign of King Darius of Persia. The temple of God is the place where the Israelites went to worship the one true God. "Temple," or "house of God," is in the Master Glossary. Be sure to use the same word you have used in previous passages.

Speaking the Word
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of the passage in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this session, retell the passage, as a group, in your own language.

First, the whole group should practice telling the passage in your own language. Remember to include the things you decided in previous steps, like chronology, words, phrases, emotions, or anything else you discussed.

One group member should tell a few sentences of the passage and pause. Another group member should tell the next part of the passage and pause. Do this until the group has retold the whole passage. The group members can correct each other in this step.

Pause this audio here.

Next, each member of the group should tell the whole passage without interruption at least one time. A group member can use random objects, motions, or their storyboard to help them remember the passage.

Pause this audio here.

Next, the group can decide whose version of the passage you like the most. You may decide that you liked the way one group member told one scene but you liked the way a different group member told another scene.

Pause this audio here.

Finally, the group can put together a final version of the passage that everyone agrees on. Each group member should practice telling this final version of the passage.

* **Associated Passages:** Ezra 4:17-24
* **Associated Articles:** Jew (ID: `eng-t77-v2@FIAKeyTerms`); temple (ID: `eng-t138-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); scribe (ID: `eng-t155-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); peace (ID: `eng-t161-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); house of God (ID: `eng-t183-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); king (ID: `eng-t226-v2@FIAKeyTerms`)

* **Associated ACAI Concepts:** Jerusalem (ID: `place:Jerusalem`); Rehum (ID: `person:Rehum.2`); Shimshai (ID: `person:Shimshai`); Euphrates River (ID: `place:Euphrates`); Darius (ID: `person:Darius.2`); Samaria (ID: `place:Samaria`); Jews (ID: `group:Jews`); Artaxerxes (ID: `person:Artaxerxes.2`); Persia (ID: `place:Persia`); House (ID: `realia:House`)

## Ezra 5:1–5 (id: eng-ezr-p8-v1)

Hear and Heart
--------------

In this step, hear Ezra 5:1–5 and put it in your hearts.

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 5:1–5 three times, in three different translations, if possible.

Pause this audio here.

Now, as a group, discuss the following questions:

1. What do you like in this passage? Pause this audio here.
2. What do you not like or not understand in this passage? Pause this audio here.
3. What does this passage tell us about God? Pause this audio here.
4. What does this passage tell us about people? Pause this audio here.
5. How does this passage affect our daily lives? Pause this audio here.
6. Who do you know who needs to hear this passage?

Setting the Stage
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 5:1–5 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

Remember that in the previous passage, the storyteller told us about times after the Jews finished building God's temple when enemies tried to stop the Jews from rebuilding Jerusalem. Now the story goes back to what the storyteller was talking about at the beginning of chapter 4\. The storyteller takes us back to the time of King Darius, when the Jews started rebuilding the temple in the second year of King Darius' reign as king of Persia. This story is about the Jews obeying God. The story is also about God keeping his promises to the Jews.

This story starts with two men named Haggai and Zechariah. Haggai and Zachariah, two of God's prophets, gave the Jews a message from God. A prophet is someone who listens to God and gives God's messages to other people. The storyteller mentions that Zechariah is from the family of Iddo, an important family of priests. We know from the book of Zechariah that Zechariah is the grandson of Iddo. In this story, the storyteller does not tell us what message Haggai and Zechariah gave the Jews, but someone wrote God's message in the books people called "Haggai" and "Zechariah."

Stop here and discuss as a group: How do people from your culture hear from God? How is that different or the same from the way that prophets in Judah heard from God? Pause this audio here.

We hear earlier in the book of Ezra that the enemies of the Jews frustrated the Jews so much that the Jews stopped building the temple during King Cyrus' reign as king of Persia. We know from the book of Haggai that instead of building the temple, the people started building their own houses and making their own houses look pretty. This made God sad because God's temple was unfinished for many years while the Jews made their own houses look pretty instead.

Stop here and tell the group a story of a time in your life when you started a project but never finished it. Explain what happened to keep you from finishing. Pause this audio here.

At the beginning of this story, Haggai and Zechariah gave a message to the Jews who lived in Jerusalem and Judah during the reign of King Darius. The storyteller in Ezra does not say what Haggai and Zechariah told the Jews, but the storyteller is very clear that Haggai and Zechariah spoke for God. This means that Haggai and Zechariah's message from God was just as true as if God said it to the Jews himself. It seems that Haggai and Zechariah told the Jews that God wanted the Jews to rebuild the temple, so Zerubbabel and Jeshua decided to start building the temple again because of what Haggai and Zechariah said.

Zerubbabel and Jeshua decided to restart building the temple even though Darius, the king of Persia at that time, had not given people permission to start. God's prophets helped and supported the Jews to rebuild the temple.

Stop here and tell a story to the group of a time in your life when you decided to do something because you knew it was right, even if the people around you did not approve of what you were doing. Pause this audio here.

While the Jews were building the temple again, two Persian officials came to visit Jerusalem. One of the men, named Tattenai, was an important leader in the Persian kingdom. Tattenai's job was to govern a place the storyteller calls the Trans\-Euphrates, or "beyond the River," which was a large area of land south of the Euphrates River that included Judah and Jerusalem. Because the Persian kingdom was so big, Tattenai's job was to manage the area of land south of the Euphrates River so that the king of Persia could focus on ruling the whole kingdom.

Stop here and look at a map of the Trans\-Euphrates area. Notice where this area is in relation to Judah and Jerusalem. Pause this audio here.

Shethar\-Bozenai was the other man who came from Persia. Shethar\-Bozenai was another special person in Persia, but was not as important as Tattenai. Shethar\-Bozenai may have been Tattenai's helper. Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai arrived in Judah with other Persian government officials. We do not know what these officials did, but they likely came to help Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: In this story, the rulers of Persia had many kinds of people help them rule the land and make sure the people in the kingdom followed the rules. How do leaders in your culture organize their officials to make sure that people are following the rules? Pause the audio here.

When Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai arrived in Judah, they talked to the Jews who were building the temple. Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai asked the Jews who gave the Jews permission to start building the temple again. Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai also asked to know the names of the men who were building the temple. Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai probably asked these questions so that they could tell King Darius what was happening in Judah. During the first two years of his rule, King Darius had to deal with many people throughout his kingdom who were trying to challenge his power as king. So, Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai could be worried that the Jews will become troublemakers.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: In your culture, what do people in authority do to find out if a group might cause trouble for the government or community? Pause this audio here.

But God was protecting the Jews. Tattenai and Shethar\-Bosenai's visit could have kept the Jews from finishing the temple, but God protected the Jews from having to stop building again. The storyteller mentions that the eye of God was on the Jews during this time. This is important to notice because the kings of Persia would often use spies called the "king's eyes" who would spy on people to help the king know what was happening. When the storyteller mentions that the eye of God was on the Jews, the storyteller is saying that God's knowledge and understanding were better and stronger than any of the Persian kings' knowledge and understanding. Tattenai and Shethar\-Bosenai did not stop the Jews from working until Tattenai and Shethar\-Bosenai asked King Darius what they should do.

Stop here and tell a story where it seemed like something bad was going to happen, but something good happened instead. How did you feel during that time?

Defining the Scenes
-------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 5:1–5 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will define the scenes, characters, and setting of this passage. Then the group will visualize this passage.

This passage has three scenes.

**In the first scene:** Haggai and Zechariah give the Jews a message from God. Jeshua and Zerubbabel help the Jews start building the temple again.

**In the second scene:** Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai ask the Jews who gave the Jews permission to build the temple again. Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai also ask the Jews for the names of the people who are building the temple.

**In the third scene:** God protects the Jews from having to stop building the temple. Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai decide not to stop the Jews from working on the temple until Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai can talk to King Darius of Persia.

The characters in this passage are:

* Haggai
* Zechariah
* The Jews
* God
* Zerubbabel
* Jeshua
* Tattenai
* Shethar\-Bozenai
* And Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai's fellow workers

As a group, pay attention to these parts of the passage's setting:

In scene one, the story starts during the reign of King Darius. In the previous passage, the Jews stopped building the temple because their enemies frustrated the Jews so much. Now, in the second year of King Darius' reign as king of Persia, Haggai and Zechariah bring a message to the Jews from God.

Stop here and discuss as a group how you will start this passage. What word or phrase will you use to show that this passage continues the story from the end of the previous passage? Pause this audio here.

In scene one, Haggai and Zechariah, the son of Iddo, give the Jews a message from God. It is important to know that when the storyteller says Zechariah is the son of Iddo, the storyteller means that Zechariah is from Iddo's family. Zechariah is actually Iddo's grandson. It is also important to know that the storyteller says Haggai and Zechariah spoke in the name of God. This means that Haggai and Zechariah were acting with God's power and were representing God. This is important because it means that what Haggai and Zechariah said was as important as if God had told the Jews the message himself.

Stop here and discuss as a group how you will talk about how Haggai and Zechariah speak in the name of God. Pause this audio here.

After the Jews hear God's message from Haggai and Zechariah, Jeshua and Zerubbabel start rebuilding the house of God again. The house of God is another way to say God's temple. The "prophets," or God's messengers, helped Jeshua and Zerubbabel and all the people who were working on the temple.
