Stop here and tell a story about a time when you did something because someone else told you it would be a good idea. Notice the words you use to describe the order of events in your story. Pause this audio here.

In scene two, Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and their fellow workers come to visit the Jews after the Jews have started building the temple again. Tattenai is the governor of the Persian province of the Trans\-Euphrates, or the "province beyond the River." Jerusalem is in this province. Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai likely met the Jews in Jerusalem, where the Jews were building the temple.

Stop here and discuss as a group what words you use for government officials who rule over a large province in your country. What do you call a government official's helpers or fellow workers? What could you call Tattenai and his fellow workers in this story? Pause this audio here.

Stop here and look at a map of the Trans\-Euphrates area again. Remind each other what you called this area in previous passages. Pause this audio here.

After Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai arrive in Jerusalem, they ask the Jews who gave them permission to start building the temple again. Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai also ask for the names of the people who are working to build the temple. The storyteller records Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai's exact words to describe what Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai asked the Jews. It is important to know that Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai were probably not angry at the Jews when they asked these questions. Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai were just doing their job and trying to understand what the Jews were doing in Jerusalem.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: In this story, the storyteller tells the exact words Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai used when they talked with the Jews to show that they were officials who were doing an official job. The government required the men to ask these questions. How do you show in your language that the questions people ask or the things that they say are official or very important? Pause this audio here.

In scene three, the storyteller states that the eye of God was on the Jews. Remember that this means that God's power and understanding were better than the Persian king's power and understanding. It also means that even though Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai could have stopped the Jews from working on the temple, God protected the Jews. Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai decided to let the Jews continue to work on the temple while they asked King Darius what he wanted Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai to do.

Stop here and describe what the "eye of God" means in your own words. Notice what words you choose! What word or phrase will you use to describe the eye of God in this story? Pause this audio here.

Now, the group should storyboard, draw, or use objects to visualize the passage and the action in it.

Embodying the Text
------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 5:1–5 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will dramatize the passage.

This passage has three scenes.

The characters in this passage are:

* Haggai
* Zechariah
* The Jews
* God
* Zerubbabel
* Jeshua
* Tattenai
* Shethar\-Bozenai
* And Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai's fellow workers

As a group, act out the passage twice. You should act out the passage in your own language.

First, act out the passage without stopping. Pay attention to the dialogue, flow, plotline, and chronology of the passage. Make sure you do not skip acting out the difficult or important parts of the passage. Help each other remember every part of the passage.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage.

The group should act out this passage a second time. At certain points in the drama, pause the drama and ask the person playing the character, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person playing the character should answer what they would be feeling or thinking if they were that character. Then continue the drama.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage a second time.

The following is an example of the drama and possible responses.

Haggai and Zechariah take a message from God to the Jews in Jerusalem. Jeshua and Zerubbabel hear God's message and begin to build the temple again. God's prophets, God's messengers, help the people who build God's temple.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing God, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* My people were so excited to build my temple and worship me, but now they have stopped. I was so excited to get to be with my people again. It makes me sad that my people have not finished my temple yet; or
* I am frustrated that my people gave up building my temple. It makes me feel like other things are more important to my people than I am. That makes me sad.

Ask the people playing Haggai and Zechariah, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The people might answer things like:

* This message is very important because it is from God. But I am scared that the Jews are not going to like the message. What is going to happen? or
* I am excited that God is speaking to his people again! It is such a privilege to be able to serve God like this.

Ask the people playing Jeshua and Zerubbabel, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The people might answer things like:

* I am so sorry that we did not put God first and work to finish his temple. Let's fix our mistake! or
* It is scary to start building the temple again because we do not know if King Darius will allow us to. But it is the right thing to do because we need to put God first. I pray that God protects us as we start the work.

Continue the drama.

Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai and their fellow workers meet the Jews in Jerusalem. Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai ask the Jews, "Who allowed you to build this house and to finish this structure?" and "What are the names of the men who are building this building?"

Pause the drama. Ask the people playing Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The people might answer things like:

* If the Jews finish the temple, they could want to fight the king and cause lots of trouble. Then the king might get angry at us for letting the Jews cause trouble! We need to make sure that it will not happen; or
* Oh, this is such a long way to travel just to talk to these people! I hope the trip is worth it.

Ask the people playing the Jews, Jeshua, and Zerubbabel, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The people might answer things like:

* It is so scary to have important people from the Persian government come to ask us questions! We know we are going against the king's orders. What if people punish us for disobeying the king's orders? What if people make us stop working on the temple? What will God do then? or
* It does not matter what the important people from Persia say or do. We must finish God's temple no matter what because God is more important.

Continue the drama.

God protects the Jews from having to stop work on the temple. Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai decide not to do anything until King Darius gets their report and sends them a reply letter.

Pause the drama. Ask the people playing Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The people might answer things like:

* If we make a mistake and order the Jews to do the wrong thing, the king could blame us for whatever happens. We should ask King Darius what he thinks we should do so that we do not get in trouble; or
* It does not seem like the Jews are doing anything dangerous, so we can let them continue to work. Let's just make sure that King Darius agrees with us.

Ask the people playing the Jews, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The people might answer things like:

* Praise God! God has protected us from getting in trouble for now, so we can keep building the temple; or
* What if King Darius does not like what we are doing? What will happen then?

Ask the person playing God, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* I am so happy that my people have put me first, even if that means they have to disobey the king; or
* My people are trying so hard to obey me. I want to protect my people so that they can continue to obey my commands.

Filling the Gaps
----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 5:1–5 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will discuss key terms and words in this passage.

In this story, the prophets Haggai and Zechariah prophesied to the Jews in Jerusalem and Judah. A prophet is someone who gives messages from God to the people. The message could be anything that God wanted to tell his people! The message could be a warning about what will happen in the future. People did not always treat prophets well, and sometimes even tried to kill prophets!

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **prophet** and **prophesy**. Look up prophet and prophesy in the Master Glossary for more information. If you have already translated this word in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

Haggai and Zechariah, the **son** of Iddo, prophesied to the Jews in Jerusalem and Judah. "Son of Iddo" is another way to say **descendant** of Iddo. A person's descendants are their children, grandchildren, and all the generations of people after them from the same family line. For more information on descendant, refer to the Master Glossary. Use the same word or phrase for descendant as you used in previous passages.

Haggai and Zechariah gave God's message to the **Jews** who were in Jerusalem. Jew is another word that refers to the Israelites, or God's people. Most people use the word Jew to refer to the Israelites who are from the tribes of Judah and Benjamin. These Israelites came back to Judah, the southern kingdom of Israel, when King Cyrus said they could come back. After these Israelites came back to Judah, most people began calling all of God's special people Jews. Use the same word or phrase for Jews as you used in previous passages. For more information on Jew, refer to the Master Glossary.

The Jews lived in **Jerusalem**, which is in the area of **Judah**. The area of land called Judah is the area of land God gave the Israelite tribe of Judah hundreds of years before King Cyrus told the Jews they could return to their homeland. For more information on Jerusalem and Judah, refer to the Master Glossary. Use the same words or phrases for Jerusalem and Judah as you used in previous passages.

Haggai and Zechariah gave God's message to the Israelites in Jerusalem "in the name of the **God** of **Israel**." This means that Haggai and Zechariah spoke for God. The message that Haggai and Zechariah gave the Israelites had the same power and importance as if God had spoken it directly to the Israelites himself. In other words, when Haggai and Zechariah spoke "in the name of the God of Israel," it meant that Haggai and Zechariah represented God and spoke with the same authority as God when they gave the Israelites God's message. \[God is the general term for God who created the world. Israel refers to the descendants of Jacob. Use the same words for God and Israel when referring to the phrase "God of Israel." For more information on God and Israel, refer to the Master Glossary.]{.mark}

\[Stop here and discuss how you will translate the phrase **in the name of the God of Israel**. If you have translated this phrase in another book of the Bible, use the same word or phrase that you have used there. Pause this audio here.]{.mark}

After hearing God's message, Zerubbabel and Jeshua start building the **house of God**. The house of God is another term for God's **temple**. It is the place on earth where God meets his people. The house of God is where God lived on earth with his people in the Old Testament. Use the same word or phrase for "house of God" or temple that you used in previous passages. For more information on the house of God and temple, refer to the Master Glossary.

Tattenai was an important Persian who came to Israel to talk with the Israelites. Tattenai was a **governor** of a part of eastern Persia. A governor is someone who represents the king in a small area of the kingdom. When a king rules a very large area of land, the king cannot rule all the people himself because it would be too much for one person to handle. A governor rules a small part of the kingdom but is still under the rule of the king. A governor will take care of small problems himself and bring bigger problems to the king. This way, the king can focus only on the big problems of the kingdom. Use the same word or phrase for governor as you used in Ezra 2:63\.

Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai arrived in Israel with **their fellow workers**. These are men who work a similar job to Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai. It probably means that Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai arrived in Jerusalem along with other people who helped rule Persia but who were not as important as Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai.

Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai asked the Israelites who gave them an **order** to build the temple. An order is a command that often comes from kings or rulers and that tells the people what to do or what not to do. In this case, Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai are asking the Israelites who gave them the order to start work on the temple again. Use the same word or phrase for order as you used in previous passages.

The storyteller describes that the eye of their God was on the elders of the Jews. This phrase means that God was watching over his people and protecting them. It is supposed to be a good thing because God is taking care of his people.

Stop here and remind each other how you chose to say "**the eye of their God**" in step three. Pause this audio here.

The elders were important people in the Israelite community because the elders led the community. An elder was often the head of an important family or group of families.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **elders**. Look up elders in the Master Glossary for more information. If you have already translated this word in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

The Persian men did not stop the Israelites from working on the temple until their report reached King Darius. A report is a description of someone's experience at an event. Normally, reports are very official and formal.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for "**report**." If you have already translated this word in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there.

* 

Speaking the Word
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of the passage in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this session, retell the passage, as a group, in your own language.

First, the whole group should practice telling the passage in your own language. Remember to include the things you decided in previous steps, like chronology, words, phrases, emotions, or anything else you discussed.

One group member should tell a few sentences of the passage and pause. Another group member should tell the next part of the passage and pause. Do this until the group has retold the whole passage. The group members can correct each other in this step.

Pause this audio here.

Next, each member of the group should tell the whole passage without interruption at least one time. A group member can use random objects, motions, or their storyboard to help them remember the passage.

Pause this audio here.

Next, the group can decide whose version of the passage you like the most. You may decide that you liked the way one group member told one scene but you liked the way a different group member told another scene.

Pause this audio here.

Finally, the group can put together a final version of the passage that everyone agrees on. Each group member should practice telling this final version of the passage.

* **Associated Passages:** Ezra 5:1-5
* **Associated Articles:** elder (ID: `eng-t43-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); Israel (ID: `eng-t74-v2@FIAKeyTerms`); Jew (ID: `eng-t77-v2@FIAKeyTerms`); Judea (ID: `eng-t81-v2@FIAKeyTerms`); prophet (ID: `eng-t104-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); temple (ID: `eng-t138-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); house of God (ID: `eng-t183-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); God (ID: `eng-t189-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); descendant (ID: `eng-t234-v2@FIAKeyTerms`)

* **Associated ACAI Concepts:** Jerusalem (ID: `place:Jerusalem`); Jews (ID: `group:Jews`); House (ID: `realia:House`); Zechariah (ID: `person:Zechariah.17`); Tattenai (ID: `person:Tattenai`); Shethar-bozenai (ID: `person:Shethar-bozenai`); Haggai (ID: `person:Haggai`); Iddo (ID: `person:Iddo.6`); Israel (ID: `person:Israel`); Zerubbabel (ID: `person:Zerubbabel.2`); Darius (ID: `person:Darius.2`); Euphrates River (ID: `place:Euphrates`); Joshua (ID: `person:Joshua.5`); Jehozadak (ID: `person:Jehozadak`); Judah (ID: `person:Judah.4`); Shealtiel (ID: `person:Shealtiel.2`)

## Ezra 5:6–17 (id: eng-ezr-p9-v1)

Hear and Heart
--------------

In this step, hear Ezra 5:6–17 and put it in your hearts.

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 5:6–17 three times, in three different translations, if possible.

Pause this audio here.

Now, as a group, discuss the following questions:

1. What do you like in this passage? Pause this audio here.
2. What do you not like or not understand in this passage? Pause this audio here.
3. What does this passage tell us about God? Pause this audio here.
4. What does this passage tell us about people? Pause this audio here.
5. How does this passage affect our daily lives? Pause this audio here.
6. Who do you know who needs to hear this passage?

Setting the Stage
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 5:6–17 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In the last story, Jeshua and Zerubbabel led the Jews as they started to build God's temple again. Two Persian government officials and their fellow workers, named Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai, questioned the Jews. The men sent a letter to King Darius, the king of Persia, to ask Darius what to do. In this story, the storyteller describes the letter that Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai sent to King Darius.

Remember, Tattenai was the governor of the province of Trans\-Euphrates, or the "province beyond the River," and the men who came with Tattenai worked in the same province. Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the group of leaders with them all sent a message to King Darius in Persia. The men wrote the message to King Darius to explain what the Jews were doing and to ask the king's advice.

Stop here and look at a map of the Trans\-Euphrates area as a group. Pause this audio here.

Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai start their message to King Darius with a formal and respectful greeting.

Stop here and divide yourselves into a few groups. Greet each other and pretend to be different people in different situations. For example, pretend to be friends who are seeing each other for the first time in a long time. What would you say or do? You could also be people meeting each other to discuss business plans. What would you say or do then? Pause this audio here.

After they greet the king, Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and their co\-workers explain to the king what they saw in Jerusalem. Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai send their message to the king very properly. In their report, Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai do not explain what they think about what the Jews are doing. Instead, the men only tell the king the facts about what happened.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: In your culture, how does a government leader write or speak to another leader who is more important than he is? Pretend that you are describing something that happened in your area to a high government official. Practice sharing that description with each other. Pause the audio here.

In their letter, Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other men explain to King Darius that they traveled to Jerusalem to see the house of the great God. The men describe that the Jews are using big, polished stones and wood beams to build the temple's walls. Tattenai and his co\-workers tell King Darius that the Jews are very careful as they work on the temple and that the Jews are making a lot of progress!

Stop here and have one person in the group describe a time when they saw people build an important building. Have everyone else in the group listen closely and notice how that person describes the building they saw. Pause this audio here.

Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other men also report to King Darius about how they talked to the Jews. Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other men explain that they asked the Jewish leaders who gave the Jews permission to work on the temple. Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the men also asked the Jewish elders for the names of the people who were working on the temple.

Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other officials also tell King Darius how the Jews answered the questions that Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other men asked the Jewish elders. The Jews explained that they are the "God of heaven and earth's servants," which is a way to say that the Jews work for their God, the one who created heaven and earth. The Jews tell Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other leaders that the Jews are rebuilding the temple that a great Israelite king built many centuries before this story. The great king the Jews mention is King Solomon. The Jews do not say King Solomon's name because the Persians would not know who King Solomon was.

The Jews also explain to Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other leaders that the Jews made the God of heaven angry. Because the God of heaven was angry with the Jews, the God of heaven allowed King Nebuchadnezzar of Babylon to capture the Jews and destroy King Solomon's temple. In their letter, Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai point out that King Nebuchadnezzar was a Chaldean. The Chaldeans were a family of people who lived in the southern part of the Babylonian kingdom.

And yet, even though many terrible events happened to the Jews a few years before this story, King Cyrus of Babylon decided to let the Jews return to Jerusalem in the first year of his time ruling Babylon.

Stop here and tell the group a story about a time when someone gave you something you did not feel like you deserved or that you had wanted for a long time. How did that make you feel? Pause this audio here.

The Jews also point out that King Cyrus gave the Jews permission to rebuild the house of God. King Cyrus also gave all the gold and silver items that King Nebuchadnezzar had taken from God's first temple to a Jew named Sheshbazzar. Sheshbazzar worked as a governor like Tattenai. Sheshbazzar helped the Jews build God's new temple. It is also important to know that the temple in Babylon that Sheshbazzar took the special items from is not a temple for God. When King Nebuchadnezzar took the items from the house of God, Nebuchadnezzar put the items in a temple to one of the Babylonian gods. When Sheshbazzar took the items back to Israel, he had to take them out of the temple for the Babylonian gods.

To finish their message to King Darius, Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other officials ask King Darius to have his servants look for a record of King Cyrus' orders. The servants look for the record in a royal archive. A royal archive was a special place where a king would store important documents and histories of the kingdom so that the kings could remember the events that happened even before the king started ruling. King Darius wanted to find King Cyrus' orders so that he could make sure that the Jews were telling the truth. Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai ask King Darius to tell them what the king thinks they should do.

Stop here and discuss these questions as a group: In your culture, where do people go to understand information from the past? What people in your community are responsible for preserving the history of your culture? How do you keep this information safe?

Defining the Scenes
-------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 5:6–17 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will define the scenes, characters, and setting of this passage. Then the group will visualize this passage.

This passage has four scenes.

**In the first scene:** Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and their fellow workers sent a letter to report back to King Darius.

**In the second scene:** Tattenai and his fellow workers explain how the Jews are building God's temple. The men also tell King Darius that they have talked to the Jews to gather information from them as well.

**In the third scene:** Tattenai and his fellow workers tell King Darius what the Jews told Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai. The Jews explain how King Nebuchadnezzar destroyed God's first temple and how the Jews began to rebuild the new temple.

**In the fourth scene:** Tattenai and his fellow workers ask King Darius to make sure the Jews are telling the truth. The men also ask the king to tell the men what to do.

The characters in this passage are:

* Tattenai
* Shethar\-Bozenai
* The other officials
* The Jewish leaders
* King Darius
* King Nebuchadnezzar
* And King Cyrus

As a group, pay attention to these parts of the passage's setting:

In scene one, the storyteller starts by explaining that Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other Persian officials are Persian leaders who governed "Trans\-Euphrates," or the province beyond the River. Trans\-Euphrates included Judah and Jerusalem. The rest of the story is a copy of the message that Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other officials sent to King Darius of Persia. It is important to know that Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the others wrote the letter in a traditional, official way. This means that the men only told King Darius facts. Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the others do not give King Darius their feelings or opinions about the events they are talking about.

In scene two, the storyteller begins Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other officials' message to the king. The men start their message by greeting King Darius with the word "Peace." This is a very polite and formal phrase to say hello to King Darius because King Darius is an important person. Some people think that the phrase the men use could be a short version of a longer greeting people used to say hello during the time of this story. This greeting was a traditional way to greet someone officially and respectfully. When Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai use this greeting, they tell the king that they hope the king is doing well and has a peaceful rule.

Stop here and discuss as a group: How will you translate the greeting in this letter so that people will understand that the letter was written to an important person? Pause this audio here.

In scene three, Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other officials very carefully explain everything that the Jews have done in Jerusalem so far. The men first explain that they traveled to visit the place where the Jews are building the house of the great God, which is the temple. It is important to know that the Persian people did not believe that Yahweh, the God of the Jews, was the only God or that Yahweh was the most powerful God. The Persians probably called God's temple the "house of the great God" because they were trying to show that they respected God, the Jewish God, even if the Persians did not obey God the same way the Jews did.

Stop here and tell the group a story about a time when you did something you did not have to do to show respect to someone. How did that person respond? How will you translate the "house of the Great God" in a way that shows that the officials try to show respect to the Jewish God? Pause this audio here.

Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other officials explain how the Jews are building the temple. The men tell King Darius that the Jews are using large cut stones and logs of wood to build the temple walls. Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other officials notice that the Jews are working carefully and well and are making a lot of progress. After the Persian officials notice the Jews' progress on God's temple, they ask the Jews who gave the Jews permission to work on the temple, and they ask for the names of the people who are working on the project.

Stop here and tell a story about someone in your community who does their work very well and is very productive. Notice the words you use to describe this person and their work. Pause this audio here.

In scene four, Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other men tell King Darius the exact words the Jews used to answer the officials' questions. First, the Jews explained who they are. The Jews told Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other men that they are the servants of the God of heaven and earth. When the Jews called God the "God of heaven and earth," the Jews were saying that their God created the heavens and earth and that God rules over the heavens and earth. It is important to know that the Persians believed that their important god, Ahuramazda, created the heavens and earth.

Stop here and discuss as a group how you will translate the phrase "God of heaven and earth." Pause this audio here.

Next, the Jews explained that they are trying to rebuild God's temple, which a great Israelite king had built many years before this story. Then, the Jews explained that many years before this story, their families had made God angry. Because God was angry, God gave the Jews into the hands of King Nebuchadnezzar. In other words, God allowed King Nebuchadnezzar of Babylon to capture the Jews and destroy the temple.

Stop here and tell a story to the group. In this section of the story, there are many events that happen one after another. Tell a story about a time in your life when many things happened to you suddenly. Notice the words you use to explain the order of events. Pause this audio here.

Next, the Jews explained that in the first year of King Cyrus of Babylon's reign, King Cyrus commanded the Jews to rebuild God's temple. The Jews also pointed out that King Cyrus had commanded the governor Sheshbazzar to take all the important gold and silver "vessels" or articles that were in the first Jewish temple and bring them to Judah again. Many of the events Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai discuss in their letter to King Darius happen at the beginning of Ezra. You may want to refer back to the first stories in Ezra to remember everything that happened. When the Persian officials tell King Darius about what King Cyrus said, they use words that mean that King Cyrus commanded the Jews to rebuild the temple and take the important silver and gold items from the first temple back with them. This is important because when a king orders someone to do something, the king's words have more power than if the king just gave the person permission. The Jews were probably trying to make the Persian officials understand that the Persians should not stop the Jews from building God's temple.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: In your culture, what words do you use to tell someone you are ordering them to do something? How do you know when someone is telling you that you must do something and not asking you to do it? Pause this audio here.

Finally, the Jews who talked to Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other officials explained that the governor Sheshbazzar came to Jerusalem to build the temple as King Cyrus had commanded him to. In Jerusalem, Sheshbazzar helped to build the foundation of the temple on the same spot as the foundation for the first temple, just as King Cyrus had commanded him to. Remember, a foundation is the base of a building that keeps the building strong. The Jews told Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other men that the Jews had been working on the temple ever since then.

In scene five, Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other officials finish their message by giving recommendations to King Darius. The men ask the king respectfully when they say, "If it seems good to the king."

Stop here and discuss as a group how you will translate the phrase, "If it seems good to the king." In your culture, how would you respectfully ask a leader to do something? Pause this audio here.

Now that the Persian officials have told King Darius everything that has happened in Judah with the Jews, the men ask the king to make sure that the Jews are telling the truth. It is important to know that Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other men are not ordering King Darius to make sure that the Jews are telling the truth. Instead, the Persian officials are asking the king to look through the kingdom's important documents in the royal archive to make sure that King Cyrus' order exists. Remember, a royal archive is a place that a ruler would use to save important records of the history of the kingdom. Finally, Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other men finish the letter by asking King Darius to tell the men what to do.

Stop here and remember as a group how you talked about royal archives in previous passages. Pause this audio here.

Now, the group should storyboard, draw, or use objects to visualize the passage and the action in it.

Embodying the Text
------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 5:6–17 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will dramatize the passage.

This passage has four scenes.

The characters in this passage are:

* Tattenai
* Shethar\-Bozenai
* The other leaders
* The Jewish leaders
* King Darius
* King Nebachadnezzar
* And King Cyrus

As a group, act out the passage twice. You should act out the passage in your own language.

First, act out the passage without stopping. Pay attention to the dialogue, flow, plotline, and chronology of the passage. Make sure you do not skip acting out the difficult or important parts of the passage. Help each other remember every part of the passage.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage.

The group should act out this passage a second time. At certain points in the drama, pause the drama and ask the person playing the character, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person playing the character should answer what they would be feeling or thinking if they were that character. Then continue the drama.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage a second time.

The following is an example of the drama and possible responses.

In scene one, the people playing Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and their fellow workers begin writing a letter to King Darius. The men greet King Darius in their letter.

Pause the drama. Ask the people playing Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other men, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The people might answer things like:

* We do not want to make the king angry if we do the wrong thing. Let's message the king so he can tell us what to do; or
* I am happy to be doing my job. I want to make sure that the king has all the information he needs to run his kingdom smoothly.

Continue the drama.

In scene two, the people playing Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other men tell King Darius about their visit to Judah. Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the men explain that they visited the house of the great God and watched the Jews use big stones and wood logs to build the temple walls. Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other men also ask the Jewish leaders who gave the Jews permission to build the temple and ask for the names of the people working on the project.

Pause the drama. Ask the people playing Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other men, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The people might answer things like:

* I want to make sure the king has all the true facts about what happened here. I am proud that my job is to tell the king important information. I want to do my job well for the king; or
* If the Jews are going to be troublemakers when they finish the temple, we must stop the work now! The king needs to know all the facts to make a wise decision.

Continue the drama.

In scene three, the people playing Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other officials tell King Darius how the Jews responded to the men's questions. The men explain that the Jews talked about their first temple, how King Nebuchadnezzar captured the Jews because the Jews' God was angry with the Jews, and King Cyrus' order to rebuild God's temple. The Jews also tell how King Cyrus ordered Sheshbazzar to take the special gold and silver articles from Babylon back to God's temple.

Pause the drama. Ask the people playing Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other officials, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The people might answer things like:

* I had no idea the Jews experienced so many things! or
* It is good that I am telling the king about what the Jews are doing in Israel because they have experienced a lot of crazy events. I have no idea what the Jews will do when the temple is finished!

Ask the people playing the Jewish leaders, "What are you feeling or thinking? The people might answer things like:

* We cannot stop building God's temple. These Persian men must understand that they cannot stop us! King Cyrus himself has ordered us to finish this project; or
* I am so grateful that we have King Cyrus' protection for this project. King Cyrus made sure we could do so many important things.

Continue the drama.

In scene four, the people playing Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other officials ask King Darius to make sure that the Jews are telling the truth. The men suggest that King Darius have some of his servants search the royal archives to make sure that King Cyrus really did order the Jews to build the temple. Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other men also ask King Darius to tell them what to do with the Jews.

Pause the drama. Ask the people playing Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other men, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The people might answer things like:

* We have given the king all the information we can. We trust the king to make the best choice about how to treat the Jews; or
* The Jews could be lying so that they can build their temple and cause lots of trouble later. We must make sure that does not happen.

Ask the person playing King Darius, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* I am happy that Tattenai and Shethar\-Bozenai have done their job well. These men have given me all the information I need to make a good decision; or
* Hmmm, this is a hard situation, and I do not want to handle it wrong. It could cause lots of problems for me and my kingdom if I do!

Filling the Gaps
----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 5:6–17 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will discuss key terms and words in this passage.

Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other officials wrote King Darius a **letter**. Remember, a letter is a way some people send a message to others. One person will write words on paper or animal skin, and another person can read that message. Use the same word or phrase for letter as you used in previous passages.

Tattenai was a Persian **governor**. Remember, a governor is a special person who helps to run a kingdom. A governor is not as important as a king, but a governor will help the king rule small parts of the kingdom. Use the same word or phrase for governor as you used in previous passages.

Tattenai was a governor of a **province** that was west of the Euphrates River. A province is an area in a big kingdom. In the story, the storyteller is saying that Tattenai was a governor of an area called the **Trans\-Euphrates**, or the "province beyond the River." This province includes Jerusalem and all of Judah. Use the same word or phrase for Trans\-Euphrates as you used in previous passages.

Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and their **fellow workers** wrote a message to King Darius to tell the king about the Jews. Remember, when someone is a fellow worker, it means that they do a similar job to someone else. This means that Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other officials' fellow workers were people who also worked for King Darius but were likely less important. Use the same word or phrase for fellow workers as you did in previous passages.

Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other men wrote a message to Darius the **king** of Persia. A king is someone who rules over a group of people. Kings can rule over a lot of people or just a few people. Some kings, like King Darius, ruled over a lot of people over a very big area of land. For more information on king, refer to the Master Glossary. Use the same word or phrase for king as you used in previous passages.

Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other officials wrote a message to King Darius to give him a **report** about what the Jews were doing in the province of **Judah**. A report is a description of someone's experience at an event. Normally, reports are very official and formal. Use the same word or phrase for report and for Judah as you used in previous passages. For more information about Judah, refer to the Master Glossary.

Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other men tell King Darius that they traveled to Judah to visit the house of the great God. This **house of God** is another phrase for the temple. It is the place where God can meet his people on earth. For more information on "house of God," refer to the Master Glossary. Use the same word or phrase for "house of God" as you used in previous passages.

The Persian officials tell King Darius that they visited the house of the great **God**. In the Bible, God refers to God who is powerful and created the whole world. It is the same God who made promises to Abraham. God is also the same God that the Jews have worshipped for many centuries. For more information on God, refer to the Master Glossary. Use the same word or phrase for God as you used in previous passages.

Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other officials say that the Jews used timber to build God's temple walls. Timber is wood that someone has cut and prepared to use to build something.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **timber**. If you have already translated this word in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other officials explain that they talked with the Israelite **elders** about the Jews building God's temple. An elder is an important member of the community. Normally, elders are leaders in the community and are often the heads of important families. For more information on elders, refer to the Master Glossary. Use the same word or phrase for elder as you used in previous passages.

Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other officials asked the Israelite elders who gave them an **order** to build God's temple. An order is a command from an important leader, like a king. An order can be a way for a leader to give another person permission to do something. Use the same word or phrase for order as you used in previous passages.

The Jews tell Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other officials that they are **servants** of the God of heaven and earth. A servant is a person who does work for someone else. Servants may work in the house or in the fields of their master. Servants usually have a low position in the community. Use the same word or phrase for servant as you used in previous passages.

The Jews explain to Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other officials that the Jews are servants of the "God of heaven and earth." The God of heaven and earth is the same God who made promises to Abraham and whom the Jews have worshiped for many centuries. By saying the God of heaven and earth in this story, the Jews are emphasizing that God created everything and rules over everything with power. Remember, heaven is the place where God lives and is also another word for the skies! For more information on heaven, refer to the Master Glossary.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **God of heaven and earth**. If you have already translated this phrase in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

The Jews also tell Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other officials that a great king of **Israel** built God's first temple. In this story, Israel refers to the land that God had given to his special people, the descendants of Jacob, who were also called Israel. This great king built this temple when the people of Israel were united in one kingdom. For more information on Israel, refer to the Master Glossary. Use the same word or phrase for Israel that you used in previous passages.

The Jews explain that King Cyrus commanded Sheshbazzar to take the gold and silver **vessels**, or **articles**, back to God's temple that King Nebuchadnezzar took from Israel several years before this story. Vessels are cups, pots, and other kinds of containers. People often use vessels to hold things like water or other kinds of liquids. Use the same word or phrase for vessels, or articles, as you used in previous passages.

King Cyrus commanded the Jews to take God's special cups or articles from the **temple** in Babylon and put them in God's new temple in Jerusalem. A temple is a place people go to worship God. The temple in Babylon was a place to worship the false Babylonian gods. God's temple in Jerusalem was a temple to worship God, the one, true God. For more information on temple, refer to the Master Glossary. Use the same word or phrase for temple as you used in previous passages.

Sheshbazzar laid the **foundations** of the new temple. Remember, foundation refers to the base of a building. Use the same word or phrase for foundation as you used in previous passages.

Tattenai, Shethar\-Bozenai, and the other officials ask King Darius to search the **royal archives** for King Cyrus' order to let the Jews build their temple. Archives are often real places or buildings where people keep important information. The important information can often be stories about what has happened to the kingdom in the past. Some people will also keep valuable treasure or money in archives. A royal archive is an archive that belongs to a king or a kingdom.

Use the same word or phrase for archives as you used in Ezra 4:15\. Here, the storyteller used the word for "royal archives." The storyteller makes it clear that these are special archives that the king keeps his records of the history of his kingdom.

Speaking the Word
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of the passage in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this session, retell the passage, as a group, in your own language.

First, the whole group should practice telling the passage in your own language. Remember to include the things you decided in previous steps, like chronology, words, phrases, emotions, or anything else you discussed.

One group member should tell a few sentences of the passage and pause. Another group member should tell the next part of the passage and pause. Do this until the group has retold the whole passage. The group members can correct each other in this step.

Pause this audio here.

Next, each member of the group should tell the whole passage without interruption at least one time. A group member can use random objects, motions, or their storyboard to help them remember the passage.

Pause this audio here.

Next, the group can decide whose version of the passage you like the most. You may decide that you liked the way one group member told one scene but you liked the way a different group member told another scene.

Pause this audio here.

Finally, the group can put together a final version of the passage that everyone agrees on. Each group member should practice telling this final version of the passage.

* **Associated Passages:** Ezra 5:6-17
* **Associated Articles:** elder (ID: `eng-t43-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); heaven (ID: `eng-t63-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); Israel (ID: `eng-t74-v2@FIAKeyTerms`); Judea (ID: `eng-t81-v2@FIAKeyTerms`); servant (ID: `eng-t123-v3@FIAKeyTerms`); temple (ID: `eng-t138-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); house of God (ID: `eng-t183-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); God (ID: `eng-t189-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); king (ID: `eng-t226-v2@FIAKeyTerms`)

* **Associated ACAI Concepts:** House (ID: `realia:House`); Jerusalem (ID: `place:Jerusalem`); Babylon (ID: `place:Babylon`); Cyrus (ID: `person:Cyrus`); Nebuchadnezzar (ID: `person:Nebuchadnezzar`); Darius (ID: `person:Darius.2`); Zerubbabel (ID: `person:Zerubbabel.2`); Tattenai (ID: `person:Tattenai`); Shethar-bozenai (ID: `person:Shethar-bozenai`); Israel (ID: `person:Israel`); Chaldeans (ID: `group:Chaldea`); Euphrates River (ID: `place:Euphrates`); Judah (ID: `person:Judah.4`); Chaldea (ID: `place:Chaldea`); Money (ID: `realia:Money`); Hewn Stone (ID: `realia:HewnStone`); Foundation (ID: `realia:Foundation`)

## Ezra 7:1–10 (id: eng-ezr-p13-v1)

Hear and Heart
--------------

In this step, hear Ezra 7:1–10 and put it in your hearts.

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 7:1–10 three times, in three different translations, if possible.

Pause this audio here.

Now, as a group, discuss the following questions:

1. What do you like in this passage? Pause this audio here.
2. What do you not like or not understand in this passage? Pause this audio here.
3. What does this passage tell us about God? Pause this audio here.
4. What does this passage tell us about people? Pause this audio here.
5. How does this passage affect our daily lives? Pause this audio here.
6. Who do you know who needs to hear this passage?

Setting the Stage
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 7:1–10 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

This true story probably happens more than 50 years after the Israelites finished rebuilding the temple in Jerusalem. In this story, King Artaxerxes rules the kingdom of Persia. You will remember that the Israelites from Judah and Benjamin were exiles in Babylon, which was in the territory of Persia\[.]{.mark} Today, we call this region Iran and Iraq. In this story, an Israelite man, Ezra, lives in Babylon but wants to go to Jerusalem.

At the time of King Artaxerxes, Babylonia was a large area, like a province, in Persia. Babylonian people built many famous temples to local gods in Babylonia. Though the storyteller does not say this directly, we know that Ezra asks the king for permission to go to Jerusalem. Ezra probably also asks for other supplies from the king that Ezra would need in Jerusalem, but the storyteller does not tell us exactly what those things are.

Ezra descends from a long line of priests, including Seraiah. Seraiah was the last high priest in Judah before the king of Babylon captured the people of Judah, about 129 years before Ezra. Seraiah is the son, or descendant, of Azariah. Azariah is the son of Hilkiah. Hilkiah is the descendant of Shallum. Shallum is the descendant of Zadok. Zadok is the descendant of Ahitub. Ahitub is the descendant of Amariah. Amariah is the descendant of Meraioth. Meraioth is the descendant of Zerahiah. Zerahiah is the descendant of Uzzi. Uzzi is the descendant of Bukki. Bukki is the descendant of Abishua. Abishua is the descendant of Phinehas. Phinehas is the descendant of Eleazar. Eleazar is the descendant of Aaron. Aaron was Moses' brother and is the man Yahweh chose as the very first high priest, who led all the other priests. In other words, even though Ezra is not a high priest, Ezra has the authority to teach Yahweh's Law because Ezra is a descendant of Aaron.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: The storyteller tells about Ezra and Ezra's ancestors, all the way back to Aaron. If these were ancestors of someone in your culture, how would you tell someone about these ancestors? Pause this audio here.

How do people in your culture talk about an important ancestor like Aaron? How do people talk about the descendants of an important ancestor? How do people talk about the authority or respect that other people give to a person because of who that person's ancestor was? Pause this audio here.

Ezra teaches people about Yahweh's law. Ezra dedicates himself to carefully study the law that Yahweh, the God of Israel, had given to Yahweh's people through Moses, or the "Book of Moses."

Stop here and, as a group, talk about who in your culture can learn and teach religious rules or laws. How do people learn these rules? How do people teach these rules? How do you think this is similar to or different than what Ezra does? Pause this audio here.

King Artaxerxes lets Ezra do everything Ezra asks for permission from the king to do because Yahweh, Ezra's God, is with Ezra. In other words, Yahweh feels pleased with Ezra, so Yahweh helps Ezra.

Stop here and discuss as a group: If a man from a different country asked your country's leader to help the people of that man's nation, how do you think this leader would respond? How is this the same or different from how Artaxerxes responds to Ezra? Pause this audio here.

Other Israelites go with Ezra to Jerusalem. These people include priests, Levites, musicians, gatekeepers, and people who will serve in Yahweh's temple. These musicians are the Levites who make music for worship in the temple. The gatekeepers guard and protect the temple. The storyteller does not mention this, but probably family members, including the elderly, women, and children, likely travel with this group. Ezra and the Israelites leave Babylonia to go to Jerusalem on the first day of the first month of the seventh year when King Artaxerxes rules over Persia.

Ezra and the other Israelites travel for four months from Babylonia to Jerusalem. Ezra and the others arrive in Jerusalem on the first day of the fifth month of the seventh year, when King Artaxerxes rules over Persia. God helps Ezra during the whole trip. Ezra completely devotes himself to learning and obeying Yahweh's Law. Ezra even teaches Yahweh's statutes and ordinances to the Israelites. Ezra does this because Ezra wants to go back to Israel to continue to teach other Israelites about Yahweh. Yahweh helps Ezra because of Ezra's devotion.

In those days, people who traveled from Babylonia in Persia to Jerusalem had to go northwest along the Euphrates River and stay on routes closer to villages to avoid robbers. After many weeks, the travelers would have to turn south to travel through the wilderness and rocky hills. Finally, the travelers had to climb steep, uphill paths to reach Jerusalem. The entire journey would be almost 1,500 kilometers.

Stop here and, as a group, look at Persia, Babylonia, the Euphrates River, and Jerusalem on a map. Pause this audio here.

Stop here and, as a group, tell a story about when a group of people traveled a long distance safely.

Defining the Scenes
-------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 7:1–10 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will define the scenes, characters, and setting of this passage. Then the group will visualize this passage.

This passage has three scenes.

**In the first scene:** Ezra comes from a long family line of priests, all the way back to Aaron, the first high priest of Israel. This story is about Ezra's trip away from Babylon to Jerusalem. Ezra knows Yahweh's law very well and teaches Yahweh's law to other people.

**In the second scene:** The Persian king, Artaxerxes, gives Ezra everything Ezra asks for because Yahweh helps Ezra. Some other Israelites go with Ezra on the journey, including priests, Levites, singers, gatekeepers, and temple servants.

**In the third scene:** Yahweh helps Ezra and the other Israelites arrive safely in Jerusalem because Ezra works hard to learn, follow, and teach Yahweh's statutes and ordinances.

The characters in this passage are:

* Ezra
* Ezra's ancestors
* Yahweh
* King Artaxerxes
* And the Israelites who travel with Ezra: priests, Levites, singers, gatekeepers, and temple servants

The names in this story are similar to part of another story in 1 Chronicles. You should translate parts of this story the same way as you did in 1 Chronicles 6:3–15, except in places where there are differences.

Stop here and listen to your translation of 1 Chronicles 6:3–15 now, if your team has already translated this similar passage. Pause this audio here.

Scene one happens while King Artaxerxes rules Persia. Ezra comes from a long family line of priests. Ezra is the "son of," meaning "descendant of," Seraiah. The storyteller does not mention every priest in the family line and may skip some generations. So, "son of" means "descendant," which may or may not be the direct son. Seraiah is the son of Azariah, who is the son of Hilkiah. Hilkiah is the son of Shallum, who is the son of Zadok. Zadok is the son of Ahitub, who is the son of Amariah. Amariah is the son of Azariah, who is the son of Meraioth. Meraioth is the son of Zerahiah, who is the son of Uzzi. Uzzi is the son of Bukki, who is the son of Abishua. Abishua is the son of Phinehas, who is the son of Eleazar. Finally, Eleazar is the son of Aaron. Aaron was the first high priest. This means that Yahweh chose Aaron as the very first high priest who led all the other priests.

Stop here and discuss as a group how you will describe that each man is the descendant of his ancestor. Also, discuss in what order you will describe these ancestors so that someone in your culture will understand that Aaron is Ezra's first and most important ancestor. Pause this audio here.

Ezra prepares to leave Babylon to go to Jerusalem. Ezra is a scribe who "seeks" the law that Yahweh gave through Moses and teaches Yahweh's law to others. Scribes had the official responsibility among the Israelites to learn and teach Yahweh's law. Ezra probably learned Yahweh's law through writing the words down again and again until Ezra could quickly and accurately retell Yahweh's law. "Seeks" means that Ezra reads Yahweh's law to learn what Yahweh wants Ezra to do. Ezra can skillfully teach other Israelites about Yahweh's law in the Book of Moses. Here, the "Law of Moses" means that Ezra specifically studies the "Book of Moses," or the writings of Genesis, Exodus, Leviticus, Numbers, and Deuteronomy.

Stop here and discuss as a group how you will describe Yahweh's law in the Book of Moses and how Ezra studies Yahweh's law. Pause this audio here.

In scene two, Ezra asks for some things from King Artaxerxes of Persia. We do not know what Ezra asks for, but the king gives Ezra all of these things. The king does this because Yahweh's "hand is on" Ezra. This is special language that means Ezra's God, Yahweh, helps Ezra.

Stop here and discuss as a group how you will talk about Yahweh's hand being on Ezra. Pause this audio here.

Some of the other Israelites who live in Babylonia travel with Ezra. These Israelites include priests, Levites, musicians, gatekeepers, and temple servants. Ezra and the other Israelites travel to Jerusalem during the seventh year when King Artaxerxes rules Persia. Priests are responsible to serve God at the temple by offering sacrifices, teaching God's Law, and leading worship. Levites are descendants of Levi, the son of Jacob. The Levites are responsible for helping the priests in the temple. The singers are specific members of the tribe of Levi who have the responsibility to sing and play musical instruments in the temple. Gatekeepers are another specific group of Levites who are responsible to guard the gates of the temple. Temple servants are a group of men responsible to serve the Levites by doing chores in the temple.

Stop here and discuss as a group how you will talk about the people who travel with Ezra. Pause this audio here.

In scene three, Ezra leaves Babylonia on the first day of the first month of the seventh year when King Artaxerxes rules Persia. Ezra arrives safely in Jerusalem on the first day of the fifth month of the same year. This happens because Ezra's God's "good hand" is on Ezra because Ezra sets his heart to study Yahweh's law. In other words, Ezra devotes himself to study and follow Yahweh's law as well as to teach these statutes and ordinances to the Israelites. God's "good hand" means that God actively makes sure that Ezra and the others safely arrive in Jerusalem.

Stop here and discuss as a group how you will describe the time when Ezra starts the journey from Babylon to when Ezra arrives in Jerusalem. Pause this audio here.

Stop here and discuss as a group how you will talk about how Ezra "sets his heart," or decides to fully study Yahweh's law. Pause this audio here.

Now, the group should storyboard, draw, or use objects to visualize the passage and the action in it.

Embodying the Text
------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 7:1–10 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will dramatize the passage.

This passage has three scenes.

The characters in this passage are:

* Ezra
* Ezra's ancestors
* Yahweh
* King Artaxerxes
* And the Israelites who travel with Ezra: priests, Levites, singers, gatekeepers, and temple servants

As a group, act out the passage twice. You should act out the passage in your own language.

First, act out the passage without stopping. Pay attention to the dialogue, flow, plotline, and chronology of the passage. Make sure you do not skip acting out the difficult or important parts of the passage. Help each other remember every part of the passage.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage.

The group should act out this passage a second time. At certain points in the drama, pause the drama and ask the person playing the character, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person playing the character should answer what they would be feeling or thinking if they were that character. Then continue the drama.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage a second time.

The following is an example of the drama and possible responses.

Scene one happens while King Artaxerxes rules Persia. Ezra descends from a long line of priests, including Seraiah. Seraiah is the son of Azariah, who is the son of Hilkiah. Hilkiah is the son of Shallum, who is the son of Zadok. Zadok is the son of Ahitub, who is the son of Amariah. Amariah is the son of Azariah, who is the son of Meraioth. Meraioth is the son of Zerahiah, who is the son of Uzzi. Uzzi is the son of Bukki, who is the son of Abishua. Abishua is the son of Phinehas, who is the son of Eleazar. Finally, Eleazar is the son of Aaron. Aaron was the chief priest.

Ezra prepares to leave Babylon to go to Jerusalem. Ezra knows Yahweh's law in the Book of Moses very well and teaches this to the other Israelites.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing Ezra, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* God has trusted my family with God's law for a long time. I feel humbled because this role comes from Yahweh and not from my own power; or
* Yahweh does not forget Yahweh's people. Yahweh is faithful to keep his promises for every generation; or
* I want to know Yahweh through his word and teach Yahweh's law to other Israelites so we can obey Yahweh; or
* We have lived in captivity for generations. I want to return to Jerusalem and worship Yahweh in Yahweh's temple. I trust that Yahweh is good and will help me to go to Jerusalem.

> Continue the drama.

In scene two, King Artaxerxes of Persia gives Ezra all of the things Ezra asks for. The king does this because Ezra's God, Yahweh, helps Ezra. Some of the other Israelites who live in Babylonia travel with Ezra. These Israelites include priests, Levites, singers, gatekeepers, and temple servants. Ezra and the other Israelites travel to Jerusalem during the seventh year when King Artaxerxes rules Persia.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing King Artaxerxes, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* Ezra's God seems powerful and worthy of my honor; or
* Even though I am not an Israelite, I respect Ezra's God. To help Ezra is the right thing for me to do. I do not want to go against Ezra's powerful God; or
* I will give Ezra authority to lead a group of Israelites to go to Jerusalem.

Continue the drama.

In scene three, Ezra leaves Babylonia on the first day of the first month of the seventh year when King Artaxerxes rules Persia. Ezra arrives in Jerusalem on the first day of the fifth month of the same year. This happens because Ezra's God helps Ezra. God helps because Ezra devotes himself to study and follow Yahweh's law as well as to teach these statutes and ordinances to the Israelites.

Pause the drama. Ask the person or group playing the Israelites who travel with Ezra, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* We feel so thankful to Yahweh that Yahweh has safely brought us to Jerusalem. Yahweh guided us during the whole long and difficult journey; or
* Yahweh has honored Ezra's faithfulness to study, obey, and teach Yahweh's law; or
* We feel hopeful about the future because God helped us and cared for us to return to Jerusalem; or
* Now we can worship Yahweh in his temple again!

Filling the Gaps
----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 7:1–10 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will discuss key terms and words in this passage.

This story is about Ezra, who comes from a long family line of priests, including Aaron, whom Yahweh chose as the first high priest.

Stop here and decide how you will translate **high priest**. If you have already translated this in other books of the Bible, then use the same word or phrase here. For more information about high priests, refer to "high priest" in the Master Glossary. Pause this audio here.

The storyteller describes Ezra as the "**son**," or **descendant** of Seraiah. The storyteller also describes the other **priests** as the sons of other priests. Here, son usually means descendant. In the story, Ezra travels with some other Israelites: priests, **Levites**, singers, gatekeepers, and **temple** servants. For more information on priest, descendant, Levites, and temple, refer to the Master Glossary. Translate priest and temple as you have in previous passages. Translate son or descendant in the same way you have translated the idea of descendant before in Ezra.

Ezra was a **scribe**, or teacher of Yahweh's law. For more information on scribe, refer to the Master Glossary

**King** Artaxerxes rules Persia. For more information on king, refer to the Master Glossary.

The storyteller in this passage uses the personal name for **God**, "**Yahweh**," to refer to Ezra's God, who gave Yahweh's law to the Israelites in the **Law of Moses**. Ezra talks about Yahweh, the God of **Israel**. Use the same words for God, Yahweh, and the Law of Moses that you have used before. For more information on God, Yahweh, Israel, and the Law of Moses, refer to the Master Glossary.

The name Ezra means "help" and might come from a longer name that means "Yahweh helps."

Stop here and decide how you will translate the name "**Ezra**." Will you translate it according to the meaning, or will you translate it according to how it sounds?

Speaking the Word
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of the passage in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this session, retell the passage, as a group, in your own language.

First, the whole group should practice telling the passage in your own language. Remember to include the things you decided in previous steps, like chronology, words, phrases, emotions, or anything else you discussed.

One group member should tell a few sentences of the passage and pause. Another group member should tell the next part of the passage and pause. Do this until the group has retold the whole passage. The group members can correct each other in this step.

Pause this audio here.

Next, each member of the group should tell the whole passage without interruption at least one time. A group member can use random objects, motions, or their storyboard to help them remember the passage.

Pause this audio here.

Next, the group can decide whose version of the passage you like the most. You may decide that you liked the way one group member told one scene but you liked the way a different group member told another scene.

Pause this audio here.

Finally, the group can put together a final version of the passage that everyone agrees on. Each group member should practice telling this final version of the passage.

* **Associated Passages:** Ezra 7:1-10
* **Associated Articles:** Israel (ID: `eng-t74-v2@FIAKeyTerms`); law (ID: `eng-t83-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); priest (ID: `eng-t103-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); temple (ID: `eng-t138-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); scribe (ID: `eng-t155-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); Yahweh (ID: `eng-t174-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); God (ID: `eng-t189-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); king (ID: `eng-t226-v2@FIAKeyTerms`); descendant (ID: `eng-t234-v2@FIAKeyTerms`); high priest (ID: `eng-t246-v1@FIAKeyTerms`)

* **Associated ACAI Concepts:** Ezra (ID: `person:Ezra`); LORD (ID: `deity:Lord`); Jerusalem (ID: `place:Jerusalem`); Israel (ID: `person:Israel`); Babylon (ID: `place:Babylon`); Instruction (ID: `keyterm:Instruction`); Artaxerxes (ID: `person:Artaxerxes`); Amariah (ID: `person:Amariah.2`); Bukki (ID: `person:Bukki.2`); Uzzi (ID: `person:Uzzi`); Abishua (ID: `person:Abishua`); Ahitub (ID: `person:Ahitub.3`); Zerahiah (ID: `person:Zerahiah`); Shallum (ID: `person:Shallum.7`); Meraioth (ID: `person:Meraioth`); Azariah (ID: `person:Azariah.7`); Seraiah (ID: `person:Seraiah.2`); Azariah (ID: `person:Azariah.6`); Zadok (ID: `person:Zadok.4`); Persia (ID: `place:Persia`); Tribe of Levi (ID: `group:Levi`); Aaron (ID: `person:Aaron`); Hilkiah (ID: `person:Hilkiah.2`); Phinehas (ID: `person:Phinehas`); Eleazar (ID: `person:Eleazar.2`); Moses (ID: `person:Moses`); Levi (ID: `person:Levi.3`); Decree (ID: `keyterm:Decree`)

## Ezra 7:11–28 (id: eng-ezr-p14-v1)

Hear and Heart
--------------

In this step, hear Ezra 7:11–28 and put it in your hearts.

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 7:11–28 three times, in three different translations, if possible.

Pause this audio here.

Now, as a group, discuss the following questions:

1. What do you like in this passage? Pause this audio here.
2. What do you not like or not understand in this passage? Pause this audio here.
3. What does this passage tell us about God? Pause this audio here.
4. What does this passage tell us about people? Pause this audio here.
5. How does this passage affect our daily lives? Pause this audio here.
6. Who do you know who needs to hear this passage?

Setting the Stage
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 7:11–28 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In the last story, we heard that Ezra had asked the king to allow Ezra to go back to Judah. In this passage, we will hear the letter that King Artaxerxes wrote as an official response to what Ezra asked the king to do. In those days, a king wrote letters to the people who lived in the king's nation as a way to tell the people the king's orders or rules for a specific situation. By writing this letter, the king gave Ezra authority to go to Jerusalem with other Israelites.

The storyteller introduces the letter in Hebrew, the language of the Israelites. The introduction says that Ezra is a priest and scribe. Ezra is an educated man who knows Yahweh's commands and statutes for Israel very well.

The Hebrew introduction ends with, "This is a copy of what King Artaxerxes wrote in the letter the king had given to Ezra."

The next part of the story is what the king wrote in another language. This language is the one which Persians used in those days to write official government letters. In the beginning of the letter, King Artaxerxes writes in the Persian style to show that the king is the one who writes the letter and that he writes the letter to Ezra. The king also gives a polite greeting, so we know the king is writing both an official and a positive message.

Stop here and discuss as a group how someone in your culture can show how this is a message one person sends to another person. Also, how can you show that this letter is a positive message? Pause this audio here.

In this beginning part of the letter, King Artaxerxes refers to himself with the official title that Persian kings used at that time. This title shows that King Artaxerxes is the most important king. King Artaxerxes also calls Ezra by an official title to show that the king officially recognizes Ezra's authority as a priest and scribe of the Law of the God of heavens. In other words, the king gives Ezra permission to teach the Law of Ezra's God to the Israelites in Jerusalem.

Stop here and discuss as a group how people in your culture refer to people who have official roles. Pause this audio here.

King Artaxerxes tells Ezra that King Artaxerxes gives an order to allow any of the Israelites in King Artaxerxes' kingdom who want to go with Ezra to go to Jerusalem. This includes priests and Levites. Artaxerxes and his seven advisers tell Ezra that they want to send Ezra to see whether the Israelites who live in Judah and Jerusalem obey the Law of Ezra's God. Artaxerxes also says that Ezra knows this Law well.

Artaxerxes and his advisors tell Ezra to take the silver and gold Artaxerxes is giving to God. Artaxerxes tells Ezra to take any other silver and gold that Ezra can get from the land of Babylon. Also, Ezra must take the offerings that the people and priests choose to give for the temple of their God in Jerusalem. Artaxerxes says to use the money to buy bulls, rams, male lambs, grain offerings, and drink offerings to use as sacrifices on the altar of the temple of Ezra's God in Jerusalem. Bulls are male cattle, rams are male adult sheep, and lambs are young sheep. Grain offerings are the best quality flour, which a priest mixes with olive oil. A drink offering is wine that a priest pours on the altar.

Stop here and, if needed, look at images of bulls, rams, and male lambs as a group. Pause this audio here.

Artaxerxes gives Ezra and the other Jews permission to use the rest of the silver and gold in a way that pleases their God. Artaxerxes tells Ezra and the other Israelites to use the articles Artaxerxes gives them to worship God in God's temple. In addition to that, Artaxerxes tells Ezra and the Israelites to take any other items they need for God's temple from Artaxerxes' royal treasury.

Stop here and discuss as a group: In your culture, how do people give money or gifts for important purposes like worship, helping others, or community needs, and who do you trust to take care of those gifts? Why do you need to trust those people? Pause this audio here.

King Artaxerxes gives a strong order to the officials who control the treasure in the area west of the Euphrates River. The officials must obey right away because, in those days, everyone had to do everything the king said. The king tells the officials to give Ezra the priest anything Ezra asks for because the king recognizes Ezra as a trusted teacher of the Law of the God of heaven.

The king lists exact amounts of silver, wheat, wine, oil, and salt that the officials may give Ezra. The officials must give up to 3,400 kilograms of silver. The officials must give 10,000 kilograms of wheat. The officials must give 2,000 liters of wine. The officials must give 2,000 liters of olive oil. The officials must give as much salt as Ezra needs.

Stop here and look at an image of silver metal as a group. Pause this audio here.

Stop here and look at an image of wheat as a group. Pause this audio here.

Stop here and look at an image of wine as a group. Pause this audio here.

Stop here and look at an image of olive oil as a group. Pause this audio here.

Stop here and look at an image of salt as a group. Pause this audio here.

Artaxerxes also commands everyone to work hard to do whatever God wants for the temple of the God of heaven. Artaxerxes explains that he does not want this God to feel angry at Artaxerxes' kingdom or the kingdom of Artaxerxes' descendants. The king forbids his officials in the province of Trans\-Euphrates to collect taxes, tributes, or duty from the priests, Levites, singers, gatekeepers, temple servants, or anyone else who works at God's temple in Jerusalem.

King Artaxerxes commands Ezra to ask God for wisdom to choose judges and officials who will use justice to lead the Israelite people who live west of the Euphrates River who know Ezra's God's laws. Artaxerxes also tells Ezra to teach the people who do not know God's laws. King Artaxerxes orders punishment for anyone who disobeys God's Law or the king's law, and the punishment should match the wrong thing that person has done. For some people who disobey, their punishment is death. Other people who disobey must leave where they live and not come back. Officials must take the property away from others who disobey. Other people who disobey must go to prison.

After reading Artaxerxes's letter, Ezra speaks to the people of Israel in Hebrew. Ezra tells the people of Israel to thank Yahweh who has been the God of their people since long ago. Ezra explains that Yahweh is the One Who caused King Artaxerxes to honor Yahweh's temple in Jerusalem, as the king shows in his letter. Ezra says that Yahweh shows kindness to Ezra by causing the king and the king's advisers, and all of the king's powerful officials to show kindness to Ezra. Ezra says that Yahweh, Ezra's God, gave Ezra the courage to gather Israelite leaders to go to Jerusalem with Ezra.

Defining the Scenes
-------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 7:11–28 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will define the scenes, characters, and setting of this passage. Then the group will visualize this passage.

This passage has four scenes.

**In the first scene:** Ezra serves as a priest and scribe. King Artaxerxes writes a letter to Ezra.

**In the second scene:** The king allows any Israelites who want to go with Ezra, including priests and Levites, to return to Jerusalem. The king also gives gifts for the Israelites to use for worshipping God.

**In the third scene:** The king commands Persian officials in Trans\-Euphrates to support Ezra generously. The king gives Ezra authority to appoint judges and officials and to teach and judge by God's Law.

**In the fourth scene:** Ezra praises God and gathers leaders to return to Jerusalem.

The characters in this passage are:

* Ezra
* King Artaxerxes
* Yahweh, the God of heaven
* King Artaxerxes' advisers
* Persian treasury officials west of the Euphrates
* The Israelites, which includes:
* Priests
* Levites
* Judges and court officials
* And temple workers: musicians, gatekeepers, and servants

As a group, pay attention to these parts of the passage's setting:

In scene one, the storyteller says that this is a copy of what King Artaxerxes wrote in the letter the king had given to Ezra. Ezra is a priest of Yahweh and a scribe. Ezra is an educated man who knows Yahweh's commands and decrees for the Israelites very well.
