When the storyteller calls Ezra a scribe, he is showing that Ezra is a leader and teacher of Yahweh's law.

Artaxerxes, king of kings, writes to Ezra the priest and scribe of the Law of the God of heaven. Artaxerxes probably did not write the letter directly. More than likely, the king said the words and a scribe wrote these words in the letter.

Stop here and discuss how you will say how Artaxerxes writes this letter to Ezra. Pause this audio here.

When Artaxerxes calls himself "king of kings," Artaxerxes means that he is the highest authority in Persia.

Stop here and discuss as a group how you will say that Artaxerxes calls himself the "king of kings." Pause this audio here.

Artaxerxes uses the title "scribe" for Ezra to show that Artaxerxes recognizes Ezra's official authority as a scribe for the Israelites. Artaxerxes refers to Ezra's God as the "God of heaven." In those days, Israelites often talked to non\-Israelites about the Israelite "God of heaven." Heaven refers to where God lives. When Artaxerxes refers to God's commands and decrees, Artaxerxes means Yahweh's law in the first five books of the Bible: Genesis, Exodus, Leviticus, Numbers, and Deuteronomy.

In scene two, King Artaxerxes allows any of the Israelites in the kingdom of Persia who want to go with Ezra to go to Jerusalem. This includes priests and Levites. Artaxerxes and his seven advisors send Ezra to see whether the Israelites living in Judah and Jerusalem obey the Law of Ezra's God. Other Persian kings also had advisors, or important men who helped the Persian kings make decisions. Artaxerxes also says that Ezra has this law "in hand." Here, "hand" means "control." This either means that Ezra carries an actual physical copy of the law or that Ezra knows and understands the law well.

Stop here and discuss how you will say that Ezra has the Law. Pause this audio here.

In scene three, Artaxerxes and his advisors give Ezra silver and gold to worship the Israelite God. Artaxerxes is probably giving the silver and gold so that the Israelites will worship God in the temple in Jerusalem. Artaxerxes also allows Ezra to take any other silver and gold that Ezra can get from Babylon. Ezra must take the offerings that the Israelite people and priests choose to give to God for God's house, meaning God's temple, in Jerusalem. Artaxerxes says to use the money to buy bulls, rams, male lambs, grain offerings, and drink offerings to use as sacrifices on the altar of the house of Ezra's God in Jerusalem. Artaxerxes gives Ezra and the other Jews permission to use the rest of the silver and gold in a way that pleases their God. Artaxerxes tells Ezra and the other Israelites to use the articles Artaxerxes gives them to worship God in God's house. In addition to that, Artaxerxes tells Ezra and the Israelites to take any other items they need for God's house from Artaxerxes' royal treasury.

King Artaxerxes now talks directly to the Persian officials who rule people in the area west of the River. Remember that this area is the Province of Trans\-Euphrates, or the "province beyond the River." The king orders these officials to give Ezra the priest anything Ezra asks for because the king recognizes Ezra as a trusted scribe of the Law of the God of heaven. In those days, people said "the River" to refer to the Euphrates River.

Stop here and, if necessary, look at the Euphrates River on a map. Pause this audio here.

The king says the exact amounts of silver, wheat, wine, oil, and salt that the officials must give to Ezra. The officials must give up to 3,400 kilograms of silver. The officials must give 10,000 kilograms of wheat. The officials must give 2,000 liters of wine. The officials must give 2,000 liters of olive oil. The officials must give as much salt as Ezra needs.

Stop here and, as a group, describe the amounts for the silver, wheat, wine, and olive oil in your language. Pause this audio here.

Artaxerxes also commands all of these Persian officials to work hard to do whatever God wants for the house of the God of heavens. Artaxerxes explains that he does not want this God to feel angry at Artaxerxes' kingdom or the kingdom of Artaxerxes' descendants. The king forbids his officials from collecting taxes, tribute, or duty from the priests, Levites, singers, gatekeepers, house servants, or anyone else who works at God's house in Jerusalem.

Then King Artaxerxes speaks to Ezra. The king says that Ezra has wisdom from God in Ezra's hand. Here, "hand" again means "control." In other words, the king means that Ezra knows God and God's law so well that Ezra can use this skill and knowledge to make decisions.

Stop here and discuss as a group how you will describe how Ezra has wisdom from God in his hand. Pause this audio here.

King Artaxerxes tells Ezra to use this wisdom from God to choose Israelite judges and officials who will justly lead the people west of the Euphrates River who know Ezra's God's laws. Ezra must teach the people who do not know God's laws and punish anyone who disobeys God's law or King Artaxerxes' law. The punishment should match the crime that each person has done. These punishments could be that the rulers put the people to death. Or the punishment could be that they exile the person, or force the people to live somewhere else. The rulers could take away a person's property, or the rulers could put a person in prison.

Stop here and discuss as a group how you will describe these punishments. Also, discuss in what order you will say these punishments. Pause this audio here.

In scene four, Ezra tells the people of Israel to praise Yahweh, the God of their "forefathers," or their ancestors. Ezra explains that Yahweh is the one who gave King Artaxerxes the heart to honor Yahweh's house in Jerusalem. By "heart," Ezra means that Yahweh is the one who caused Artaxerxes to think or feel the desire inside himself to honor Yahweh in this way. Ezra tells the Israelites that Yahweh shows kindness to Ezra by causing the king and the king's advisers, and all of the king's powerful officials, to show kindness to Ezra. Ezra says that the "hand of Yahweh my God was on me." Remember that "hand" means control or authority. Here, this means that Yahweh helps Ezra to go to Jerusalem. In other words, Yahweh, Ezra's God, gave Ezra the courage to gather Israelite leaders to go to Jerusalem with Ezra.

Stop here and discuss as a group how you will describe how Yahweh helps Ezra. Pause this audio here.

Now, the group should storyboard, draw, or use objects to visualize the passage and the action in it.

Embodying the Text
------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 7:11–28 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will dramatize the passage.

This passage has four scenes.

The characters in this passage are:

* Ezra
* King Artaxerxes
* Yahweh, the God of Heaven
* King Artaxerxes' advisers
* Persian treasury officials west of the Euphrates
* The Israelites, which includes:
* Priests
* Levites
* Judges and court officials
* And temple workers: musicians, gatekeepers, and servants

As a group, act out the passage twice. You should act out the passage in your own language.

First, act out the passage without stopping. Pay attention to the dialogue, flow, plotline, and chronology of the passage. Make sure you do not skip acting out the difficult or important parts of the passage. Help each other remember every part of the passage.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage.

The group should act out this passage a second time. At certain points in the drama, pause the drama and ask the person playing the character, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person playing the character should answer what they would be feeling or thinking if they were that character. Then continue the drama.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage a second time.

The following is an example of the drama and possible responses.

Scene one introduces the letter King Artaxerxes gave to Ezra. Ezra is a priest and scribe, educated in Yahweh's laws. The king refers to himself as the highest authority in Persia, acknowledges Ezra's authority over the Israelites, and recognizes Ezra's God as the God of heaven.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing King Artaxerxes, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* I am the highest authority in Persia. I respect Ezra and Ezra's God; or
* I believe Ezra's God has real power. I want peace and safety for my kingdom and my descendants, so I order my servants to fully obey me. When I honor Ezra's God, I protect my kingdom and show wisdom; or
* I will use my authority to protect the priests, Levites, musicians, gatekeepers, and servants. When I give this protection, no official may argue. I honor God's temple and expect my servants to respect it too.

Continue the drama.

In scene two, Artaxerxes allows any Israelites in Persia to go with Ezra to Jerusalem, including priests and Levites. Ezra keeps the Law of God with Ezra.

Pause the drama. Ask the group playing the Israelites, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The group might answer things like:

* We feel hopeful to go to Jerusalem. We will trust Yahweh, who has faithfully taken care of our people for generations and is now allowing us to return to Jerusalem! or
* Yahweh can even work through rulers of other nations! or
* God's favor on Ezra helps Ezra to teach and lead. We will follow Ezra; or
* Ezra trusts Yahweh's ways. We must also trust Yahweh to protect and guide us; or
* Yahweh blesses and provides for his work when Yahweh's servants are faithful to Yahweh.

Continue the drama.

In scene three, Artaxerxes and his advisors generously give Ezra silver and gold for the Israelite God. The Israelites who live in Jerusalem will use this to worship God in the temple in Jerusalem. Artaxerxes tells Ezra to use the money to buy bulls, rams, male lambs, grain offerings, and drink offerings to use as sacrifices on the altar of the temple of Ezra's God in Jerusalem. In addition to that, Artaxerxes tells Ezra and the Israelites to take any other items they need for God's temple from Artaxerxes' royal treasury.

King Artaxerxes tells the officials west of the Euphrates River to give Ezra the priest anything Ezra asks for because the king recognizes Ezra as a trusted teacher of the Law of the God of heaven. The king tells the officials to give Ezra silver, wheat, wine, oil, and salt. Artaxerxes does not want Ezra's God to feel angry at Artaxerxes or Artaxerxes' descendants. The king forbids his officials from collecting taxes, duties, or tributes from the priests, Levites, singers, gatekeepers, temple servants, or anyone else who works at God's temple in Jerusalem.

King Artaxerxes tells Ezra to ask God for wisdom to choose fair leaders. Ezra must teach God's laws to the people. The king will punish anyone who disobeys God's law or the king's law, and the punishment will match the wrong thing that the person did.

Pause the drama. Ask the group playing the officials in Persia, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The group might answer things like:

* Because King Artaxerxes honors Ezra's God, the king is protecting our people and kingdom; or
* Ezra's God must truly be a powerful God for King Artaxerxes to freely give so generously to this God's temple; or
* We respect the king and will obey the king.

Continue the drama.

In scene four, Ezra tells the Israelites to praise Yahweh. Ezra explains that God gave King Artaxerxes the heart to honor the temple. Ezra emphasizes how God's kindness gives Ezra courage.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing Ezra, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* God causes even foreign kings' decisions to fulfill God's purposes. God made the king and all his officials show kindness to me; or

* I feel deeply thankful to God; or
* God is faithful and kind to our people, from the time of our ancestors and even now in our time; or
* I feel confident to go to Jerusalem. God shows us God's power and kindness. God is guiding us to obey God; or
* God gives me the courage to bring Israel's leaders and travel safely to Jerusalem. I will teach God's law, help the people follow the law, and choose leaders who are fair. I will correct anyone who does wrong and ask God for wisdom in every decision; or
* Yahweh is strong and kind. Yahweh blesses those who trust him. I will serve Yahweh and honor Yahweh completely.

Filling the Gaps
----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 7:11–28 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will discuss key terms and words in this passage.

This story is **King** Artaxerxes' letter to Ezra. This is a copy of Artaxerxes' letter or **order**. Translate king and order in the same way as you did previously. King is in the Master Glossary.

Ezra is the **priest** and **scribe** of the "**Law** of the **God** of **heaven**." Ezra knows **Yahweh's** **commands** and **statutes** for **Israel**. The "Law of the God of heaven" refers to Yahweh's law in the Book of Moses. For more information on priest, scribe, law, heaven, God, Yahweh, commands and statutes, and Israel, refer to the Master Glossary. Translate these words as you have previously in the book of Ezra.

Stop here and discuss how you will say, "God of heaven." Translate this title in the same way you did in Ezra 1:2\. Pause this audio here.

The king orders that the **Israelites** in his **kingdom**, including the **Levites** and priests, can go to Jerusalem if they want to. Translate Israelites, kingdom, and Levites in the same way that you have previously, and refer to Israelites, kingdom, and Levites in the Master Glossary for more information.

The king gives Ezra many gifts to worship the God of heavens in **God's house**. Here, house refers to Yahweh's **temple**. For more information on "house of God" and temple, refer to the Master Glossary. Use the same word for "house of God," or temple, that you have used before.

The king tells Ezra to use the money for three kinds of offerings: **freewill offerings**, **grain offerings**, and **drink offerings**. People use the finest and best quality flour to give a "grain offering." This means that when a person grinds grain, this is the grain that takes the longest time to grind. To make the grain or food offering, the person mixes this flour with olive oil. Then a priest takes a handful of the grain offering and burns it on the altar, so the smoke has a nice smell when the priest burns the flour on the altar. A drink offering is wine that a priest pours on the **altar**. For more information on freewill offerings, grain offerings, drink offerings, **sacrifice**, and altar, refer to the Master Glossary. Translate freewill offerings, sacrifice, and altar in the same way you have previously.

Stop here and discuss how you will talk about the grain offering and drink offering. Pause this audio here.

King Artaxerxes tells his officials that they should give up to 100 **talents** of silver, 100 **cors** of wheat, and 100 **baths** of **wine** and olive oil to the Israelites. Talents, cors, and baths were units of measurement. A talent was around 34 kilograms, a cor was around 100 kilograms, and a bath was around 20 liters. The officials must give up to 3,400 kilograms of silver. The officials must give 10,000 kilograms of wheat. The officials must give 2,000 liters of wine. The officials must give 2,000 liters of olive oil. Translate wine in the same way you have previously. Wine is in the Master Glossary.

Stop here and discuss as a group how you will talk about these measurements. For more information about **talent**, refer to the Master Glossary. Will you use metric units of weight? Pause this audio here.

In the story, Ezra travels with some other Israelites: priests, **Levites**, **musicians**, **gatekeepers**, and temple **servants**. For more information on servants, refer to the Master Glossary. Translate Levites, musicians, gatekeepers, and temple servants in the same way you did in previous passages.

King Artaxerxes does not want God to feel **wrath**, or strong anger, towards Artaxerxes or his "sons," meaning **descendants**. Translate descendants in the same way as you have in previous passages. For more information on wrath and descendants, refer to the Master Glossary.

Stop here and discuss how you will talk about "wrath." Pause this audio here.

King Artaxerxes tells the treasurers of the **royal treasury** in the **Trans\-Euphrates** not to force the priests, Levites, musicians, gatekeepers, temple servants, or other workers at the house of God to pay **tax**, **tribute**, or **duty**. Taxes are regular payments to the ruler. Tributes are gifts or payments to show respect to a powerful king. Duties are fees that people pay at city gates or trading places. Translate royal treasury, "province of Trans\-Euphrates," taxes, tribute, and duty in the same way as you did previously.

Ezra has wisdom from God. When someone has wisdom, they can make good decisions that lead to good results.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **wisdom**. For more information on wisdom, refer to the Master Glossary. Pause this audio here.

King Artaxerxes tells Ezra to appoint magistrates and **judges** who know Yahweh's law to judge, or rule, the Israelites in the Trans\-Euphrates region. In those days, magistrates and judges meant almost the same thing. In Persia, these words may refer to two different types of judges. The magistrates are the type of judges who handle everyday problems. The other type of judge handles problems that involve the king and the government. For more information on judges, refer to the Master Glossary.

Stop here and discuss how you will talk about the **magistrates** and judges. Pause this audio here.

Ezra **blesses** or praises Yahweh for causing the **heart** of the king to "glorify," meaning honor, Yahweh's temple. The heart refers to the person's thoughts, feelings, and will. Use the same word for heart and bless or praise as you used in previous passages. For more information on heart and bless, refer to the Master Glossary.

Ezra also thanks Yahweh for Yahweh's **steadfast love** or loving\-kindness. When Yahweh loves someone steadfastly, Yahweh always shows that person kindness and mercy. Look up steadfast love in the Master Glossary for more information. Translate steadfast love in the same way as you have in previous passages.

Ezra says he has courage because Yahweh, Ezra's God, had Yahweh's hand on Ezra. Here, hand refers to Yahweh's power.

Speaking the Word
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of the passage in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this session, retell the passage, as a group, in your own language.

First, the whole group should practice telling the passage in your own language. Remember to include the things you decided in previous steps, like chronology, words, phrases, emotions, or anything else you discussed.

One group member should tell a few sentences of the passage and pause. Another group member should tell the next part of the passage and pause. Do this until the group has retold the whole passage. The group members can correct each other in this step.

Pause this audio here.

Next, each member of the group should tell the whole passage without interruption at least one time. A group member can use random objects, motions, or their storyboard to help them remember the passage.

Pause this audio here.

Next, the group can decide whose version of the passage you like the most. You may decide that you liked the way one group member told one scene but you liked the way a different group member told another scene.

Pause this audio here.

Finally, the group can put together a final version of the passage that everyone agrees on. Each group member should practice telling this final version of the passage.

## Ezra 8:1–14 (id: eng-ezr-p15-v1)

Hear and Heart
--------------

In this step, hear Ezra 8:1–14 and put it in your hearts.

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 8:1–14 three times, in three different translations, if possible.

Pause this audio here.

Now, as a group, discuss the following questions:

1. What do you like in this passage? Pause this audio here.
2. What do you not like or not understand in this passage? Pause this audio here.
3. What does this passage tell us about God? Pause this audio here.
4. What does this passage tell us about people? Pause this audio here.
5. How does this passage affect our daily lives? Pause this audio here.
6. Who do you know who needs to hear this passage?

Setting the Stage
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 8:1–14 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

The family names in this story are the same as the names in Ezra 2, but in a different order. Translate the names the same as you did in the previous story, but follow the order of the names in this story.

Stop here and listen to the names in your translation of Ezra 2:3–20\. Pause this audio here.

In this story, Ezra describes the people who traveled with Ezra from Babylon to Jerusalem. Ezra describes these people according to the name of each common ancestor, whose name is also the name of the large family group or clan that descends from that ancestor. For each clan, Ezra says the names of the family leader. For most of these family leaders, Ezra also mentions the family leader's father's name. Then Ezra says who travels with that family leader from Babylon to Jerusalem during the time when King Artaxerxes rules.

Stop here and discuss the ways people in your culture talk about and name large family groups. Pause this audio here.

The first two people whom Ezra says travel with Ezra are Gershom and Daniel. Gershom and Daniel are both descendants of Aaron, the first priest of the Israelites. Gershom descends from Phinehas, the son of Aaron's third son Eleazar, and Daniel descends from Ithamar, Aaron's fourth son. The family group, or clan, that descends from Phinehas uses the name "Phinehas" for their clan. The clan that descends from Ithamar uses the name "Ithamar" for their clan. Both Gershom and Daniel come from family lines of priests, and so these men are also priests.

The next person whom Ezra says travels with Ezra is Hattush. Hattush is a descendant of Shecaniah, who is a descendant of King David. Even though Hattush is a descendant of King David, Hattush does not have any power or authority in the Israelite community because the Persians still have power over the Israelites.

Then Ezra tells about 12 other clans who travel with Ezra to Jerusalem. For each of these clans, David first mentions their ancestor's name and the name of their family leader and the number of men who came with the family leader to Jerusalem. For most of these family leaders, Ezra also says the name of the family leader's father. In those days, the Israelites often said a man's name and his father's name to call him the son of his father. The Israelites probably did this to tell the difference between men who had the same name, but whose fathers probably had different names. The Israelites also did this to show which family and tribe a man came from.

These fathers or clan leaders may or may not still be alive. Women and children probably also travel with this group of Israelites to Jerusalem. Ezra mentions only the men to show the family ancestry and the people responsible to lead their clan during the journey to Jerusalem.

Stop here and discuss the ways people keep records of family groups in your culture. Whose names do you include or not include? For what reasons would you include or not include a name? How is this the same or different than how Ezra lists the Israelites here? Pause this audio here.

The first of these clan ancestors is Parosh. From Parosh's clan, Zechariah leads 150 men who officially recorded their names as part of the Parosh clan.

The next clan ancestor is Pahath\-Moab. From Pahath\-Moab's clan, Eliehoenai, the son of Zerahiah, leads 200 men.

From Zattu's clan, Shecaniah, the son of Jahaziel, leads 300 men.

From Adin's clan, Ebed, the son of Jonathan, leads 50 men.

From Elam's clan, Jeshaiah, the son of Athaliah, leads 70 men.

From Shephatiah's clan, Zebadiah, the son of Michael, leads 80 men.

From Joab's clan, Obadiah, the son of Jehiel, leads 218 men.

From Bani's clan, Shelomith, the son of Josiphiah, leads 160 men.

From Bebai's clan, Zechariah, the son of Bebai, leads 28 men.

From Azgad's clan, Johanan, the son of Hakkatan, leads 110 men.

From Adonikam's clan, Eliphelet, Jeuel, and Shemaiah lead 60 men. Ezra describes Eliphelet, Jeuel, and Shemaiah as "last." This might mean that these men were the last men to arrive to go to Jerusalem. Or, more likely, this might mean that these men are the last men from Adonikam's clan to return to Jerusalem.

From Bigvai's clan, Uthai and Zaccur lead 70 men.

Defining the Scenes
-------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 8:1–14 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will define the scenes, characters, and setting of this passage. Then the group will visualize this passage.

This passage has one scene.

**In the first scene:** Ezra lists the leaders of the families who are going to Jerusalem from Babylon during King Artaxerxes' reign. Ezra mentions each family according to the name of their ancestor. Each family leader represents a larger group of people who travel together.

The characters in this passage are:

* Ezra
* Gershom, who is a descendant of Phinehas
* Daniel, who is a descendant of Ithamar
* Hattush, who is a descendant of David and Shecaniah
* Zechariah, who is a descendant of Parosh, with 150 men from the clan of Parosh
* Eliehoenai, son of Zerahiah, who is a descendant of Pahath\-Moab, with 200 men from the clan of Pahath\-Moab
* Shecaniah, son of Jahaziel, who is a descendant of Zattu, with 300 men from the clan of Zattu
* Ebed, son of Jonathan, who is a descendant of Adin, with 50 men from the clan of Adin
* Jeshaiah, son of Athaliah, who is a descendant of Elam, with 70 men from the clan of Elam
* Zebadiah, son of Michael, who is a descendant of Shephatiah, with 80 men from the clan of Shephatiah
* Obadiah, son of Jehiel, who is a descendant of Joab, with 218 men from the clan of Joab
* Shelomith, son of Josiphiah, who is a descendant of Bani, with 160 men from the clan of Bani
* Zechariah, son of Bebai, who is a descendant of Bebai, with 28 men from the clan of Bebai
* Johanan, son of Hakkatan, who is a descendant of Azgad, with 110 men from the clan of Azgad
* Eliphelet, Jeuel, and Shemaiah, who are descendants of Adonikam, with 60 men from the clan of Adonikam
* And Uthai and Zakkur, who are descendants of Bigvai, with 70 men from the clan of Bigvai

As a group, pay attention to these parts of the passage's setting:

Ezra tells this story directly in his own words after he arrives in Jerusalem. Ezra lists the leaders of the families who returned from Babylon during King Artaxerxes' reign. Ezra mentions each family according to the name of their ancestor. Each family "head" or leader represents a larger group of people traveling together.

The first ancestor Ezra mentions is Phinehas. The "son of" Phinehas is Gershom. Here, "son of" means descendant. Gershom travels with Ezra. Ithamar's descendant who travels with Ezra is Daniel. David's descendant is Hattush, the son of Shecaniah. This means that Hattush descends from David through Shecaniah.

Stop here and discuss how you will describe in your language how Hattush descends from David through Shecaniah. Pause this audio here.

Then Ezra tells about 12 other clans who travel with Ezra to Jerusalem. The men from these clans do not have responsibilities as priests and do not descend from David; these men are just ordinary Israelites. The first clan ancestor Ezra mentions is Parosh. From Parosh's clan, Zechariah leads 150 men who officially recorded their names as part of the Parosh clan.

The next clan ancestor is Pahath\-Moab. From Pahath\-Moab's clan, Eliehoenai, the son of Zerahiah, leads 200 men. Pahath\-Moab means the "governor of Moab."

From Zattu's clan, Shecaniah, the son of Jahaziel, leads 300 men. We know from other stories that Zattu is Shecaniah's ancestor. However, Ezra does not actually mention Zattu's name here, but some Bible translations include Zattu's name.

Stop here and discuss whether or not you will include Zattu's name here. Pause this audio here.

From Adin's clan, Ebed, the son of Jonathan, leads 50 men.

From Elam's clan, Jeshaiah, the son of Athaliah, leads 70 men.

From Shephatiah's clan, Zebadiah, the son of Michael, leads 80 men.

From Joab's clan, Obadiah, the son of Jehiel, leads 218 men.

From Bani's clan, Shelomith, the son of Josiphiah, leads 160 men. We know from other stories that Bani is Shelomith's ancestor. However, Ezra does not actually mention Bani's name here. But some Bible translations include Bani's name.

Stop here and discuss whether or not you will include Bani's name here. Pause this audio here.

From Bebai's clan, Zechariah, the son of Bebai, leads 28 men.

From Azgad's clan, Johanan, the son of Hakkatan, leads 110 men.

From Adonikam's clan, Eliphelet, Jeuel, and Shemaiah lead 60 men. Ezra describes Eliphelet, Jeuel, and Shemaiah as "last." This might mean that these men were the last men to arrive to go to Jerusalem. Or, more likely, this might mean that these men are the last men from Adonikam's clan to return to Jerusalem.

From Bigvai's clan, Uthai and Zakkur lead 70 men.

Now, the group should storyboard, draw, or use objects to visualize the passage and the action in it.

Embodying the Text
------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 8:1–14 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will dramatize the passage.

This passage has one scene.

The characters in this passage are:

* Ezra
* Gershom, who is a descendant of Phinehas
* Daniel, who is a descendant of Ithamar
* Hattush, who is a descendant of David and Shecaniah
* Zechariah, who is a descendant of Parosh, with 150 men from the clan of Parosh
* Eliehoenai, son of Zerahiah, who is a descendant of Pahath\-Moab, with 200 men from the clan of Pahath\-Moab
* Shecaniah, son of Jahaziel, who is a descendant of Zattu, with 300 men from the clan of Zattu
* Ebed, son of Jonathan, who is a descendant of Adin, with 50 men from the clan of Adin
* Jeshaiah, son of Athaliah, who is a descendant of Elam, with 70 men from the clan of Elam
* Zebadiah, son of Michael, who is a descendant of Shephatiah, with 80 men from the clan of Shephatiah
* Obadiah, son of Jehiel, who is a descendant of Joab, with 218 men from the clan of Joab
* Shelomith, son of Josiphiah, who is a descendant of Bani, with 160 men from the clan of Bani
* Zechariah, son of Bebai, who is a descendant of Bebai, with 28 men from the clan of Bebai
* Johanan, son of Hakkatan, who is a descendant of Azgad, with 110 men from the clan of Azgad
* Eliphelet, Jeuel, and Shemaiah, who are descendants of Adonikam, with 60 men from the clan of Adonikam
* And Uthai and Zakkur, who are descendants of Bigvai, with 70 men from the clan of Bigvai

As a group, act out the passage twice. You should act out the passage in your own language.

First, act out the passage without stopping. Pay attention to the dialogue, flow, plotline, and chronology of the passage. Make sure you do not skip acting out the difficult or important parts of the passage. Help each other remember every part of the passage.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage.

The group should act out this passage a second time. At certain points in the drama, pause the drama and ask the person playing the character, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person playing the character should answer what they would be feeling or thinking if they were that character. Then continue the drama.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage a second time.

The following is an example of the drama and possible responses.

During King Artaxerxes' time as king, Ezra gathers Israelite clan leaders to go with Ezra to Jerusalem. Each of the clan leaders leads other members of their clan on the journey. Ezra lists the clan leaders who went with him to Jerusalem.

Pause the drama. Ask the group playing the clan leaders, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* We feel grateful to Yahweh to see so many families ready to go back to Jerusalem; or
* I feel excited. I think we will see Jerusalem soon; or
* I must help my clan stay safe; or
* God will guide us and keep us safe on the journey to Jerusalem; or
* We respect Ezra because Ezra follows Yahweh. We will follow Ezra.

Ask the group playing Ezra, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* I am grateful to have people help me lead all these people; or
* I need help to remember all the details as we prepare and pack to leave.

Filling the Gaps
----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 8:1–14 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will discuss key terms and words in this passage.

This story happens while **King** Artaxerxes rules Persia. For more information on king, see the Master Glossary.

Ezra gathers Israelites to travel with Ezra to Jerusalem. The following names include the names of priests, a "son" or **descendant** of King David, the ancestor from whom each clan receives its name, the names of the current leaders, and the names of most of these leaders' fathers. These leaders are the heads of fathers' **households**. Translate "heads of fathers' households" in the same way as the same title in Ezra 1:5\. Also, translate son when it means "descendants" in the same way as you did previously. For more information, refer to descendants and "household" in the Master Glossary.

Ezra gives the names of the fathers of Eliehoenai, Shecanaiah, Ebed, Jeshaiah, Zebadiah, Obadiah, Shelomith, Zechariah, and Johanan. Ezra calls these men the **sons** of their fathers. In these cases, sons means the male child of their father. Use the same word for son when it means male child as you have used previously, and refer to son in the Master Glossary for more information.

Speaking the Word
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of the passage in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this session, retell the passage, as a group, in your own language.

First, the whole group should practice telling the passage in your own language. Remember to include the things you decided in previous steps, like chronology, words, phrases, emotions, or anything else you discussed.

One group member should tell a few sentences of the passage and pause. Another group member should tell the next part of the passage and pause. Do this until the group has retold the whole passage. The group members can correct each other in this step.

Pause this audio here.

Next, each member of the group should tell the whole passage without interruption at least one time. A group member can use random objects, motions, or their storyboard to help them remember the passage.

Pause this audio here.

Next, the group can decide whose version of the passage you like the most. You may decide that you liked the way one group member told one scene but you liked the way a different group member told another scene.

Pause this audio here.

Finally, the group can put together a final version of the passage that everyone agrees on. Each group member should practice telling this final version of the passage.

## Ezra 8:15–23 (id: eng-ezr-p16-v1)

Hear and Heart
--------------

In this step, hear Ezra 8:15–23 and put it in your hearts.

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 8:15–23 three times, in three different translations, if possible.

Pause this audio here.

Now, as a group, discuss the following questions:

1. What do you like in this passage? Pause this audio here.
2. What do you not like or not understand in this passage? Pause this audio here.
3. What does this passage tell us about God? Pause this audio here.
4. What does this passage tell us about people? Pause this audio here.
5. How does this passage affect our daily lives? Pause this audio here.
6. Who do you know who needs to hear this passage?

Setting the Stage
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 8:15–23 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this story, the storyteller continues the previous story about the Israelite people whom Ezra gathered to go to Jerusalem.

In this story, Ezra says that he assembles these Israelites at the river that flows in the direction toward Ahava. This might be a canal. A canal is a long, narrow waterway that people dig to carry boats from one place to another or to carry water to farmers' fields.

Stop here to look at an image of a canal. Pause this audio here.

Ezra and the other Israelites make a camp by that canal for three days. This means that the people set up tents in that area. Tents are temporary places to live. The Israelites stay here before they move to the next location on their journey to Jerusalem.

Stop here to look at an image of a tent. Pause this audio here.

Ezra checks the people and priests whom Ezra gathered and realizes that none of these people are Levites. So, Ezra calls for Eliezer, Ariel, Shemaiah, Elnathan, Jarib, another Elnathan, Nathan, Zechariah, and Meshullam, who are all leaders. Ezra also calls for Joiarib and another Elnathan, who have a lot of knowledge. Ezra tells this group of men to go meet Iddo, who is the leader in Casiphia, along with the other Levites who are temple servants there. Ezra's messengers ask the Levites in Casiphia to send servants for God's temple. Casiphia is near Babylon.

Ezra says that, because God is good to Ezra and the others, the messengers bring Sherebiah back with them. Ezra describes Sherebiah as a capable man, a descendant of Mahli, the son of Levi, the son of Israel. Here, "Israel" refers to Jacob. Eighteen of Sherebiah's sons and brothers also come with Sherebiah.

Other Levites, Hashabiah and Jeshaiah, both descendants of Merari, also come with 20 of their brothers and sons to help Ezra.

The messengers also bring back 220 temple servants. These are the descendants of the group of temple servants whom David and David's officials assigned to help the Levites. People officially record the names of all of these 220 temple servants.

At the Ahava River, Ezra tells everyone to fast. To fast means not to eat food for a short time and to instead use that time to pray for a specific thing. Ezra starts this fast so that the Israelites will act humbly toward God and ask God for a safe journey for all the adults, their children, and their possessions. Ezra does not want to ask the king to provide his army and horsemen to protect Ezra and the Israelites from thieves on the road. In other words, Ezra would have felt shame if Ezra asked for help from the king because the king might think that Ezra did not really trust Ezra's God to protect Ezra. Horsemen are both the soldier who rides a horse and the horse that the soldier rides on.

Stop here to look at an image of a horse. Pause this audio here.

Then Ezra quotes the words that he had told the king before the events of this story. Ezra had told the king that God is good to everyone who follows God and that God is very angry at anyone who leaves God. Ezra means that God acts kindly toward the people who trust God. God also acts harshly toward the people who choose to turn away from God. So, Ezra and the other Israelites fast and ask God for protection. Ezra tells us that God answered the Israelites' prayer. You will remember that the storyteller is telling the story after Ezra and the people went back to Judah and Jerusalem, so here, Ezra is telling this part of the story after the Israelites returned to Jerusalem and Judah. God has protected the Israelites on their journey!

Defining the Scenes
-------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 8:15–23 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will define the scenes, characters, and setting of this passage. Then the group will visualize this passage.

This passage has four scenes.

**In the first scene:** Ezra gathers the Israelites to camp for three days near the river that flows towards Ahava. Ezra carefully looks at all the people and priests and sees that none of them are Levites.

**In the second scene:** Ezra sends a group of messengers to ask Iddo, the leader in Casiphia, to send Levites to come help with God's temple in Jerusalem.

**In the third scene:** God helps Ezra and the other Israelites and causes Levites and temple servants to come to help.

**In the fourth scene:** Ezra tells everyone at the Ahava River to fast and act humbly towards God. Ezra and the other people ask God to keep them, their children, and their possessions safe during the journey. Ezra reminds the people that Ezra did not ask the king for help because Ezra had told the king that God helps those who obey God and punishes those who turn away from God. God answers Ezra and the Israelite's prayers.

The characters in this passage are:

* Ezra
* Ezra's messengers: Eliezer, Ariel, Shemaiah, Elnathan, Jarib, another Elnathan, Nathan, Zechariah, Meshullam, Joiarib, and Elnathan
* Iddo and the other Levites in Casiphia
* The Levites: Sherebiah, Hashabiah, and Jeshaiah, and the other Levite men in their families
* 220 temple servants
* King Artaxerxes, the king's soldiers, and the king's horsemen
* And God

As a group, pay attention to these parts of the passage's setting:

Ezra tells this story in his own words. Ezra tells this story to other Israelites sometime after these events happened. Ezra talks about himself and about the other Israelites who go with Ezra.

Stop here and discuss how you will tell this story so that people will understand that Ezra himself tells this story to other Israelites sometime after the events of the story happened. Pause this audio here.

In scene one, Ezra assembles the Israelites at the river or canal that flows in the direction toward Ahava. Ezra and the other Israelites camp there in tents for three days. A tent is a temporary, movable shelter, and in those days, people made tents out of dried animal skins. Ezra checks the people and priests at the camp. Here, "people and priests" shows a difference between ordinary people and the priests who are Levites. The other Levites who are not priests help the Levite priests to take care of God's holy place. Ezra organizes the group and checks to see if any of these other Levites are among the group of people. Ezra does not find any of these Levites.

Stop here and discuss how you will talk about the difference between the ordinary people and the priests. Pause this audio here.

In scene two, Ezra sends for 11 men. In other words, Ezra asks someone to go find these men from among the group who camp there at the river. Ezra wants these men to all come to meet Ezra so Ezra can talk to these men all together. Ezra describes Eliezer, Ariel, Shemaiah, Elnathan, Jarib, another Elnathan, Nathan, Zechariah, and Meshullam as "heads" or leaders. Ezra describes Joiarib and another Elnathan as men who have understanding. Joiarib and Elnathan probably have general wisdom or, perhaps, an understanding of God's laws. Ezra says the name Elnathan three times. These are three different men.

Stop here and discuss how you will talk about the three different Elnathans so that people know these are two different men. Pause this audio here.

Ezra chooses these 11 men as his representatives or messengers. Ezra says that he "places in the messengers' mouths what to say." This means that Ezra tells the messengers what words to say. Ezra tells the 11 men to give Ezra's message to Iddo, the "head" or leader, Iddo's "brothers," and the temple servants. When Ezra says Iddo's "brothers," this refers to Iddo's fellow Levites. Ezra calls the temple servants by the title of their role. These temple servants did the lower\-level daily tasks in the temple. These men all live in a place called Casiphia. So, Ezra sends the messengers to Casiphia to tell Ezra's message to Iddo, the other Levites, and the Levites' temple servants.

Ezra specifically calls Casiphia, "the place." In those days, the Israelites in Babylon may have called the location of a place of worship, "the place." This probably means that Iddo leads a large community of Levites and the temple servants who assist the Levites to worship God in Casiphia.

Ezra asks Iddo and the other Levites to send servants who will serve God in the "house of our God," or God's temple.

In scene three, Ezra tells the Israelites that "God's good hand is on us," so messengers bring back Levites to help. In those days, "hand" often referred to someone's power. So, Ezra means that God helps Ezra and the other Israelites. The messengers bring Sherebiah, a man who has understanding. Here, "understanding" probably refers to practical wisdom, like the ability to act wisely with common sense and avoid foolish and harmful behavior. Or, "understanding" might mean the spiritual insight of someone who knows and obeys God. Either way, this means that Sherebiah was a person with good ability and intelligence to do religious duties as a Levite well. Sherebiah is one of the sons of Mahli. Mahli is a descendant of Levi. Levi is one of Israel's sons. You will remember that God gave the name "Israel" to Jacob, so Levi is one of the 12 sons of Jacob. The messengers also bring Sherebiah's sons and brothers, meaning Sherebiah's sons and other male relatives. Eighteen of Sherebiah's sons and male relatives come with the messengers to meet Ezra.

The messengers also bring Hashabiah and Jeshaiah, both of whom are descendants of Merari. Along with Hashabiah and Jeshaiah, 20 of their sons and "brothers" come. This means that the combination of Hashabiah's sons, Jeshaiah's sons, and their other male relatives totals 20 men.

The messengers also bring 220 of the temple servants. Ezra says that King David and King David's officials were the ones who first set up this type of group of temple servants as specific servants to help the Levites during the time when David ruled as king. This means that this type of group of temple servants continued to help the Levites for many generations. At this time, someone wrote the names of the 220 temple servants in books.

In scene four, Ezra "calls" out or tells the Israelites at the camp near the river by Ahava to do a fast. A fast is a specific amount of time when people do not eat food so that they can focus on a spiritual need. Ezra does this so that the Israelites at the camp will humble themselves before God. In other words, Ezra wants the people to recognize that God has authority over the people. So, Ezra wants the people to ask for God's help and to rely on God. Ezra also wants the Israelites to "seek" from God to make a straight way for them, their children, and their possessions. Here, "seek" means for the Israelites to ask or plead with God for help. By "straight way," the Israelites are asking God to protect them from dangers such as people who might try to rob them on the journey from the camp to Jerusalem. The possessions include the expensive gifts King Artaxerxes gave to Ezra in the previous story.

Stop here and discuss how you will describe what Ezra and the Israelites do as they humble themselves and seek God to make a straight way for them. Pause this audio here.

Ezra says that Ezra feels "ashamed" to ask the king for soldiers and horsemen to help protect the Israelites on the journey. Here, Ezra means that Ezra did not ask the king for help and will not ask the king for help. Ezra then says the words that Ezra previously told the king. Ezra had said that God's good hand helps everyone who seeks God. God acts with strong anger against everyone who chooses not to follow God.

Stop here and discuss how you will describe how God acts towards the people who follow God and towards the people who do not follow God. Pause this audio here.

Ezra finally says that the Israelites fast and seek "our God" and ask God to protect the Israelites. God listens to the Israelites' prayer. Ezra knows that God has answered the Israelites' prayer because Ezra tells this story after Ezra and the Israelites have already arrived safely.

Now, the group should storyboard, draw, or use objects to visualize the passage and the action in it.

Embodying the Text
------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 8:15–23 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will dramatize the passage.

This passage has four scenes.

The characters in this passage are:

* Ezra
* Ezra's messengers: Eliezer, Ariel, Shemaiah, Elnathan, Jarib, another Elnathan, Nathan, Zechariah, Meshullam, Joiarib, and Elnathan
* Iddo and the other Levites in Casiphia
* The Levites: Sherebiah, Hashabiah, and Jeshaiah, and the other Levite men in their families
* 220 temple servants
* King Artaxerxes, the king's soldiers, and the king's horsemen
* And God

As a group, act out the passage twice. You should act out the passage in your own language.

First, act out the passage without stopping. Pay attention to the dialogue, flow, plotline, and chronology of the passage. Make sure you do not skip acting out the difficult or important parts of the passage. Help each other remember every part of the passage.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage.

The group should act out this passage a second time. At certain points in the drama, pause the drama and ask the person playing the character, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person playing the character should answer what they would be feeling or thinking if they were that character. Then continue the drama.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage a second time.

The following is an example of the drama and possible responses.

In scene one, Ezra gathers the Israelites by the Ahava River. After carefully observing the priests and other people there, Ezra realizes no Levites are there.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing Ezra, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* God helped me check the people and find out that there are no Levites. I am thankful to God for his help; or
* We need Levites to serve in God's temple. I must fix this before we can start our journey; or
* I am responsible to make sure Levites come on our journey.

> Continue the drama.
> 
> In scene two, Ezra calls leaders and knowledgeable men to go to Casiphia to ask Iddo to send Levites to help.

Pause the drama. Ask the group playing Ezra's messengers, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* Ezra trusts us to tell his message to Iddo. We have a big responsibility. We must have Levites to serve in the temple in Jerusalem; or
* We need wisdom from God to explain Ezra's message clearly.

> Continue the drama.
> 
> In scene three, some Levites and 220 temple servants come to join the journey with Ezra and the other Israelites.

Pause the drama. Ask the group playing the Levites and temple servants, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* We feel honored and ready to serve Yahweh. God chose us to help in his temple! or
* We feel thankful to Yahweh for allowing us to be a part of Yahweh's good plan for our people; or
* We need Yahweh to continue to guide us on the upcoming journey, and when we serve Yahweh in his temple in Jerusalem.

> Continue the drama.
> 
> In scene four, Ezra and the other Israelites fast and pray to ask God to protect and help them.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing Ezra, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* God answered our desire for Levites to serve in his temple. We can trust God to answer our other prayers because we are following God. God is faithful to answer our prayers. God has done this for our people again and again for many generations! or
* I must humbly depend on God; or
* I trust God and will rely only on God; or
* I do not need to ask for help or protection from the king's soldiers. God will help and protect us; or
* I do not want the king to misunderstand if I ask for soldiers to help us travel safely. I want the king to see that Yahweh has the power to protect us; or
* I told the king that God helps the people who follow God, and God acts in anger towards the people who choose to turn away from God. I must follow God. I want God's help, and I want the king to see God's power to help; or
* We cannot rely on our own strength; we need to ask God for help; or
* Thank you, God, for responding with protection and guidance!

Filling the Gaps
----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 8:15–23 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will discuss key terms and words in this passage.

Ezra gathers the Israelites, both **priests** and other people. Ezra does not find any **Levites** in this group. For more information on priests and Levites, refer to the Master Glossary, and translate priests and Levites in the same way as you did previously.

Ezra sends messengers to ask the Levites and temple **servants** to come with Ezra so they can serve in the **temple** in Jerusalem. Usually, a servant is a man or woman who works for another person, the master. These temple servants specifically work as servants in the temple to help the Levites with ordinary tasks so that the Levites can do their religious duties. Ezra calls the temple servants by the title of their role, the specific name that King David gave to these servants: "Nethinim." In Ezra's language, Nethinim are the specific group of temple servants whom King David and his officials set up to help the Levites. The Nethinim in this story are the descendants of the group King David set up. Look up servant and temple in the Master Glossary for more information.

Stop here and discuss how you will talk about the temple servants who help the Levites in your language. Pause this audio here.

Ezra talks about the **house of our God**. Ezra is referring to the temple. For more information on "house of God," see the Master Glossary, and translate house of God and **God** in the same way as you did previously.

Ezra tells the Israelites to start fasting, or not to eat food for a short time, so that they can pray to God. Ezra also says that he does not ask the **king** for help because the king might think Ezra does not trust God. Ezra says that God helps people who follow God, but feels great anger towards people who do not follow God. For more information on king, see the Master Glossary.

Stop here and, as a group, discuss what word or phrase you will use for **fasting**. For more information about fasting, refer to the Master Glossary. Translate fasting in the same way as you have in other books of the Bible.

Speaking the Word
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of the passage in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this session, retell the passage, as a group, in your own language.

First, the whole group should practice telling the passage in your own language. Remember to include the things you decided in previous steps, like chronology, words, phrases, emotions, or anything else you discussed.

One group member should tell a few sentences of the passage and pause. Another group member should tell the next part of the passage and pause. Do this until the group has retold the whole passage. The group members can correct each other in this step.

Pause this audio here.

Next, each member of the group should tell the whole passage without interruption at least one time. A group member can use random objects, motions, or their storyboard to help them remember the passage.

Pause this audio here.

Next, the group can decide whose version of the passage you like the most. You may decide that you liked the way one group member told one scene but you liked the way a different group member told another scene.

Pause this audio here.

Finally, the group can put together a final version of the passage that everyone agrees on. Each group member should practice telling this final version of the passage.
