## Ezra 8:24–30 (id: eng-ezr-p17-v1)

Hear and Heart
--------------

In this step, hear Ezra 8:24–30 and put it in your hearts.

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 8:24–30 three times, in three different translations, if possible.

Pause this audio here.

Now, as a group, discuss the following questions:

What do you like in this passage? Pause this audio here.

What do you not like or not understand in this passage? Pause this audio here.

What does this passage tell us about God? Pause this audio here.

What does this passage tell us about people? Pause this audio here.

How does this passage affect our daily lives? Pause this audio here.

Who do you know who needs to hear this passage?

Setting the Stage
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 8:24–30 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In the previous story, Ezra gathered the people by the Ahava River in the region of Babylon. This group of Israelite men, women, and children had chosen to leave Babylon to travel with Ezra to Jerusalem. As the people prepared for the long journey, Ezra sent a message and asked for some Levites to join them. Then the people all prayed and fasted together. The people asked Yahweh to protect the people during the journey. Ezra decided not to ask King Artaxerxes for soldiers to protect the people. Instead, Ezra trusted that Yahweh would look after the people.

Stop here and, as a group, look at a map that shows Babylon and Jerusalem. Pause this audio here.

In this story, Ezra prepares to take the holy items and offerings with Ezra and the people. These are gifts that people have set apart, or given, especially for Yahweh's temple in Jerusalem. These temple gifts came from three groups: King Artaxerxes of Persia and his officials, the leaders of the region around Judah and Jerusalem, and the Israelites who were traveling with Ezra. You may remember that Artaxerxes was the king who had previously allowed Ezra to lead a group of Israelites from Babylon back to Jerusalem. Ezra knows that the temple gifts all belong to Yahweh. Ezra understands that it is important for the people to treat these items with care and respect until the group reaches Jerusalem.

Before they set out on the journey, Ezra chooses several leaders from the priests and several leaders from the Levites to look after and carry the temple gifts. Scholars do not completely agree about the number of leaders Ezra chose, but most people think that there were probably 12 leading priests and also 12 Levites. The 12 Levites include Sherebiah, Hashabiah, and 10 of their brothers or relatives.

Ezra carefully counts and weighs out the temple gifts. The gifts include silver, gold, and other treasures. Ezra does not weigh these items straight into the hands of the priests and Levites. Ezra weighs the gifts first on a weighing scale or balance of some kind, and then Ezra gives the items to the priests and Levites to look after. Ezra counts and weighs the treasures to make sure the group does not lose anything on the journey.

Stop here and, as a group, look at a picture of a weighing scale that people may have used at that time to weigh items. Pause this audio here.

The storyteller talks about silver, gold, and bronze articles and vessels for the temple. Vessels are containers such as cups, bowls, or other items people use to hold things during worship. Bowls are round containers that people use to hold liquids or offerings in the temple.

The people of those lands sometimes measured large amounts of silver and gold in talents. A talent is a unit of weight used to measure precious metals. One talent is about 34 kilograms. The storyteller also uses the word "darics" when listing the temple treasures to describe how much they are worth. Darics are a type of money or coin that people made out of gold.

Here is a list of the items that Ezra weighs out, along with how much each item weighs:

* About 22,100 kilograms of silver.
* About 3,400 kilograms of silver articles.
* About 3,400 kilograms of gold.
* 20 gold bowls that weigh about 8\.4 kilograms in total.
* 2 polished articles that look very valuable, like gold. We do not know the weight of these.

Stop here and, as a group, look at a picture of some valuable temple items like bowls or cups so that you imagine the kind of items that Ezra seeks to protect. Pause this audio here.

Scholars do not know what people made the final two temple gifts from. Some scholars think that people made these two vessels from metal, either copper or bronze, but then polished them to look like gold.

In total, the weight of these temple gifts is the same as the weight of about 415 adults.

Ezra reminds the priests and Levites that the priests and Levites are "holy," set apart for Yahweh. Ezra also says that the temple items are "holy," set apart for Yahweh. This shows that only the holy people who serve in the temple should carry the holy items for the temple.

Ezra tells the priests and the Levites that the silver and gold are a freewill offering to Yahweh. This means that the people gave the silver and gold as gifts to show their devotion to Yahweh because they wanted to, not because Yahweh told the people to.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: In your culture, when do people give gifts freely? When do people give gifts because leaders ask the people to? What is the difference between the two? Pause this audio here.

Ezra tells the priests and the Levites that they must guard the temple gifts very carefully. Ezra explains what will happen once the group arrives in Jerusalem. The priests and the Levites will give the temple gifts to the leading priests, the Levites, and the heads of the families of Israel in Jerusalem. The priests and Levites will then weigh the items again in the storerooms of the house of Yahweh. These storerooms were probably the outer rooms of the temple in Jerusalem, which the people used to store things.

The priests and Levites accept this responsibility. The priests and Levites take charge of the silver, gold, and sacred items, and prepare to carry them on the journey.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: In your culture, how do people keep valuable things safe on a long trip? Pause this audio here.

We see in this story that Ezra takes responsibility for the gifts for Yahweh's temple and makes sure that the priests and Levites look after these things well. Next, the group will start on the journey to Jerusalem. The priests and Levites will carry all the temple gifts carefully.

Defining the Scenes
-------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 8:24–30 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will define the scenes, characters, and setting of this passage. Then the group will visualize this passage.

This passage has three scenes.

**In the first scene:** Ezra chooses 12 leading priests and 12 Levites. Ezra gives the priests and Levites responsibility for the valuable items for God's temple. Ezra weighs out and lists the items carefully.

**In the second scene:** Ezra tells the priests and Levites that they and the holy items belong to Yahweh. Ezra says the priests and Levites must guard the items carefully until they have given the valuable items to the priests and Levites at the temple in Jerusalem.

**In the third scene:** The priests and Levites accept this responsibility and take the items, ready for the journey.

The characters in this passage are:

* Ezra
* 12 leading priests
* 12 Levites, including Sherebiah and Hashabiah
* Heads of families
* King and his officials
* The exiles
* And Yahweh

As a group, pay attention to these parts of the passage's setting:

In scene one, the Israelite men, women, and children gather along with Ezra at the Ahava River in Babylon. Ezra chooses 12 leaders from the priests and 12 leaders from the Levites to look after and carry the temple gifts from Babylon to Jerusalem. The 12 Levites include Sherebiah, Hashabiah, and 10 brothers or relatives.

Remember, the priests and the Levites have a special responsibility for the temple and for serving in the temple. So, Ezra chooses these people specially to handle and deliver the temple gifts. Ezra chooses 12 priests and 12 Levites. Ezra may consider the number 12 important because it might remind people of the 12 tribes of Israel.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: In your culture, what kinds of people do you choose for special tasks, like caring for important places or valuable things? How do people choose these people, and how do people treat these people? Pause this audio here.

After Ezra has chosen the priests and Levites who will help him, Ezra then carefully counts and weighs out the silver, the gold, and the articles. People have given these items as special gifts to Yahweh.

It is important to note that Ezra does not weigh these items straight into the hands of the priests and Levites. Ezra weighs the items first on a weighing scale or balance of some kind. Then Ezra gives the treasures to the priests and Levites. To "weigh out" means to measure by weight so that people get the right amount. When Ezra weighs out the items into their hands, each person receives the correct amount.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for "weighed out into their hands." Pause this audio here.

The reason Ezra counts and weighs the treasures is to make sure that the people do not lose anything on the journey when they travel from Babylon to Jerusalem.

Stop here and, as a group, look at a map that shows Babylon and Jerusalem. Pause this audio here.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: In your culture, when do people count, measure, or weigh things carefully? What items might people weigh? Why is this important? What word or phrase will you use for "measuring by weight"? Pause this audio here.

In scene two, Ezra reminds the priests and Levites that the priests and Levites are holy, set apart for Yahweh. Ezra also says that the temple items are holy, set apart for Yahweh. Ezra makes a comparison between the people and the temple items. Ezra repeats the word "holy" often. This shows that only the holy people who serve in the temple should carry the holy items for the temple.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: Can you think of things in your culture that people consider special or holy? How do people treat these things differently from ordinary things? Pause this audio here.

Ezra tells the priests and the Levites that the silver and gold are a freewill offering to Yahweh. This means that the people wanted to give the silver and gold as gifts to show their devotion, love, and loyalty to Yahweh. A gift of this kind shows that Yahweh's people want to be close to Yahweh, not just obey rules.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: Why do you think Ezra reminds the priests and Levites that the silver and gold are a freewill offering to Yahweh? What difference might it make that the people gave these treasures gladly? Pause this audio here.

Ezra tells the priests and the Levites to guard all the temple items very carefully. Ezra then explains to the priests and the Levites what will happen once they arrive in Jerusalem. Ezra tells the priests and Levites that they will give the treasures to the leading priests, the Levites, and the heads of the families of Israel. The priests and Levites will weigh the items again in the storerooms of the house of Yahweh. These storerooms were probably the outer rooms of the temple in Jerusalem, where the people kept things.

In the final part of scene two, the priests and Levites agree to protect the treasures. The priests and Levites take charge of the silver, gold, and sacred articles, and prepare to carry these things on the journey from Babylon to Jerusalem.

Now, the group should storyboard, draw, or use objects to visualize the passage and the action in it.

Embodying the Text
------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 8:24–30 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will dramatize the passage.

This passage has three scenes.

The characters in this passage are:

* Ezra
* 12 leading priests
* 12 Levites, including Sherebiah and Hashabiah
* Heads of families
* King and his officials
* The exiles
* And Yahweh

As a group, act out the passage twice. You should act out the passage in your own language.

First, act out the passage without stopping. Pay attention to the dialogue, flow, plotline, and chronology of the passage. Make sure you do not skip acting out the difficult or important parts of the passage. Help each other remember every part of the passage.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage.

The group should act out this passage a second time. At certain points in the drama, pause the drama and ask the person playing the character, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person playing the character should answer what they would be feeling or thinking if they were that character. Then continue the drama.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage a second time.

The following is an example of the drama and possible responses.

Act out Ezra choosing 12 priests and 12 Levites to take care of the temple treasures.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing Ezra, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* I know these treasures belong to Yahweh, so I must choose the right people to take care of these treasures; or
* I hope the people I have chosen understand how important their task is; or
* I pray that Yahweh protects us as we start on our long journey to Jerusalem with these treasures.

Continue the drama.

Act out Ezra weighing out the treasures carefully.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing Ezra, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* These items belong to Yahweh. I must be careful to count and weigh everything correctly; or
* Every piece of silver and gold is sacred. I want to treat these items with respect; or
* I am grateful that the people have given these offerings willingly for the temple, and I want to honor the people's devotion.

Continue the drama.

Act out the priests and Levites watching as Ezra weighs out the treasures.

Pause the drama. Ask the people playing the priests and Levites, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* These treasures look heavy and valuable! We must be very careful as we handle these treasures; or
* Ezra is taking this task very seriously! Ezra is counting and weighing everything very carefully.

Continue the drama.

Act out Ezra telling the priests and Levites that they are holy and the treasures are holy. Ezra tells the priests and Levites to look after the treasures carefully until they reach the temple.

Pause the drama. Ask the people playing the priests and Levites, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The people might answer things like:

* I feel a little nervous because this is such an important task; or
* I feel proud to be chosen for this important work; or
* I want to honor Yahweh by guarding these treasures carefully.

Continue the drama.

Act out the priests and Levites as they take charge of the items ready for the journey.

Pause the drama. Ask the people playing the priests and Levites, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The people might answer things like:

* We must protect these offerings. These offerings belong to Yahweh and are very important for the temple; or
* I feel a little worried about thieves on the road during our journey. I trust Yahweh will help us.

Continue the drama.

Act out Yahweh as he sees how Ezra, the priests, and the Levites take care of the treasures.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing Yahweh, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* I am pleased that Ezra and the people respect the holy treasures; or
* I am watching to see if the priests and Levites are careful and faithful; or
* I care about my people and want them to reach Jerusalem safely with the holy treasures.

Filling the Gaps
----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 8:24–30 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will discuss key terms and words in this passage.

Ezra chooses 12 "leading **priests**" to help carry and guard the treasures for God's temple. Priests are Levite men who serve God and help the Israelites make sacrifices to God. These leaders direct the other priests and make sure the treasures are safe. A leading priest is a priest with authority over other priests. For more information on "priest," refer to the Master Glossary.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for "**leaders of the priests**." Pause this audio here.

In the next story, the people will travel to **God's house** or **temple** in Jerusalem. The temple is the special place in Jerusalem where God's people worship God and bring offerings. In this story, Ezra's priests and Levites prepare to carry the holy items and treasures to the temple. Use the same word or phrase for God, and "house of God" or temple, that you used in previous passages. For more information on God, "**house of God**," or temple, refer to the Master Glossary.

Ezra also chooses 12 **Levites** who are not priests to help carry and guard the treasures alongside the priests. Levites are members of the tribe of Levi who are set apart and have special duties in the temple to help the priests. In this story, the Levites work closely with the priests to handle the treasure well. Use the same word or phrase for Levites as you used in previous passages. For more information on Levites, refer to the Master Glossary.

Among the Levites, the storyteller describes some as **relatives** or **brothers** of Sherebiah and Hashabiah. This shows that family connections were important when Ezra chose the right people for the task. A relative or brother is a male relative from the same family or extended family. Use the same word or phrase for relative or brother as you used in previous passages.

Ezra counts and weighs large amounts of **silver** to give to the priests and Levites. Silver is a valuable, shiny, grey metal that people used in temple offerings. In this story, silver is a treasure that people gave to Yahweh for the temple. People often use silver for money, gifts, or valuable objects. Use the same word or phrase for silver as you used in previous passages.

Stop here and look at a picture of a piece of silver as a group. Pause this audio here.

Along with silver, Ezra counts and weighs **gold** as part of the temple treasures. Gold is a valuable, shiny, yellow metal. In this passage, gold is a treasure that the people gave to Yahweh's temple because they wanted to show their devotion. Use the same word or phrase for gold as you used in previous passages.

Stop here and look at a picture of a piece of gold as a group. Pause this audio here.

Two polished **bronze** items are part of the temple treasures that Ezra counts. Bronze is a strong metal that people made from copper and tin. Scholars think these items were valuable and shiny, like gold. Bronze is a metal that people use to make strong, useful, or valuable objects. Use the same word or phrase for bronze as you used in previous passages. For more information on bronze, refer to the Master Glossary.

Stop here and look at a picture of a piece of bronze as a group. Pause this audio here.

The storyteller describes some of the silver, gold, and bronze **articles** that people offered for the temple. Some of these silver, gold, and bronze articles are containers or objects that people used to hold things during temple worship. A vessel is a container, cup, bowl, or other item that can hold something. Some of these items are articles that priests use in the temple to help them with their work. Use the same word or phrase for the silver, gold, and bronze articles as you used in previous passages.

The gold includes **bowls** that are part of the temple treasures. Bowls are open, round containers that people often used in the temple for offerings or rituals. A bowl is a round container that people used to hold liquids or objects. Use the same word or phrase for bowl as you used in previous passages.

Stop here and, as a group, look at a picture of the kinds of silver, gold, and bronze items that Ezra described in this story. These could be articles, vessels, or bowls. Think about what words you will use to describe those items. Pause this audio here.

The storyteller sometimes mentions **darics** when listing the temple treasures to describe how much they are worth. Darics are a type of money or coin that people made of gold. Darics were valuable coins that people used in trade or gave as gifts in ancient times. If you use the word for darics, use the same word or phrase for daric as you used in previous passages.

Stop here and look at a picture of a daric as a group. Pause this audio here.

People sometimes measure large amounts of silver and gold in **talents**. A talent is a unit of weight that people used to measure precious metals. One talent is about 34 kilograms. If you use the word for talent, use the same word or phrase for talent as you used in previous passages. For more information on talent, refer to the Master Glossary.

Ezra reminds the priests and Levites that they are **holy**, and the temple items are holy. Things that are holy are things that Yahweh has set apart as special for Yahweh. Things that are holy belong to Yahweh, and people should treat those things with respect. Use the same word or phrase for holy as you used in previous passages. For more information on holy, refer to the Master Glossary.

Ezra calls the silver and gold for the temple "**freewill offerings**." This means the people voluntarily give these treasures to Yahweh out of love and devotion. A freewill offering is something that people give freely, not through force or duty. Use the same word or phrase for freewill offering as you used in previous passages. For more information on freewill offering, refer to the Master Glossary.

Ezra plans that after arriving in Jerusalem, the leading priests, Levites, and **heads of Israelite families** or **heads of father's houses** will receive the treasures. A head of a father's house is a leader responsible for a family group among the Israelites. Use the same word or phrase for "heads of Israelite families" or "heads of fathers' houses," and for **household,** as you used in previous passages.

In Jerusalem, the priests will keep the treasures in the chambers or storerooms of the temple. Storerooms or chambers are often outer, enclosed rooms in the temple that people used to store valuable items.

Stop here and discuss: What word or phrase will you use for **storerooms**? Pause this audio here.

Some of the gifts for the temple came from the **king** of Persia. A king is someone who rules over a country, region, or people. Use the same word or phrase for king, **Babylon**, and **Jerusalem** as you used in previous passages. For more information on king, refer to the Master Glossary.

**Advisors** or counselors for the king also give gifts for the temple. Advisors help the king make decisions and manage the kingdom. An advisor is someone who gives advice or helps make important decisions. Use the same word or phrase for advisor as you used in previous passages.

Some **officials** also give gifts for the temple. An official is a person in a position of authority or responsibility. Officials are people who have authority in the government or administration. Use the same word or phrase for officials as you used in previous passages.

The silver, gold, and items are **offerings** to God's temple. An offering is something that people give to Yahweh as a gift of devotion, worship, or support. An offering is a gift that people give for a religious or sacred purpose. Use the same word or phrase for offering as you used in previous passages. For more information on "offering," refer to the Master Glossary.

Ezra "sets apart" or consecrates the priests, Levites, and temple treasures for Yahweh. To set apart means to make something special for a holy purpose. When someone sets something apart, they choose or reserve that thing for people to use in a holy or important way.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **consecrate**. Use the same word or phrase for consecrate as you used in previous books of the Bible, and refer to the Master Glossary for more information about consecrate.

Speaking the Word
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of the passage in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this session, retell the passage, as a group, in your own language.

First, the whole group should practice telling the passage in your own language. Remember to include the things you decided in previous steps, like chronology, words, phrases, emotions, or anything else you discussed.

One group member should tell a few sentences of the passage and pause. Another group member should tell the next part of the passage and pause. Do this until the group has retold the whole passage. The group members can correct each other in this step.

Pause this audio here.

Next, each member of the group should tell the whole passage without interruption at least one time. A group member can use random objects, motions, or their storyboard to help them remember the passage.

Pause this audio here.

Next, the group can decide whose version of the passage you like the most. You may decide that you liked the way one group member told one scene but you liked the way a different group member told another scene.

Pause this audio here.

Finally, the group can put together a final version of the passage that everyone agrees on. Each group member should practice telling this final version of the passage.

* **Associated Passages:** Ezra 8:24-30
* **Associated Articles:** Gold (ID: `a664@FIAImages`); Gold (ID: `a1995@FIAImages`); Gold (ID: `a1996@FIAImages`); Bronze (ID: `a499@FIAImages`); Bronze (ID: `a500@FIAImages`); Bronze (ID: `a1335@FIAImages`); Bronze (ID: `a1336@FIAImages`); Bronze (ID: `a1341@FIAImages`); Bronze (ID: `a1486@FIAImages`); Raw Pieces of Silver (ID: `a1711@FIAImages`); Raw Pieces of Silver (ID: `a1712@FIAImages`); Raw Pieces of Silver (ID: `a1713@FIAImages`); holy (ID: `eng-t67-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); household (ID: `eng-t70-v2@FIAKeyTerms`); priest (ID: `eng-t103-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); temple (ID: `eng-t138-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); house of God (ID: `eng-t183-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); God (ID: `eng-t189-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); offering (ID: `eng-t194-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); king (ID: `eng-t226-v2@FIAKeyTerms`); Levite (ID: `eng-t244-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); consecrate (ID: `eng-t254-v1@FIAKeyTerms`)

## Ezra 8:31–36 (id: eng-ezr-p18-v1)

Hear and Heart
--------------

In this step, hear Ezra 8:31–36 and put it in your hearts.

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 8:31–36 three times, in three different translations, if possible.

Pause this audio here.

Now, as a group, discuss the following questions:

1. What do you like in this passage? Pause this audio here.
2. What do you not like or not understand in this passage? Pause this audio here.
3. What does this passage tell us about God? Pause this audio here.
4. What does this passage tell us about people? Pause this audio here.
5. How does this passage affect our daily lives? Pause this audio here.
6. Who do you know who needs to hear this passage?

Setting the Stage
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 8:31–36 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In the previous story, Ezra and the exiles who were returning to Judah gathered beside the Ahava River to prepare for the journey from Babylon to Jerusalem. Ezra carefully weighed and recorded all the temple treasures. Ezra gave the treasures to the priests and Levites. The priests and Levites agreed to guard the treasures on the long journey.

Stop here and, as a group, look at a map that shows Babylon and Jerusalem. Pause this audio here.

The storyteller tells us that the journey starts at the Ahava River. This was likely a canal or waterway in the Babylonian region. The group leaves on the twelfth day of the first month. It would have been mid\-April as the weather became warmer in that part of the world. Ezra began to travel from Babylon to the Ahava Canal to gather everyone on the first day of the first month. It took Ezra 12 days to assemble the exiles, search for Levites, and make preparations.

Ezra and the exiles travel together in a large group, probably over 5,000 people. The group carries families, animals, and the treasures for the temple. The journey covers about 1,450 kilometers, and takes the group about four months. The exiles would have arrived in Jerusalem around the beginning of the month of August.

The storyteller does not give us many details about the journey itself. The storyteller says that the hand of God is upon the group. This means Yahweh gives the group his power and protection. Yahweh saves the people from enemies and from ambushes along the way. Travelers in those days were often in danger from wild animals or robbers, who hid beside the road. We do not know if bandits actually attack the people, but Yahweh brings the group safely through the journey.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: In your community, what dangers are people in when they travel long distances? How do people stay safe on a journey? Pause this audio here.

The group arrives in Jerusalem and stays for three days. The storyteller does not explain exactly why. The people probably rested after the long journey. Or, maybe the exiles arrived on a Friday and waited for the Sabbath to finish before they weighed the treasures.

Stop here and look at a picture of temple treasures of silver, gold, and bronze as a group. Pause this audio here.

On the fourth day, the group goes to the temple. Ezra weighs all the treasures and gives them to the two priests and two Levites in charge of the temple treasures. The priests are Meremoth, the son of Uriah, and Eleazar, the son of Phinehas. The Levites are Jozabad, son of Jeshua, and Noadiah, son of Binnu. We do not know for sure if these are the same people that the storyteller mentions in other parts of the Bible.

Ezra and the priests and Levites carefully record the weight of the treasure, so there is a clear record of the treasure. The people probably recorded this information to show King Artaxerxes that the group had delivered and counted all the treasures correctly. In the Persian Empire, it was normal to keep detailed records like this. The storyteller does not say this directly, but we can probably assume the people kept everything safe.

Then, the exiles, or the people who had come from Babylon, make offerings to Yahweh. At this time, people gave burnt offerings to Yahweh to show that they worshipped God and were devoted to God. People gave sin offerings or purification offerings to Yahweh to become clean after someone had become ritually unclean or had accidentally broken one of Yahweh's laws. The people make a burnt offering of 12 bulls, 96 rams, and 77 lambs for all of Israel. The large number of animals shows that the burnt offerings are not just for the exiles, but for, or on behalf of, the entire nation of Israel. The burnt offerings covered or represented everyone in the community. The people also make the sin offering or purification offering of 12 male goats.

Stop here and, as a group, look at a picture of a bull, a ram, a lamb, and a male goat. Pause this audio here.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: When someone arrives safely somewhere after a long journey, what do people do to celebrate or show that they are thankful that they have arrived safely? Pause this audio here.

Ezra takes King Artaxerxes' written orders to the local governors and to the king's satraps or important leaders from the entire region, and to the local governors from the province beyond the river. This was the region west of the Euphrates River, which people sometimes called the Trans\-Euphrates.

Stop here and, as a group, look at a map of the Trans\-Euphrates area. Pause this audio here.

A satrap was a governor in the Persian Empire that the king appointed to manage a large area. The satrap collected taxes, kept the region in order, and ensured that people obeyed the king's laws. You will remember that these written orders were letters that gave instructions from the king. In these letters, the king asks the local leaders to support the people and the temple. So, by delivering these letters, Ezra makes sure that the people have the help and resources they need to rebuild the temple and continue to worship.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: Why is it important to have official approval, like the king's letters? How do leaders or authorities in your community give permission or approval for projects? Pause this audio here.

This story shows \[that the people of God were preparing to travel to Jerusalem, and they finally did it!]{.mark} The people travel safely, worship God, and follow the king's instructions. In the next story, we will hear that Ezra is concerned when he discovers that some of the people have not followed Yahweh's commands.

Defining the Scenes
-------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 8 31–36 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will define the scenes, characters, and setting of this passage. Then the group will visualize this passage.

This passage has five scenes.

**In the first scene:** The group begins the long journey from Babylon to Jerusalem. God protects the people from danger, including enemies or robbers. We do not know if attacks happened, but God brought the people safely through the journey.

**In the second scene:** The people arrive in Jerusalem and stay there for three days. The people probably needed to rest after the long journey.

**In the third scene:** The people go to the temple. Here, Ezra weighs all the treasures and gives them to the priests and Levites, who will take care of the treasures. Ezra carefully records the weight of the treasures. This may be to show King Artaxerxes that the people have delivered the treasures properly.

**In the fourth scene:** The returned exiles offer sacrifices to Yahweh, the God of Israel: 12 bulls for all Israel, 96 rams, and 77 lambs as burnt offerings, and 12 male goats as purification offerings.

**In the fifth scene:** The people give the king's written orders to the local governors and the Persian satraps of the province of Trans\-Euphrates, the area west of the Euphrates River. In these letters the king gives instructions to those officials to support the exiles and the temple.

The characters in this passage are:

* Ezra
* The returned exiles
* The priests, including Meremoth and Eleazar
* The Levites, including Jozabad and Noadiah
* The satraps and local governors
* And Yahweh, the God of Israel

As a group, pay attention to these parts of the passage's setting:

The people begin their journey from Babylon to Jerusalem. It is a long and dangerous journey over a distance of around 1,400 kilometres. Over 5,000 people travel as a group in a long line. The group carries animals and heavy treasures. Robbers might attack the people, so the people probably feel afraid. However, "the hand of God was on them," which means that God protects the people all the way.

Stop here and, as a group, look at a map that shows Babylon and Jerusalem. Notice how far the group travels and imagine walking for four months through this land. Pause this audio here.

It is important to notice that Ezra tells this story himself. This reminds us that Ezra is central to the story. Ezra tells us that the people started on their journey on the twelfth day of the first month.

When the people arrive in Jerusalem, the people stay there for three days. The group rests after their long, dangerous journey before they do anything else. On the fourth day, after three days of resting, Ezra and the people go to the temple. We know this because the storyteller says that what happens next is "within the house of our God." The "house of our God" is another way to say the temple.

At the temple, Ezra weighs all the treasures and gives them to the temple priests and Levites to look after. The storyteller names the men who receive them: the priests Meremoth son of Uriah and Eleazar son of Phinehas, and the Levites Jozabad son of Jeshua and Noadiah son of Binnui. The storyteller gives the fathers' names to show exactly who these men are. The fathers' names show that these men come from the right families, so God allows these men to serve in the temple.

Ezra weighs the treasures "into the hands" of Meremoth, Eleazar, Jozabad, and Noadiah. This is a careful process. It means that Ezra first weighs the treasures on a scale, then Ezra hands the treasures over to the men. Finally, Ezra makes a record of the weights of the treasures. \[The storyteller probably says that Ezra, or someone, makes a record at that time. This means that Ezra probably writes down how much each treasure weighs in order to show that the people have not lost or stolen anything on the way.]{.mark}

At this point, the storyteller changes how he tells the story. Before, Ezra spoke as part of the story. Now, someone else tells what Ezra and the people do. Many scholars think someone different added this short section to complete the story. We do not know for sure.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: In your language, how do you change who is telling a story in the middle of the story? In this story, how will you show that Ezra has stopped talking and someone else is telling this part of the story? Pause this audio here.

The exiles, or the people who had returned from captivity, make offerings to Yahweh, the God of Israel. People normally gave burnt offerings to Yahweh to show that they worshipped Yahweh, were devoted to Yahweh, and were thankful to Yahweh. People gave sin offerings or purification offerings to Yahweh to become clean after someone had become ritually unclean or had accidentally broken one of Yahweh's laws. In a burnt offering, the people burned the whole animal. In a sin or purification offering, the people burned only part of the animal, and the priests kept the rest.

Notice that Ezra gives a list of animals for the offerings. This means that Ezra names each kind of animal, one after another: bulls, rams, lambs, and goats. This careful list shows that the people want to respect and honour Yahweh with the offerings they make.

Notice the numbers in this list too. Most of the numbers connect to 12\. This is a way to remind us of the 12 tribes of Israel. The number 77 is different, so some experts think the number should be 72\. Others think 77 means a large or complete number. No one knows for sure.

At the end of the list of offerings, the storyteller says that "all this was a burnt offering to Yahweh." This is confusing because the male goats were sin offerings or purification offerings, but not burnt offerings. Scholars think that the storyteller is only talking about the burnt offerings—the bulls, rams, and lambs, which are the largest part of the offering.

Stop here and discuss as a group how you will list these offerings—the bulls, rams, and lambs for the burnt offerings and the male goats for the sin or purification offering. Pause this audio here.

After the offerings, the group delivers the king's letters to the Persian satraps and governors from west of the Euphrates River. Usually, a single satrap would look after this region with several governors under him. However, the storyteller uses a word that means more than one satrap. So, scholars think that satraps from more than one region helped the returned exiles.

The king had told the leaders to give help to the Jewish people and the temple in Jerusalem. So, after they read the king's orders, these leaders probably gave practical support. The leaders likely provided money, food, animals, or protection for the temple and for the people who served there.

Now, the group should storyboard, draw, or use objects to visualize the passage and the action in it.

Embodying the Text
------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 8:31–36 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will dramatize the passage.

This passage has five scenes.

The characters in this passage are:

* Ezra
* The returned exiles
* The priests, including Meremoth and Eleazar
* The Levites, including Jozabad and Noadiah
* The satraps and local governors
* And Yahweh, the God of Israel

As a group, act out the passage twice. You should act out the passage in your own language.

First, act out the passage without stopping. Pay attention to the dialogue, flow, plotline, and chronology of the passage. Make sure you do not skip acting out the difficult or important parts of the passage. Help each other remember every part of the passage.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage.

The group should act out this passage a second time. At certain points in the drama, pause the drama and ask the person playing the character, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person playing the character should answer what they would be feeling or thinking if they were that character. Then continue the drama.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage a second time.

The following is an example of the drama and possible responses.

Act out the exiles starting the long journey from Babylon to Jerusalem.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing the exiles, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* Nervous. It is a long journey and I feel afraid that robbers might attack us; or
* Hopeful. I hope we all reach Jerusalem safely with the treasures; or
* Grateful. I am so glad that Yahweh is watching over us all as we travel.

Continue the drama.

Act out the people arriving in Jerusalem and staying for three days.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing exiles, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* Tired. I need to rest well after such a long journey; or
* Happy. I am so pleased to see Jerusalem again; or
* Thoughtful. I am thinking about the tasks we must do next at the temple.

Continue the drama.

Act out Ezra weighing the treasures and handing them over to the priests and Levites at the temple.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing Ezra, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* Focussed. I must record everything carefully to show that we have taken care of the treasures on our journey; or
* Determined. These treasures belong to Yahweh and I want to honour him; or
* Proud. I feel glad to serve Yahweh in this important work.

Continue the drama.

Act out the exiles offering the bulls, rams, lambs, and male goats to Yahweh.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing the exiles, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* Devoted. I want to honour God by making these offerings; or
* Thankful. I am grateful to worship Yahweh in this way after the long journey; or
* Hopeful. I hope Yahweh accepts our sacrifices and blesses Israel.

Continue the drama.

Act out Ezra and the people giving the Persian leaders of the province of Trans\-Euphrates the written orders from King Artaxerxes.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing the Persian leaders, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* Respectful. I must follow the king's instructions carefully and help these people as the king commanded; or
* Responsible. I want to support the people of Israel; or
* Careful. I want to make sure the temple work continues smoothly.

Filling the Gaps
----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 8:31–36 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will discuss key terms and words in this passage.

The exiles travel to Jerusalem. Ezra says "the **hand of our God** was on us" during the exiles' journey. This means God gives the exiles help, power, and protection. The general word for the Creator God is in the Master Glossary. Use the same word or phrase for "hand of our God" as you used in previous passages.

Ezra says that God keeps the group safe from the hand, or power of the enemy. This means Yahweh protects the exiles from people who want to harm them. Use the same word or phrase for **hand of the enemy** as you used in previous passages.

Ezra says that God protects the group from enemies and ambushes on the journey. Ambushes are surprise attacks by people who hide and wait to harm travellers.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **ambushes**. If you have already translated this word in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

The exiles arrive in Jerusalem and stay there for three days. After three days, Ezra says that he and the exiles go to the **house of our God** in Jerusalem. The "house of our God" is Ezra's way to talk about the **temple**, and Ezra makes it clear that the temple is the people of Israel's special temple when he calls it the house of "our" God specifically. It is not the temple for any other god. Use the same word or phrase for temple or house of our God as you used in previous passages. For more information on temple or "house of God," refer to the Master Glossary.

Ezra and the exiles bring **silver**, **gold**, and **articles** to the temple. The people give these valuable items for God's house. Silver and gold are precious, shiny metals. People use silver and gold to make money, gifts, and offerings. Articles are containers or objects that people use in temple worship. Use the same words or phrases for silver, gold, and articles as you used in previous passages.

Ezra measures the treasure before he gives the treasure to the **priests** and **Levites**. To weigh out means to measure by weight so that each person receives the correct amount. Use the same word or phrase for the action of **weighing out into the hands** of the priests and Levites as you used in previous passages. For more information on priests and Levites refer to the Master Glossary, and use the same words for priests and Levites as you used previously.

The **returned exiles** offer burnt offerings to the God of Israel. Returned exiles are the people of Israel who return to Jerusalem after living in Babylon. Use the same word or phrase for returned exiles or exiles as you used in previous passages.

The exiles make **burnt offerings** and **sin offerings** or **purification offerings** to God. A burnt offering means the people burn an animal completely on the altar to show worship and honour to God. People gave purification offerings to God to become clean after someone had become ritually unclean or had accidentally broken one of Yahweh's laws. Use the same word or phrase for burnt offerings and sin offerings or purification offerings as you used in previous passages. For more information about burnt offerings and sin offerings or purification offerings, refer to the Master Glossary.

The burnt offerings include 12 **bulls** for all Israel, 96 **rams**, and 77 **lambs**. The sin offering or purification offering includes 12 **male goats**. Use the same word or phrase for bulls, rams, lambs, and male goats as you used in previous passages.

The exiles gave the burnt offerings to **Yahweh**. Yahweh is the personal name for the God of Israel. You will notice that sometimes the storyteller uses the general word for the Creator God, sometimes he calls God the "God of Israel," and sometimes he uses the personal name "Yahweh" for God. In this part of the passage, use the same word for Yahweh that you have used previously, and refer to Yahweh in the Master Glossary for more information.

The group delivers the king's **orders** or instructions to the local leaders. These were special instructions that people wrote down from the king to give to the king's servants. Use the same word or phrase for orders as you used in previous passages.

The exiles deliver the **king**'s written orders to the local **governors** and Persian satraps. A king rules over a country, region, or people. A satrap rules over a region of the Persian Empire. There may be several governors within one region to help the satrap rule, but we are not sure exactly. These leaders collect taxes, keep order, and make sure people obey the king's laws. Use the same words or phrases for king and governor as you used in previous passages. For more information on king, refer to the Master Glossary.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **satrap**. A satrap is probably a more important ruler than a governor, but less important than the king. Pause this audio here.

The group delivers the king's orders to the governors of the **province beyond the River**. A province is a large area of land. The province beyond the River means the Persian territory west of the Euphrates River, which includes Judah and Jerusalem. The area is sometimes called the **Trans\-Euphrates** or the province beyond the River. Use the same word or phrase for Trans\-Euphrates or "province beyond the River" as you have previously.

Speaking the Word
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of the passage in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this session, retell the passage, as a group, in your own language.

First, the whole group should practice telling the passage in your own language. Remember to include the things you decided in previous steps, like chronology, words, phrases, emotions, or anything else you discussed.

One group member should tell a few sentences of the passage and pause. Another group member should tell the next part of the passage and pause. Do this until the group has retold the whole passage. The group members can correct each other in this step.

Pause this audio here.

Next, each member of the group should tell the whole passage without interruption at least one time. A group member can use random objects, motions, or their storyboard to help them remember the passage.

Pause this audio here.

Next, the group can decide whose version of the passage you like the most. You may decide that you liked the way one group member told one scene but you liked the way a different group member told another scene.

Pause this audio here.

Finally, the group can put together a final version of the passage that everyone agrees on. Each group member should practice telling this final version of the passage.

* **Associated Passages:** Ezra 8:31-36
* **Associated Articles:** Lamb (ID: `a337@FIAImages`); Lamb (ID: `a338@FIAImages`); Lamb (ID: `a339@FIAImages`); Ram (ID: `a331@FIAImages`); Ram (ID: `a332@FIAImages`); Ram (ID: `a333@FIAImages`); Male Goat (ID: `a183@VideoBibleDictionary`); Male Goat (ID: `a201@FIAImages`); Male Goat (ID: `a202@FIAImages`); Bull (ID: `a453@FIAImages`); priest (ID: `eng-t103-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); temple (ID: `eng-t138-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); burnt offering (ID: `eng-t154-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); Yahweh (ID: `eng-t174-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); house of God (ID: `eng-t183-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); God (ID: `eng-t189-v1@FIAKeyTerms`); king (ID: `eng-t226-v2@FIAKeyTerms`); Levite (ID: `eng-t244-v1@FIAKeyTerms`)

## Ezra 9:1–4 (id: eng-ezr-p19a-v1)

Hear and Heart
--------------

In this step, hear Ezra 9:1–4 and put it in your hearts.

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 9:1–4 three times, in three different translations, if possible.

Pause this audio here.

Now, as a group, discuss the following questions:

1. What do you like in this passage? Pause this audio here.
2. What do you not like or not understand in this passage? Pause this audio here.
3. What does this passage tell us about God? Pause this audio here.
4. What does this passage tell us about people? Pause this audio here.
5. How does this passage affect our daily lives? Pause this audio here.
6. Who do you know who needs to hear this passage?

Setting the Stage
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 9:1–4 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

The storyteller in this story tells us about what Ezra did in Jerusalem. You will remember from the previous story that Ezra and a group of exiles travelled from Babylon to Jerusalem. When Ezra arrived in Jerusalem, Ezra gave instructions to the king of Persia's officials. In this story, the storyteller tells us that the events in this story happen after Ezra's arrival. Later in the book, the storyteller tells us this story happens four months after Ezra arrives in Jerusalem.

In this passage, the storyteller does not tell us where Ezra is. However, later in the book, the storyteller tells us that Ezra is in front of the temple in Jerusalem. Officials or leaders of the Israelite people come to talk to Ezra. The leaders are the heads of families or clans of the Israelite people. The leaders, who are probably men, have authority over the Israelite community.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: Who are the leaders in your community? Who do the leaders represent and speak for? How do these leaders come to a more important leader when there is a problem? Pause this audio here.

The leaders want to tell Ezra something important. The leaders tell Ezra about a problem for the Israelites. The leaders talk to Ezra about the Israelites who came back from Babylon to Jerusalem at the time of Zerubbabel, about 60 years earlier. The leaders tell Ezra that the Israelites have not kept themselves separate or apart from people in Jerusalem and the surrounding countryside who are not Israelites.

Stop here and do this activity as a group: Talk about the people who live in the lands around your community but who are not part of your community. What special names do you have for those people? Pause this audio here.

The leaders mean that the Israelites are living with women who are not Israelites. The Israelites have taken women into the Israelite homes to live with the men as wives. A wife is a woman who is married to a man. You may remember from the book of Deuteronomy that Yahweh commanded the Israelites not to marry the people from certain tribes that lived in and around Canaan. At the time of Ezra, many different people groups lived in Jerusalem and lived next to the Israelites. The different people are the neighbours of the Israelites. The different people follow different gods than the Israelites. It is an "abomination," or a detestable thing, to Yahweh when people worship another god. An abomination is something a person does that disgusts or repels people or Yahweh. An abomination makes people feel horror.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: What actions or things make you feel disgusted or horrified? Pause this audio here.

The leaders list the people groups in Canaan and the surrounding countries who follow different gods. Some of the groups of people did not exist by the time of Ezra, but the leaders want Ezra to know that the neighbouring people are like the people groups the leaders list. You will remember that the Canaanites are the people who lived in Canaan before Yahweh gave Canaan to the Israelites. There are different tribes of Canaanites, including the Hittites, Perizzites, Jebusites, and Amorites. The Ammonites, Moabites, and Egyptians are from countries next to Judah and Israel.

Stop here and, as a group, look at a map of the lands around Jerusalem that shows the original places where the Canaanites lived, including the Hittites, Perizzites, Jebusites, and Amorites, and where the Moabites, Egyptians, and Ammonites lived. Pause this audio here.

It is likely that only the Ammonites, Moabites, and Egyptians are living in or near Jerusalem at the time of Ezra. The leaders tell Ezra that the Israelite men and their sons have taken wives from the daughters of the other people. At the time of Ezra, it was common for a father to organize the marriage of his son to a woman. The Israelites married people from the surrounding lands who worshipped other gods. The two groups of people mixed with each other. The leaders call the Israelites "the holy race" or "the holy seed." Race is a way to describe a group of people who share the same general physical features as each other and have a shared culture or traditions. The leaders are talking about the Israelites who have returned to Jerusalem from exile, the offspring or descendants from whom a new nation of people will grow. Yahweh set apart the Israelites to make the Israelites holy. The leaders tell Ezra that it is other leaders and officials who have started this! These people are leading other men to be unfaithful to God, also! The Israelites did not keep Yahweh's commands and were unfaithful to Yahweh.

Stop here and do this activity as a group: Talk about a person or people who encouraged other people to do something by being the first to do it themselves. Pause this audio here.

When the Israelite leaders tell Ezra of the sin that the people of Israel have done, Ezra is very upset. Ezra shows how he feels by doing signs of mourning. At the time of Ezra, when someone died, it was normal for a person to show their grief and sadness by doing certain actions. Ezra tears the garment or the tunic that Ezra wears close to his body.

Stop here and, as a group, look at a picture of a person who wears a tunic. Pause this audio here.

Ezra also tears his mantle or cloak, a piece of clothing that Ezra wears over the rest of his clothes.

Stop here and, as a group, look at a picture of a person who wears a mantle or cloak. Pause this audio here.

Ezra pulls hair from his head and out of his beard. At the time, it was common for a person to shave their head to show that they were mourning. However, Yahweh commanded the Israelites not to shave their heads. To be obedient to Yahweh, Ezra pulls out some hair but leaves the rest.

Ezra sits down. At the time, a person showed extreme sadness and grief by sitting down without saying anything. Ezra is horrified and overwhelmed by grief because of what the leaders told Ezra.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: In your community, how do people show that they are mourning because of something bad someone else did? Pause this audio here.

A group of people gathers around Ezra. The people are strict followers of the words of the God of Israel, or God's law for the Israelites. The people fear that the God of Israel will judge the people. People who feel strong emotions like fear may tremble and shake. The people are not trembling at the time of the story. The storyteller is using the description of the people to help us understand that the people are strict followers of the God of Israel, and the people have a strong respect and awe for God's law. The people did not gather to threaten Ezra.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: Talk about a group of people who are devoted to God and are afraid of sinning against God. How do those people show how they feel about God? Pause this audio here.

The unfaithful exiles are the Israelites who returned to Jerusalem with Zerubbabel and afterward. These are a different group of exiles from the people who returned to Jerusalem with Ezra. The exiles were unfaithful because the exiles did not follow Yahweh's command to only marry other Israelites.

Ezra sits until the evening offering. Traditionally, the priests at the temple made an offering to Yahweh near the end of each day when the sun was low in the sky but before the dark of night. The evening offering to Yahweh did not require the priests to kill an animal. Ezra feels appalled and horrified. Ezra is not able to do anything because Ezra feels so much grief. Ezra is humble and behaves as someone who does not deserve respect.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: Think about a time when you were overcome by grief and sadness. What did you do or not do to show how you felt?

Defining the Scenes
-------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 9:1–4 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will define the scenes, characters, and setting of this passage. Then the group will visualize this passage.

This passage has two scenes.

**In the first scene:** The leaders tell Ezra that the Israelite people have married people who are not Israelites. Ezra tears his clothes and pulls out his hair to show how upset Ezra is, and Ezra sits down to mourn.

**In the second scene:** Israelites who follow Yahweh's commands gather around Ezra until the time of the evening offering.

The characters in this passage are:

* Israelite leaders
* Ezra
* Israelite people
* Women from surrounding people groups and countries
* And Israelites who follow Yahweh's commands

As a group, pay attention to these parts of the passage's setting:

The storyteller starts the story by telling us that this story happens after Ezra and the other Israelites made sacrifices and gave messages to the Persian officials. The story in this passage happens four months after the events in the previous passage. The Israelite leaders come to talk to Ezra. The Israelite leaders tell Ezra about three groups of people: the ordinary people of Israel, the priests who serve Yahweh in the temple, and the rest of the people from the tribe of Levi. We do not know if the leaders want Ezra to think of the three groups of people separately or if the leaders talk about the three groups to mean everyone, including the priests and the Levites.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: How do you talk about all the people in your community? How would you help someone to understand that you mean everybody, including the leaders or other important people? Pause this audio here.

The leaders talk to Ezra about the Israelites who came back from Babylon to Jerusalem at the time of Zerubbabel, about 60 years earlier. The leaders tell Ezra that the Israelites did not stay separate from the other people in Jerusalem. The leaders do not mean that the Israelites went near the other people, but that the Israelites married the other people. The leaders call the other people "people of the land" to tell Ezra that they are talking about people who are foreign and not Israelites. The Israelites have married women from different people groups and countries who worship different gods. When people worship different gods, it is an abomination to Yahweh.

Stop here and do this activity as a group: Tell a story about something or some action that you think is horrible or disgusting. What words do you use to talk about that thing, and how will you describe it here? You may want to listen to your translation of Leviticus 18:22 to see how you translated abomination there. Pause this audio here.

The Israelite leaders list the tribes of people who lived in Canaan and people from surrounding countries as examples of people who have detestable or abominable practices. The Hittites, Perizzites, Jebusites, and Amorites lived in Canaan with other Canaanites. The Egyptians, Ammonites, and Moabites lived in countries next to Judah and Israel.

Stop here and, if needed, look as a group at a map of the lands around Jerusalem that shows the original places where the Canaanites lived, including the Hittites, Perizzites, Jebusites, and Amorites, and where the Moabites, Egyptians, and Ammonites lived. Pause this audio here.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: In your translation, how will you list the people or places with detestable practices that the leaders list to Ezra? Pause this audio here.

The Israelite men married some of the daughters or women of the foreign people. Also, some of the Israelite men's sons married some of the foreign women. The Israelite men and non\-Israelite women have had children. The Israelites, the holy people whom Yahweh set apart for himself, are now mixed with people who are not Israelites. The leaders and officials of the Israelites were the ones who started marriage with foreigners; these men were the most unfaithful or disloyal of all the people. The Israelites were unfaithful because they did not keep their oath to Yahweh not to marry foreigners.

After Ezra listens to the leaders of the Israelites, Ezra shows everyone that he is very upset. Ezra mourns for the people of Israel who have been unfaithful to Yahweh. Ezra tears his clothes, pulls at his hair and beard, and sits down in silence.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: What words would you use to describe someone who sits in silence because they are very sad and who mourns? What special phrase do you use to describe the person? Pause this audio here.

Ezra sits and is silent for some time. We do not know how long Ezra sat because we do not know what time of day the leaders came to speak to Ezra. While Ezra sits, people come to be with Ezra and stay with Ezra. We do not know if all these people are different people from the leaders or if the leaders stay with Ezra and the new people join them. The storyteller uses a special phrase to describe the new people to help us to know that the people who join Ezra keep Yahweh's commands for the people of Israel. The storyteller says that these people "tremble at the words of the God of Israel." These people may not be trembling right now, but the storyteller describes the people in this way to show that these people are afraid of God. These people respect and honor God and God's laws for his people.

Stop here and discuss this question as a group: The people who gather around Ezra are very strict followers of God. How will you translate that these people "tremble at the words of the God of Israel"? Pause this audio here.

The new people come to be with Ezra because they know the Israelites who returned to Jerusalem were unfaithful to Yahweh. Ezra stays in the same place until the evening, when it is time for the priests at the temple to make the evening offering. You will remember that the evening offering was usually a grain offering, not an animal sacrifice. The other people stay with Ezra.

Now, the group should storyboard, draw, or use objects to visualize the passage and the action in it.

Embodying the Text
------------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 9:1–4 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will dramatize the passage.

This passage has two scenes.

The characters in this passage are:

* Israelite leaders
* Ezra
* Israelite people
* Women from surrounding people groups and countries
* And Israelites who follow Yahweh's commands

As a group, act out the passage twice. You should act out the passage in your own language.

First, act out the passage without stopping. Pay attention to the dialogue, flow, plotline, and chronology of the passage. Make sure you do not skip acting out the difficult or important parts of the passage. Help each other remember every part of the passage.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage.

The group should act out this passage a second time. At certain points in the drama, pause the drama and ask the person playing the character, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person playing the character should answer what they would be feeling or thinking if they were that character. Then continue the drama.

Pause this audio here and act out the passage a second time.

The following is an example of the drama and possible responses.

The Israelite leaders tell Ezra that the Israelite people have married people who are not Israelites. Ezra tears his clothes and pulls out his hair to show how upset Ezra is, and Ezra sits down to mourn.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing Israelite leaders, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* We are really worried. We know we have disobeyed Yahweh; or
* Ezra, we need to know what to do. Please help us; or
* Will Yahweh forgive us?

Ask the person playing Ezra, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* My people have sinned against Yahweh; or
* This is terrible. Yahweh may kill everyone who has married a foreign woman; or
* I am really sad and upset.

Ask the person playing the Israelite people, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* We wanted to marry women and have a family; or
* We feel sad and frightened because we have sinned against Yahweh; or
* What should we do now?

Ask the person playing women from surrounding tribes and countries, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* We are married to Israelite men; or
* We want to have children and make a good home with the Israelite men; or
* We are confused because we did not know the men were doing something wrong.

Continue the drama.

Israelites who follow Yahweh's commands gather around Ezra until the time of the evening offering.

Pause the drama. Ask the person playing Israelites who follow Yahweh's commands, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* Ezra mourns for our people; or
* We feel sad and worried; or
* What will Ezra tell us? or
* Will we survive Yahweh's judgement on us?

Ask the person playing Ezra, "What are you feeling or thinking?" The person might answer things like:

* How will we survive this? or
* I am overwhelmed by sadness for my people; or
* I need to pray to Yahweh.

Filling the Gaps
----------------

Listen to an audio version of Ezra 9:1–4 in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this step, the group will discuss key terms and words in this passage.

Some of the Israelite **leaders** come to Ezra. The leaders are important people in the Israelite community. Use the same word or phrase for leaders as you used in previous passages.

The people of **Israel**, the **priests**, and the **Levites** have not kept themselves separate. Use the same words or phrases for Israel, priests, and Levites as you used in previous passages. For more information on Israel, priest, and Levite, refer to the Master Glossary.

The people have not kept separate from the "neighbouring people," the people from other lands who are not Israelites. The neighbouring people live next to the Israelite people in and near Jerusalem.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **neighbouring people**. If you have already translated this phrase in another book of the Bible, use the same word or phrase that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

The neighboring people do "detestable things" or "abominations." The people worship false gods.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **detestable practices**. If you have already translated this word in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

The Israelites have mixed the "**holy** race" or "seed" with the people from surrounding lands. Use the same word for holy as you have used previously, and refer to holy in the Master Glossary for more information. The race or seed are the descendants of Israel who have returned to Jerusalem from Babylon.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **race** or **seed**. Look up seed in the Master Glossary for more information. Pause this audio here.

The leaders and **officials** have led the way in being unfaithful to Yahweh. We do not know the responsibilities of the two groups of people: the leaders and the officials. Officials help govern people, and the Babylonians may have appointed the officials. Leaders are a type of community leader, but the Babylonians may not have appointed them. Use the same word for official as you used in previous passages. The leaders and officials were unfaithful; they did not follow Yahweh's laws.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **unfaithfulness**. If you have already translated this word in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

Ezra tore the garment or tunic that Ezra wore close to his body.

Stop here and, as a group, look at a picture of a person who wears a tunic, if needed. Pause this audio here.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **tunic**. Look up tunic in the Master Glossary for more information. If you have already translated this word in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

Ezra tore his "mantle" or "cloak," which Ezra wore over his clothes.

Stop here and look at a picture of a person wearing a mantle or cloak as a group if needed. Pause this audio here.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **cloak**. Look up cloak in the Master Glossary for more information. If you have already translated this word in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

Ezra was appalled. Ezra felt horrified.

Stop here and discuss as a group how you will describe how Ezra felt when he was **horrified** or very, very sad. If you have already translated this feeling in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there. Pause this audio here.

People who trembled at the words of the **God** of Israel or God's law gathered around Ezra. Use the same word or phrase for God as you used in previous passages. For more information on God, refer to the Master Glossary.

People gathered because of the unfaithfulness of the **exiles**. Use the same word or phrase for exiles as you used in previous passages.

Ezra sat until the evening offering. The priests made an offering to Yahweh in the evening before the sun set.

Stop here and discuss as a group what word or phrase you will use for **evening offering**. Look up offering in the Master Glossary for more information. If you have already translated this word in another book of the Bible, use the same word that you have used there.

Speaking the Word
-----------------

Listen to an audio version of the passage in the easiest\-to\-understand translation.

Pause this audio here.

In this session, retell the passage, as a group, in your own language.

First, the whole group should practice telling the passage in your own language. Remember to include the things you decided in previous steps, like chronology, words, phrases, emotions, or anything else you discussed.

One group member should tell a few sentences of the passage and pause. Another group member should tell the next part of the passage and pause. Do this until the group has retold the whole passage. The group members can correct each other in this step.

Pause this audio here.

Next, each member of the group should tell the whole passage without interruption at least one time. A group member can use random objects, motions, or their storyboard to help them remember the passage.

Pause this audio here.

Next, the group can decide whose version of the passage you like the most. You may decide that you liked the way one group member told one scene but you liked the way a different group member told another scene.

Pause this audio here.

Finally, the group can put together a final version of the passage that everyone agrees on. Each group member should practice telling this final version of the passage.
